Collective vs Distributive Universal
Author distinguishes collective from distributive universal using examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a collective universal (al-kull) and a distributive universal (al-kulliyya) by analyzing how the predicate applies to the subject's individuals.

Core Idea
A collective universal applies to the group as one; a distributive universal applies to each member individually.

In Islamic logic and usul al-fiqh, understanding how a judgment relates to its subject is essential. A universal proposition can either be collective, where the predicate holds for the subject as a whole, or distributive, where it holds for every individual. This lesson clarifies that crucial difference using the author's examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate
In a universal proposition, find the thing being judged (subject) and what is being said about it (predicate). For example, in 'The eight angels carry the Throne', the subject is 'the eight angels' and the predicate is 'carrier of the Throne'.
2
Check applicability to individuals
Ask: Does the predicate hold for each individual of the subject separately? If yes, it's distributive. If it holds only for the group together, it's collective. For the angels example, each angel alone does not carry the Throne; only the group does.
3
Test with the eight angels example
The author says: if the judgment 'carrier of the Throne' applied to each angel individually, each would independently carry it. But in reality, it is the group. So this is a collective universal.
This example shows the collective universal clearly.
4
Test with 'Every human is an animal'
Here, each human individually is an animal. So it is a distributive universal: the judgment follows every individual.
This is the standard example of distributive universal.
5
Summarize the difference
Collective: judgment does not follow each individual. Distributive: judgment follows each individual. The author's rule: 'al-kull' (collective) vs 'al-kulliyya' (distributive).

on all of them; for if it were on all of them, each one of the eight would be independently carrying the Throne alone, whereas the reality is that the carrier of the Throne is the totality of the eight. So if the separation of the eight were supposed, the judgment of carrying the Throne would not follow each one of them. As for the distributive universal, it is the judgment on every individual of the individuals of the subject that fall under the description, as when you say: 'Every human is an animal'; for every individual of the individuals of human is independently subject to the judgment that it is an animal, so each of them follows the judgment individually. The scholars of rhetoric have established a rule here, namely: that the word 'kull' (every/all) when conjoined with a particle of negation — I mean a negative particle — if the negative particle comes before the word 'kull', then it is of the collective universal, so the judgment does not fall on the subject except collectively, and it does not follow every individual of its individuals, like his saying: 'Not everything a man wishes does he attain; the winds blow with what the ships do not desire.' For a man may wish something and attain it, and how many a wish has its owner attained, as is not hidden. And like their saying: 'Not every white thing is fat,' and 'Not every round thing is a cake,' for the white thing may be fat, and the round thing may be a cake, in some cases but not all. And if the negative particle comes after the word 'kull', then it is of the distributive universal, so the judgment of the predicate on the subject is inclusive of every individual, like the saying of Abu Najm: 'Umm al-Khiyar has come to accuse me of a sin, all of which I did not commit,' with 'kull' in the nominative; because his intention is that he did not commit a single one of what she accused him of. And from this meaning is the saying of the one with the two hands when he said: 'Did the prayer shorten or did you forget?' He said: 'All of that did not happen,' meaning none of it happened: I did not forget nor was it shortened, meaning in my estimation, as is established in Usul, in the topic of Dalalat al-Iqtida'. And this rule is probabilistic, due to the occurrence of multiple verses in which the negative particle comes before the word 'kull', yet they are distributive, not collective, like His statement — exalted —: 'Indeed, Allah does not love every treacherous, ungrateful [person]' and His statement: 'Indeed, Allah does not love every conceited, boastful [person]' and similar verses; because the judgment of negating love is general to every individual of the treacherous, ungrateful individuals, and the conceited, boastful individuals, as you see. So you have known that the difference between 'al-kull' and 'al-kulliyya' is that with 'al-kull' the judgment does not follow every individual of its individuals, and that with 'al-kulliyya' the judgment follows every individual of its individuals. Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes between two types of universal judgments: the collective universal (al-kull al-majmu'i) and the distributive universal (al-kulliyya). The author begins by explaining the collective universal using the example of eight angels carrying the Throne: if the judgment 'carrier of the Throne' applied to each individually, each would independently carry it, but in reality it is the group as a whole. So the collective universal means the predicate applies to the subject as a composite whole, not to each individual separately. The distributive universal, by contrast, means the predicate applies to every individual of the subject independently, as in 'Every human is an animal' — each human individually is an animal. The author then presents a rule from the science of rhetoric (balagha) about the position of the word 'kull' relative to a negative particle. If the negative precedes 'kull' (e.g., 'ma kullu ...'), it indicates collective universal: the negation is of the whole, not necessarily each part. Examples from poetry: 'Not everything a man wishes does he attain' — meaning some wishes are attained, some are not, not that none are attained. And common Arabic sayings: 'Not every white thing is fat,' 'Not every round thing is a cake.' If the negative follows 'kull' (e.g., 'kulluhu lam ...'), it indicates distributive universal: the negation applies to every individual. Example from Abu Najm's verse: 'a sin, all of which I did not commit' meaning he committed none of it. Also the hadith where the Prophet (ﷺ) was asked about shortening prayer and forgot; he said 'all of that did not happen' meaning neither shortening nor forgetfulness occurred. The author notes that this rule is usually reliable (aghlabiyya) but not absolute, citing Quranic verses where the negative precedes 'kull' yet the judgment is distributive, e.g., 'Allah does not love every treacherous, ungrateful [person]' — meaning He loves no treacherous person at all, not just some. The passage concludes by summarizing the difference: in collective universal, the judgment does not follow each individual; in distributive, it does. Examples: affirmative 'Every human is an animal' (distributive) and negative 'No human is a stone' (implicitly distributive). This distinction is crucial in usul al-fiqh for understanding general and particular commands.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a collective universal?
A) Every human is an animal.
B) The eight angels carry the Throne.
C) All men are mortal.
D) No student is absent.

Key Takeaway
A collective universal applies to the whole subject, not each part; a distributive universal applies to every individual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the following proposition is collective or distributive: 'The Muslim army defeated the enemy.' (Hint: Did each soldier individually defeat the enemy, or the army as a whole?)
?
Find an example of a collective universal in a Quranic verse (e.g., 'They are the ones who will be successful' – does it refer to each individually or the group?).

Negation Rule for Collective vs Distributive Universal
Author presents a rule from balagha about negation position and acknowledges its probabilistic nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a negated universal proposition is collective or distributive based on the position of the negative particle relative to 'kull'.

Core Idea
Negation before 'kull' indicates collective; negation after 'kull' indicates distributive, but exceptions exist.

A key tool from Arabic rhetoric (balagha) helps us decide whether a negated universal is collective or distributive: the position of the negative particle. When negation precedes 'kull', the judgment is collective; when it follows, it is distributive. However, the author warns that this rule is probabilistic, as Quranic verses show exceptions. This lesson equips you to apply and qualify this rule.

Guided Path
1
Locate 'kull' and the negative particle
Identify the word 'kull' (every/all) and any negative particle such as 'ma', 'la', 'lam'. Determine if the negative comes before or after 'kull'.
2
Apply the default rule
If the negative precedes 'kull', the universal is collective: the negation applies to the group as a whole, not necessarily each part. If it follows 'kull', it is distributive: the negation applies to each individual.
3
Analyze examples with negative before 'kull'
Example: 'Not every white thing is fat' (ما كل بيضاء شحمة) – this means some white things are fat, some are not; the negation is collective. Similarly, 'Not everything a man wishes does he attain' – some wishes are attained, some not.
4
Analyze examples with negative after 'kull'
Example: 'a sin, all of which I did not commit' (ذنبًا كله لم أصنع) – means I committed none of it. The negation distributes to each individual sin. Also 'All of that did not happen' (كل ذلك لم يكن) – meaning none of it happened.
5
Note the probabilistic nature and exceptions
The author states the rule is aghlabiyya (probabilistic) because Quranic verses like 'Allah does not love every treacherous, ungrateful [person]' have negative before 'kull' but the meaning is distributive (no such person is loved). So always check context.

Commentary
This passage defines and distinguishes between two types of universal judgments: the collective universal (al-kull al-majmu'i) and the distributive universal (al-kulliyya). The author begins by explaining the collective universal using the example of eight angels carrying the Throne: if the judgment 'carrier of the Throne' applied to each individually, each would independently carry it, but in reality it is the group as a whole. So the collective universal means the predicate applies to the subject as a composite whole, not to each individual separately. The distributive universal, by contrast, means the predicate applies to every individual of the subject independently, as in 'Every human is an animal' — each human individually is an animal. The author then presents a rule from the science of rhetoric (balagha) about the position of the word 'kull' relative to a negative particle. If the negative precedes 'kull' (e.g., 'ma kullu ...'), it indicates collective universal: the negation is of the whole, not necessarily each part. Examples from poetry: 'Not everything a man wishes does he attain' — meaning some wishes are attained, some are not, not that none are attained. And common Arabic sayings: 'Not every white thing is fat,' 'Not every round thing is a cake.' If the negative follows 'kull' (e.g., 'kulluhu lam ...'), it indicates distributive universal: the negation applies to every individual. Example from Abu Najm's verse: 'a sin, all of which I did not commit' meaning he committed none of it. Also the hadith where the Prophet (ﷺ) was asked about shortening prayer and forgot; he said 'all of that did not happen' meaning neither shortening nor forgetfulness occurred. The author notes that this rule is usually reliable (aghlabiyya) but not absolute, citing Quranic verses where the negative precedes 'kull' yet the judgment is distributive, e.g., 'Allah does not love every treacherous, ungrateful [person]' — meaning He loves no treacherous person at all, not just some. The passage concludes by summarizing the difference: in collective universal, the judgment does not follow each individual; in distributive, it does. Examples: affirmative 'Every human is an animal' (distributive) and negative 'No human is a stone' (implicitly distributive). This distinction is crucial in usul al-fiqh for understanding general and particular commands.

In the saying 'Not every white thing is fat', the negation 'ma' precedes 'kull'. According to the rule, this indicates:
A) Distributive universal (no white thing is fat).
B) Collective universal (only some white things are not fat).
C) The rule does not apply because it's poetry.
D) It is an exception to the rule.

Key Takeaway
The position of negation relative to 'kull' is a useful heuristic but not infallible; always confirm the intended meaning.

?
Given the Arabic phrase 'لَا يُحِبُّ اللَّهُ كُلَّ خَوَّانٍ', determine whether the negation precedence indicates collective or distributive, and then check the meaning to see if it follows the rule or is an exception.
?
Construct an original Arabic sentence where the negative particle follows 'kull' to express that none of the individuals did something.

Propositions

Distinguishing al-Kull, al-Kulliyya, and al-Kulli
Author distinguishes collective 'kull' from universal 'kulliyya' and universal concept 'kulli'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-kull (collective whole), al-kulliyya (universal proposition), and al-kulli (universal concept), and correctly categorize any given use of 'kull'.

Core Idea
Kull does not distribute over parts; kulliyya distributes over individuals; kulli is predicable synonymously.

The author clarifies three logically distinct uses of 'kull' that often cause confusion. Understanding these distinctions is critical for interpreting propositions in Islamic sciences correctly, especially when dealing with general and universal statements in Usul al-Fiqh and Kalam.

Guided Path
1
Locate the occurrence of 'kull' in a proposition
Identify whether 'kull' is the subject or part of a quantifier. Note the position of any negation relative to 'kull'. The author gives a rule: negation after 'kull' indicates a universal negative, but you must check whether it is collective or universal.
Example: 'All of that did not happen' – negation after 'kull' shows universal negative, but is it collective?
2
Determine if 'kull' is collective or universal
If the judgment applies to each part individually, it is universal (kulliyya). If the judgment applies to the whole collectively, it is collective (kull). Compare 'chair has nails' (collective) with 'every human is animal' (universal).
3
Distinguish between collective kull and universal concept kulli
A universal concept (kulli) can be predicated synonymously of each individual (e.g., 'Zaid is human'). A collective kull cannot; you must use possession or derivative predication (e.g., 'chair has nails' or 'chair is nailed').
This is the first aspect the author mentions.
4
Apply the distinction to Quranic examples
Analyze 'Allah does not love every treacherous traitor' – it is universal (kulliyya) because each traitor is individually not loved, even though negation precedes 'kull'. The author says this rule is majority, not absolute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان حرف النفي بعد لفظة (كل) فهو من الكلية فالحكم بالمحمول على الموضوع شامل لكل فرد ، كقول أبي النجم : قد أصبحَتْ أمُّ الخيار تدعي عليَّ ذنبا كله لم أصنع برفع لفظة (كل) ؛ لأن مراده أنه لم يصنع شيئًا واحدا مما ادعت عليه. ومن هذا المعنى قوله الله الذي اليدين لما قال : أقصرت الصلاة أم نسیت؟ : كل ذلك لم يكن، أي لم يكن شيء منه : لم أنْسَ ولم تُقصر، يعني في ظني، كما هو مقرر في الأصول، في مبحث دلالة الاقتضاء. وهذه القاعدة أغلبية ؛ لورود آيات متعددة فيها حرف النفي قبل لفظة (كل)، مع أنها كلية، لا كلّ مجموعي، كقوله تعالى: ﴿ إِنَّ اللهَ لَا يُحِبُّ كُلَّ خَوَانٍ كَفُورٍ ﴾ [الحج/ ٣٨] وقوله: ﴿ إِنَّ اللَّهَ لَا يُحِبُّ كُلَّ مُختَالٍ فَخُورٍ ﴾ [لقمان/ ١٨] ونحو ذلك من الآيات؛ لأن الحكم بنفي المحبة عام لكل فرد من أفراد الخوانين الكفورين، والمختالين الفخورين، كما ترى. فقد عرفت أن الفرق بين (الكل) و(الكلية)، أن (الكل) لا يتبع الحكم فيه كل فرد من أفراده، وأن (الكلية) يتبع الحكم فيها كل فرد من أفرادها، ومثالها موجبة كل إنسان حيوان، وسالبة: لا شيء من الحجر بحيوان؛ لأن إثبات الحيوانية للإنسان شامل لكل فرد من أفراده، ونفي الحيوانية عن الحجر شامل لكل فرد من أفراده. وأما الفرق بين (الكل) و (الكلي) فمن جهتين : الجهة الأولى : أنا قدمنا أن (الكلي) لا يمنع تعقلُ مدلوله من حمله على كثيرين حمل مواطأة، فيجوز حمل الكلي على كل فرد من أفراده حمل مواطأة، كقولك: (عمرو إنسان)، و(زيد إنسان)، و(خالد إنسان) إلخ، فالإنسان كلي وقد صح حمله على كل فرد من أفراده حمل مواطأة كما تقدم إيضاحه. أما (الكل) فلا يجوز حمله على جزء من أجزائه حمل مواطأة، بل حمل إضافة أو اشتقاق. فالكرسي مثلاً : كلُّ مركب من خشب ومسامير، فلا يجوز أن تقول : الكرسي مسمار ولا أن تقول : الكرسي خشب، ولكن يصح حمله على أجزائه بالإضافة والاشتقاق، فالإضافة كأن تقول : الكرسي ذو مسامير، والاشتقاق كقولك : الكرسي مسمّر. وكالشجرة؛ فإنها كل مركب من جذوع وأغصان، فلا يقال: الشجرة جذوع ولا الشجرة أغصان وإنما يقال : الشجرة ذات جذوع، وذات أغصان مثلاً.
And if the negative particle occurs after the word 'kull', then it is [a case of] universal [negative], so the judgment of the predicate to the subject includes every individual, like the saying of Abu Najm: 'Umm al-Khiyar has accused me of a sin, all of which I did not commit' with the word 'kull' raised (nominative) because he meant that he did not do a single thing of what she accused him of. And of this meaning is his saying (the Prophet) – upon whom be the two hands! – when he said: 'Did the prayer shorten or did I forget?' – 'All of that did not happen', i.e., nothing of it happened: I did not forget nor was it shortened, meaning in my estimation, as is established in Usul, in the topic of contextual indication (dalalat al-iqtida'). And this rule is majority [not universal]; because multiple verses occur in which the negative particle precedes 'kull', while they are universal, not collective 'kull', such as His saying – exalted: 'Indeed Allah does not love every treacherous traitor' [22:38] and His saying: 'Indeed Allah does not love every conceited boaster' [31:18] and similar verses; because the judgment of negating love is general for every individual of the treacherous traitors and the conceited boasters, as you see. So you have known that the difference between 'al-kull' and 'al-kulliyya' is that in 'al-kull' the judgment does not apply to every individual of its parts, while in 'al-kulliyya' the judgment applies to every individual of its parts. And its example is an affirmative [proposition]: 'Every human is an animal', and a negative: 'No stone is an animal'; because affirming animality for humans includes every individual of its parts, and negating animality from stone includes every individual of its parts. As for the difference between 'al-kull' and 'al-kulli', it is from two aspects: The first aspect: We have previously stated that 'al-kulli' does not prevent the understanding of its meaning from being predicated of many by way of 'haml muwata'a' (mutual predication), so it is permissible to predicate the universal of every individual of its individuals by way of 'haml muwata'a', like your saying: 'Amr is a human', 'Zaid is a human', 'Khalid is a human', etc. So 'human' is a universal and it is valid to predicate it of every individual of its individuals by 'haml muwata'a' as previously explained. As for 'al-kull', it is not permissible to predicate it of a part of its parts by 'haml muwata'a', but rather by 'haml idafa' (idafa predication) or 'haml ishtiqaq' (derivative predication). For example, a chair: it is a whole composed of wood and nails, so you cannot say: 'The chair is a nail' nor can you say: 'The chair is wood', but it is valid to predicate it of its parts by idafa and ishtiqaq. So idafa is like your saying: 'The chair has nails', and ishtiqaq is like your saying: 'The chair is nailed'. And like a tree: it is a whole composed of trunks and branches, so one does not say: 'The tree is trunks' nor 'the tree is branches', but rather one says: 'The tree has trunks', 'has branches', for example.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is definitional: it clarifies three related but distinct logical terms: 'kull' (collective whole), 'kulliyya' (universality in judgment), and 'kulli' (universal concept). The author begins by explaining a rule: when the negative particle (e.g., 'la') appears after the word 'kull', the judgment is universal negative, meaning the predicate is negated of every individual of the subject. He gives a poetic example from Abu Najm: 'All of which I did not commit' meaning he did not commit any single act. He also cites a hadith where the Prophet said 'All of that did not happen' meaning nothing of it happened. However, the author qualifies this as a majority rule ('aghlabiyya'), not absolute, because there are Quranic verses where the negative precedes 'kull' and yet the proposition is universal, not collective. For example, 'Allah does not love every treacherous traitor' – this is a universal negative, applying the negation of love to each individual treacherous traitor. The author then explicitly distinguishes 'kull' from 'kulliyya': in 'kull' (collective), the judgment does not apply distributively to each part; in 'kulliyya' (universal proposition), the judgment applies to every individual. He gives the example of the universal affirmative 'Every human is an animal' – the predicate 'animal' applies to every individual human. And the universal negative 'No stone is an animal' – the negation applies to every individual stone. Next, the author contrasts 'kull' with 'kulli' (universal concept) from two aspects. First aspect: 'kulli' is predicable of each of its individuals by 'haml muwata'a' (synonymous predication, e.g., 'Zaid is human'). In contrast, 'kull' cannot be predicated of its parts by 'haml muwata'a'; one cannot say 'The chair is wood' or 'The chair is a nail' because 'chair' is a whole composed of wood and nails. Instead, one uses 'haml idafa' (predication of possession, e.g., 'The chair has nails') or 'haml ishtiqaq' (derivative predication, e.g., 'The chair is nailed'). The tree example similarly: 'tree' is a whole of trunks and branches, so one says 'The tree has trunks', not 'The tree is trunks'. This distinction is crucial in logic for understanding how terms function in propositions and definitions.

الكلية
الكلي
حمل مواطأة
حمل إضافة
حمل اشتقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of 'al-kull' (collective whole) as opposed to 'al-kulliyya' (universal proposition)?
A) Every human is mortal.
B) The chair has nails.
C) No stone is animal.
D) Every Muslim believes in Allah.

Key Takeaway
The critical distinction is that collective 'kull' does not distribute the judgment over its parts, while universal 'kulliyya' does, and universal concept 'kulli' can be predicated of each individual synonymously.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the following statement uses 'kull' collectively or universally: 'The army all fought bravely.' (Collective: the army fought as a unit, not each soldier necessarily). Then, explain why 'human' is a kulli and can be predicated of Zaid synonymously.

Propositions
beginner
6 min
Types of Predication: Haml Muwata'a, Idafa, and Ishtiqaq
Author distinguishes three types of predication to clarify the difference between kull and kulli.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct examples of haml muwata'a (synonymous predication), haml idafa (possession predication), and haml ishtiqaq (derivative predication).

Core Idea
Predication type determines whether a predicate applies synonymously, by possession, or derivatively.

In distinguishing al-kull from al-kulli, the author introduces three types of predication that clarify how terms relate to their subjects. This is essential for understanding definitions and logical predication in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Define haml muwata'a
Predication where the predicate is synonymous in meaning with the subject and applies directly to each individual. Example: 'Zaid is human.' This is used for universal concepts (kulli).
Also called 'predication of agreement' or 'synonymous predication'.
2
Define haml idafa
Predication of possession or relation. Example: 'The chair has nails.' The predicate indicates ownership or connection, not identity.
Used when the subject is a whole and the predicate is a part.
3
Define haml ishtiqaq
Predication using a derived form. Example: 'The chair is nailed.' The predicate is derived from a part (nail).
Shows a quality or attribute derived from a part.
4
Apply the distinction to the tree example
For a tree, which is a whole of trunks and branches, we cannot say 'The tree is trunks' (muwata'a). Instead, we use idafa: 'The tree has trunks' or ishtiqaq: 'The tree is trunked' (if such a word exists).

The Four Logical Relations Between Terms
Author defines the four relative partitions and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the four logical relations (tabayun, musawah, umum mutlaq, umum min wajh) between any two terms using examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
The four term relations are the bedrock of propositional truth evaluation in Islamic logic.

In this passage, the author presents the four fundamental relations that can hold between any two terms: mutual exclusion, equality, absolute generality-particularity, and partial generality-particularity. Understanding these relations is essential because they directly determine whether a proposition is true or false. We will focus on the definitions and examples of each relation as given in the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four relations
Recognize that the author lists tabayun, musawah, umum mutlaq, and umum min wajh as the four possible relations between terms. The passage defines two in detail and implies the other two.
2
Understand the distinguishing criterion
The relations are defined by whether each term's extension exists without the other. For umum mutlaq, only one term has instances outside the other; for umum min wajh, both have exclusive instances.
3
Analyze the example of umum mutlaq
Study the example of 'animal' and 'human': animal exists without human (horses, mules), but human never without animal. Thus animal is absolutely general, human absolutely particular.
This shows that the broader term (animal) has instances that are not human.
4
Analyze the example of umum min wajh
Study the example of 'human' and 'white': they meet in a white human, but each exists without the other (white in snow, human in black person). Thus partial overlap.
5
Infer the definitions of tabayun and musawah
From the truth condition section, deduce that tabayun means no overlap (e.g., human and stone) and musawah means complete overlap (e.g., human and rational).
These are not explicitly defined in the given passage but are clear from context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كان الذي يفارِقُ واحدًا منهما فقط دون الآخر، فهما اللذان بينهما العموم والخصوص المطلق، والذي يفارق أعم مطلقاً، والذي لا يفارق أخص مطلقا، كالحيوان والإنسان؛ فإن الحيوان يفارق الإنسان؛ لوجوده دونه في الفرس والبغل مثلاً، والإنسان لا يمكن أن يفارق الحيوان؛ إذ لا إنسان إلا وهو حيوان، فلا يفارق الإنسانُ الحيوان بحال، فالحيوان أعم مطلقا، والإنسان أخص مطلقا، فالنسبة بينهما العموم والخصوص المطلق .
وإن كان كل منهما يفارقُ الآخر فهما اللذان بينهما العموم والخصوص من وجه كالإنسان والأبيض، فإنهما يجتمعان في الإنسان الأبيض، كالعربي والرومي، وينفرد الأبيض عن الإنسان في الثلج والعاج مثلاً، وينفرد الإنسان عن الأبيض في الزنجي مثلاً، فهو إنسان أسود.
وإذا عرفت هذه النسب الأربع فاعلم أنها هي الميزانُ الذي يُعرف به الصادق والكاذب من القضايا :
فكل قضية كانت النسبة بين طرفيها التباين فهي صادقة السلبين، كاذبة الإيجابين - أعني بالسلبين : السلب الكلي والجزئي، وبالإيجابين: الإيجاب الكليّ والجزئي ، فلا تكذب سالبة مطلقا، ولا تصدق موجبة مطلقا .
فلو ركبت قضية من الإنسان والحجر صدقت في كل سلب، كقولك : لا شيء من الحجر بإنسان)، و(لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر)، و(بعض الحجر ليس بإنسان)، وبعض الإنسان ليس بحجر)، كل ذلك صادق )
(1)
وكذبَتْ في كل إيجاب كقولك : كل إنسان حجر)، أو (كل حجر إنسان)، أو (بعض الإنسان حجر)، و (بعض الحجر إنسان)، كل ذلك كاذب .
وإن كانت النسبة بين طرفيها المساواة فهي صادقة الإيجابين، كاذبة السلبين - أعني الإيجاب الكليَّ والإيجاب الجزئي، والسلب الكلي والسلب الجزئي ، فلا تكذب في إيجاب مطلقاً، ولا تصدق في سلب مطلقا .
فلو ركبت قضية من الإنسان والناطق فإنها تصدق في كل إيجاب، كقولك : كل إنسان ناطق)، و(كل ناطق إنسان)، و(بعض الإنسان ناطق) ، و ( بعض الناطق إنسان)، فكله صادق .
وتكذب في كل سلب كقولك : لا شيء من الإنسان بناطق)، و (لا شيء من الناطق بإنسان). وبعض الإنسان ليس بناطق)، و(بعض الناطق ليس بإنسان)، فكل ذلك كاذب .
وإن كانت النسبة بين طرفيها العموم والخصوص من وجه فهي كاذبة صادقة الجزئيتين - أعني الجزئية الموجبة، والجزئية السالبة ، الكليتين - أعني الكلية الموجبة والسالبة ـ فلا تكذب في جزئية مطلقا،
(1) يلاحظ هنا أن مفهوم المخالفة لا اعتبار له في فن المنطق، فلا يعترض على صدق بعض الحجر ليس بإنسان ونحوها بأنه يلزم منه أن يكون البعض الآخر إنسانًا، فيناقض الاشيء من الحجر (بإنسان.
If one of them differs from the other only (without the other differing from it), then they are the two that have absolute generality and particularity. The one that differs is absolutely general, and the one that does not differ is absolutely particular, like 'animal' and 'human'. For animal differs from human because it exists without it in horse and mule, for example, while human cannot differ from animal, since there is no human unless it is an animal. So human never differs from animal. Thus animal is absolutely general, human is absolutely particular, and the relation between them is absolute generality and particularity.
And if each of them differs from the other, then they are the two that have generality and particularity from one aspect, like 'human' and 'white'. They meet in the white human, like the Arab and the Roman, and white is distinct from human in snow and ivory, for example, and human is distinct from white in the black person, for example, for he is a black human.
And when you have known these four relations, know that they are the scale by which the true and the false of propositions are known.
For every proposition whose two terms have the relation of mutual exclusion, it is true in both negatives and false in both affirmatives—I mean by the two negatives: the universal negative and the particular negative, and by the two affirmatives: the universal affirmative and the particular affirmative. So a negative is never false, and an affirmative is never true.
For if you construct a proposition from 'human' and 'stone', it is true in every negative, like your saying 'No stone is a human', and 'No human is a stone', and 'Some stone is not a human', and 'Some human is not a stone'—all that is true. (1)
And it is false in every affirmative, like your saying 'Every human is a stone', or 'Every stone is a human', or 'Some human is a stone', and 'Some stone is a human'—all that is false.
And if the relation between its two terms is equality, then it is true in both affirmatives, false in both negatives—I mean the universal affirmative and the particular affirmative, and the universal negative and the particular negative. So an affirmative is never false, and a negative is never true.
For if you construct a proposition from 'human' and 'rational', it is true in every affirmative, like your saying 'Every human is rational', and 'Every rational is human', and 'Some human is rational', and 'Some rational is human'—all that is true.
And it is false in every negative, like your saying 'No human is rational', and 'No rational is human', and 'Some human is not rational', and 'Some rational is not human'—all that is false.
And if the relation between its two terms is generality and particularity from one aspect, then the two particulars are true and the two universals are false—I mean the particular affirmative and the particular negative, and the two universals—I mean the universal affirmative and the universal negative. So a particular is never false.
(1) It is noted here that the concept of contradiction is not considered in the art of logic, so one should not object to the truth of 'Some stone is not a human' and the like by saying that it implies that the other part is human, contradicting 'No stone is a human.'
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage defines the four logical relations between terms and then shows how each relation determines the truth or falsehood of categorical propositions. The author begins by completing the discussion of the four relative partitions. He explains that if only one term differs from the other (i.e., the first term exists without the second but the second never exists without the first), then we have absolute generality-particularity (العموم والخصوص المطلق). The broader term is absolutely general, the narrower is absolutely particular. The example given is 'animal' and 'human': animal exists without human (e.g., in horses and mules), but human never exists without animal, so animal is general, human is particular, and the relation is absolute generality-particularity.

Then he describes the second kind: if each term differs from the other (i.e., each exists without the other in some instances), then we have generality-particularity from one aspect (العموم والخصوص من وجه). The example is 'human' and 'white': they intersect in a white human, but white exists without human (e.g., snow, ivory), and human exists without white (e.g., a black person). So the relation is partial overlap.

Having defined the four relations (mutual exclusion, equality, absolute general-specific, and partial general-specific), the author states that they are the 'scale' (ميزان) by which the truth and falsehood of propositions are known. He proceeds to apply each relation to the four categorical propositions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative). For mutual exclusion (تباين), both negatives (universal and particular) are true, and both affirmatives are false. He illustrates with 'human' and 'stone': it is true that no human is a stone, no stone is a human, some human is not a stone, some stone is not a human; but it is false to say every human is a stone, every stone is a human, some human is a stone, some stone is a human.

For equality (مساواة), both affirmatives are true and both negatives are false, exemplified by 'human' and 'rational': every human is rational, every rational is human, some human is rational, some rational is human are true; while the corresponding negatives are false.

For partial generality-particularity (العموم والخصوص من وجه), the two particular propositions (affirmative and negative) are true, but the two universal propositions are false. The author's text is cut off but is completed by the rule that particulars are never false and universals are never true in this relation. A footnote clarifies a potential misunderstanding: the concept of contradiction (مخالفة) is not considered; thus one should not object that 'Some stone is not a human' implies the remaining stones are human, contradicting 'No stone is a human.' This note guards against a common logical error.

Key Terms

العموم والخصوص المطلق
العموم والخصوص من وجه

Key Takeaway
The four term relations—mutual exclusion, equality, absolute general-specific, and partial overlap—are defined by whether each term's extension exists without the other.

?

Propositions

Using Term Relations to Determine Truth of Propositions
Author explains how each relation determines truth of categorical propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to predict the truth values of the four categorical propositions (A, E, I, O) for any two terms if you know their logical relation, based on the patterns given in the passage.

Core Idea
The four term relations are the key to determining which categorical propositions are true and which are false.

The author now applies the four relations to categorical propositions. He states that once you know the relation between the subject and predicate terms, you can determine whether universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative are true or false. We will examine the patterns for the three relations explicitly covered: mutual exclusion, equality, and partial overlap.

Guided Path
1
Recall the four categorical proposition types
Universal affirmative (A: all S are P), universal negative (E: no S is P), particular affirmative (I: some S are P), particular negative (O: some S are not P).
2
Learn the rule for tabayun (mutual exclusion)
When terms have no overlap, both E and O (negatives) are true, and both A and I (affirmatives) are false. Example: human and stone.
The passage says 'every negative is true' and 'every affirmative is false'.
3
Learn the rule for musawah (equality)
When terms are coextensive, both A and I are true, both E and O are false. Example: human and rational.
The passage says 'every affirmative is true' and 'every negative is false'.
4
Learn the rule for umum min wajh (partial overlap)
When terms partially overlap, both particular propositions (I and O) are true, and both universal propositions (A and E) are false. Example: human and white.
The passage says 'the two particulars are true and the two universals are false'.
5
Note the missing pattern for umum mutlaq
The passage does not explicitly give the truth pattern for absolute generality-particularity, but it can be derived: if S is broader than P, then all P are S is true, but all S are P is false; some S are P is true, some S are not P is true; etc.
This pattern is not in the passage but is standard in Islamic logic.

Propositions

Truth Conditions of Categorical Propositions by Term Relations
Author classifies categorical propositions by subject-predicate relation into equality, partial overlap, and absolute generality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the truth values of the four categorical proposition forms given any pair of terms based on their logical relation (equality, partial overlap, absolute generality).

Core Idea
The truth of a categorical proposition depends on the set relation between its subject and predicate terms.

In this passage, the author lays out a systematic method to evaluate categorical propositions based on how the subject and predicate terms relate. Instead of memorizing arbitrary truth tables, you can now derive the truth of any proposition by understanding whether the terms are equal, partially overlapping, or one fully includes the other. This skill is fundamental for analyzing arguments in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logical relation between the two terms
Determine if the terms are coextensive (equality), partially overlapping (some but not all), or one term completely includes the other (absolute generality). Example: 'human' and 'speaking' are coextensive; 'human' and 'white' partially overlap; 'human' and 'animal' have absolute generality (human more specific).
Use set theory analogy if helpful.
2
Apply the rule for equality (musāwāt)
If terms are coextensive, then all affirmative propositions (universal and particular) are true, and all negative propositions are false. Test with 'human' and 'speaking'.
No counterexample exists.
3
Apply the rule for partial overlap (ʿumūm wa khuṣūṣ min wajh)
If terms partially overlap, then both universal propositions (affirmative and negative) are false, and both particular propositions are true. Test with 'human' and 'white'.
The author warns against the fallacy of mukhālafa here.
4
Apply the rule for absolute generality (ʿumūm wa khuṣūṣ muṭlaq)
This depends on which term is more general. If the subject is more specific (e.g., 'human' subject, 'animal' predicate), then it follows the same rule as equality: affirmatives true, negatives false. If the subject is more general (e.g., 'animal' subject, 'human' predicate), then both universals are false and both particulars are true (similar to partial overlap but with a different underlying relation).
The passage explicitly covers only the first subcase; the second is logically inferred.

فإن كان الموضوع أخصَّ مطلقا جرت على حكم المتساويين، فلو ركبت قضية من الإنسان والحيوان وجعلت الإنسان هو الموضوع، فإنها تصدق في كل إيجاب، وتكذب في كل سلب، كالمتساويين، فقولك : كل إنسان (حيوان)، أو (بعض الإنسان (حیوان کله صادق وقولك : (لا شيء من الإنسان بحيوان أو (بعض الإنسان ليس بحيوان)، كله كاذب.
وإن كان الموضوع أعم والمحمول أخص، كما لو ركبت قضية من الإنسان والحيوان وجعلت الحيوان موضوعًا والإنسان محمولاً،
And if the relation between its two terms is equality, then it is true in both affirmatives, false in both negatives – I mean universal affirmative and particular affirmative, and universal negative and particular negative – so it is never false in an affirmative, and never true in a negative. Thus, if you compose a proposition from 'human' and 'speaking', it is true in every affirmative, as when you say: 'Every human is speaking', 'Every speaking is human', 'Some human is speaking', 'Some speaking is human' – all that is true. And it is false in every negative, as when you say: 'No human is speaking', 'No speaking is human', 'Some human is not speaking', 'Some speaking is not human' – all that is false. And if the relation between its two terms is partial overlap (general and specific from one aspect), then it is false in both universals, true in both particulars – I mean particular affirmative and particular negative – so it is never false in a particular, and never true in a universal. (1) Note here that the concept of entailment of the contrary (mukhalafa) has no consideration in the art of logic, so one should not object to the truth of 'Some stone is not human' and the like by saying that it entails that some other stone is human, contradicting 'No stone is human'. Thus, if you compose a proposition from 'human' and 'white', it is true in every particular, as when you say: 'Some white is human', 'Some human is white', 'Some white is not human', 'Some human is not white' – all that is true. And it is false in every universal, as when you say: 'Every human is white', 'Every white is human', 'No human is white', 'No white is human' – all that is false. And if the relation between its two terms is absolute generality and specificity, then it has two states: First: that the subject is more specific and the predicate is more general. Second: that the subject is more general and the predicate is more specific. If the subject is absolutely more specific, it follows the rule of equal terms. So if you compose a proposition from 'human' and 'animal' and make 'human' the subject, it is true in every affirmative and false in every negative, like equal terms. So your saying: 'Every human is animal', or 'Some human is animal' – all that is true; and your saying: 'No human is animal' or 'Some human is not animal' – all that is false. And if the subject is more general and the predicate is more specific, as if you compose a proposition from 'human' and 'animal' and make 'animal' the subject and 'human' the predicate...
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage defines the truth conditions of categorical propositions based on the logical relation between subject and predicate terms. The author enumerates three types of relations: equality (musāwāt), partial overlap (ʿumūm wa khuṣūṣ min wajh), and absolute generality/specificity (ʿumūm wa khuṣūṣ muṭlaq). For each, he specifies which of the four standard categorical forms (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) are true or false.

First, when the terms are coextensive (equality), such as 'human' and 'speaking', both universal and particular affirmatives are true, while both universal and particular negatives are false. This is because every instance of one is an instance of the other.

Second, when the terms partially overlap (like 'human' and 'white'), each term applies to some instances of the other but not all. Here, both particular propositions (affirmative and negative) are true, because there exist humans who are white and humans who are not white. The universal propositions are false because not every human is white and not every white is human. The author adds a footnote warning against a common fallacy: from 'Some stone is not human' one should not infer that some other stone is human, because that would be applying the concept of implication of the contrary (mukhalafa) which is not valid in formal logic.

Third, the author treats the relation of absolute generality/specificity (one term is fully included in the other). This has two subcases: (A) when the subject is more specific and the predicate more general (e.g., 'human' as subject, 'animal' as predicate). Here the proposition behaves like equality: affirmatives are true, negatives false, because all humans are animals. (B) when the subject is more general and the predicate more specific (e.g., 'animal' as subject, 'human' as predicate). The passage cuts off before completing this subcase, but the expected rule is that the universal affirmative is false (not all animals are human), the particular affirmative is true (some animals are human), the universal negative is false (some animals are human), and the particular negative is true (some animals are not human).

Key Terms
المساواة (al-musāwāt): Equality: the relation between two terms when they are coextensive (every instance of one is an instance of the other and vice versa). [Note: Stable term across mantiq works; used consistently for coextensive terms.]
العموم والخصوص من وجه (al-ʿumūm wa al-khuṣūṣ min wajh): Partial overlap: two terms that apply to some instances of each other but not all. [Note: Standard term in categorical logic; no significant shift.]
العموم والخصوص المطلق (al-ʿumūm wa al-khuṣūṣ al-muṭlaq): Absolute generality/specificity: one term is entirely subsumed under the other (full inclusion). [Note: Standard term; no significant shift.]
إيجاب (ījāb): Affirmative judgment: a proposition that asserts the applicability of the predicate to the subject. [Note: Stable across logic works.]
سلب (salab): Negative judgment: a proposition that denies the applicability of the predicate to the subject. [Note: Stable.]
كلية (kulliyya): Universal proposition: a judgment that applies to every individual of the subject class. [Note: Stable.]
جزئية (juziyya): Particular proposition: a judgment that applies to some individuals of the subject class. [Note: Stable.]
مخالفة (mukhalafa): Entailment of the contrary: an invalid inference in logic where from a particular negative one erroneously infers that the complement must be affirmative. [Note: This term is mentioned as a fallacy; it is not a standard logical concept but a caution against a common error.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) No human is animal.
B) Some human is not animal.
C) Every human is animal.
D) Some human is animal.

Key Takeaway
The truth of categorical propositions is determined by the set relation (equality, partial overlap, or absolute generality) between subject and predicate, with specific rules for each relation.

Truth Conditions for General-Specific Absolute Relation
Author explains truth of propositions with general-specific absolute relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the truth value of any categorical proposition given that its terms have a general-specific absolute relation.

Core Idea
When one term includes the other, truth depends entirely on which term is subject.

In this passage, the author explains how to evaluate categorical propositions when the subject and predicate have a general-specific absolute relation. This is a foundational skill for analyzing statements in Islamic logic, used in disciplines like usul al-fiqh where jurists must assess the scope of rulings based on general or specific terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the relation
Determine whether the two terms are in a general-specific absolute relation, meaning one term's extension is entirely inside the other's, but not vice versa. For example, 'human' is specific to 'animal' because every human is an animal but not every animal is human.
This relation is distinct from equality and partial overlap.
2
Check which term is subject
Look at the categorical proposition and identify the subject term. The truth conditions differ based on whether the subject is the more specific term or the more general term.
The two states are: subject specific-predicate general, and subject general-predicate specific.
3
Apply the rule for subject = specific
If the subject is the more specific term (e.g., 'human' in 'Every human is animal'), then all affirmative propositions (universal and particular) are true, and all negative propositions are false. This is the same ruling as for terms with equal extension.
Example: 'Every human is animal' is true; 'No human is animal' is false.
4
Apply the rule for subject = general
If the subject is the more general term (e.g., 'animal' in 'Every animal is human'), then all particular propositions (affirmative and negative) are true, but all universal propositions are false. This matches the ruling for terms that are 'general from one aspect'.
Example: 'Some animal is human' is true; 'Every animal is human' is false.
5
Test with unrelated terms
When terms have no inclusion relation (like 'human' and 'white'), all universal propositions are false and all particular propositions are true, because there is partial overlap.
This is the only case where some particular negatives can be true; in the general-specific case, they are true only when subject is general.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And it is never true universally. So if you form a proposition from 'human' and 'white', it is true in every particular, like your saying: 'Some white is human' and 'Some human is white', and 'Some white is not human', and 'Some human is not white' – all that is true. And it is false in every universal, like your saying: 'Every human is white', and 'Every white is human', or 'No human is white', and 'No white is human' – all that is false. If the relation between its two terms is general-specific absolute, it has two states: the first: that the subject is more specific and the predicate more general. The second: that the subject is more general and the predicate more specific. If the subject is absolutely more specific, it follows the ruling of equals. So if you form a proposition from 'human' and 'animal' and make 'human' the subject, it is true in every affirmative and false in every negative, like equals. Your saying: 'Every human is animal', or 'Some human is animal' – all is true, and your saying: 'No human is animal' or 'Some human is not animal' – all is false. If the subject is more general and the predicate more specific, as if you formed a proposition from 'human' and 'animal' and made 'animal' the subject and 'human' the predicate, it proceeds according to the ruling of 'general from one aspect': it is not false in a particular, nor true in a universal. So if you say: 'Some animal is human' or 'Some animal is not human', all that is true, unlike the universal, for it is here absolutely false, as if you said: 'Every animal is human' or 'No animal is human' – all that is false.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage details the truth conditions for categorical propositions when the terms have a general-specific absolute relation (العموم والخصوص المطلق). The author begins by showing that when terms are completely unrelated in meaning (like 'human' and 'white' – where neither term includes the other), no universal proposition is ever true; all four universal forms (Every A is B, Every B is A, No A is B, No B is A) are false. However, every particular proposition (Some A is B, Some A is not B, etc.) is true because there is overlap. Then the author narrows to the general-specific absolute relation, where one term is entirely included in the other. He states two states: (1) subject is the more specific (e.g., human is subject, animal is predicate). In this case, the affirmative propositions (universal and particular) are true, and the negatives are false – exactly like the case of equal terms (المتساويين). So 'Every human is animal' and 'Some human is animal' are true; 'No human is animal' and 'Some human is not animal' are false. (2) subject is the more general (animal) and predicate the more specific (human). Here the truth conditions match those of the 'general from one aspect' relation: particulars are always true (both affirmative and negative), but universals are always false (both affirmative and negative). So 'Some animal is human' and 'Some animal is not human' are true; 'Every animal is human' and 'No animal is human' are false. The author uses clear examples from common terms like human, animal, and white to illustrate the logical pattern.

Key Takeaway
In a general-specific absolute relation, the truth of a categorical proposition depends on which term is the subject: if the subject is specific, affirmatives are true; if the subject is general, only particulars are true.

Classification of Propositions: Categorical vs Conditional
Author classifies propositions into categorical and conditional with criteria for categorical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a categorical proposition from a conditional proposition by applying the two criteria given in the text.

Core Idea
Propositions are either categorical (judgment absolute) or conditional (judgment dependent).

In this passage, the author lays down the foundational division of propositions (qadaya) into two exhaustive types: categorical (hamliyya) and conditional (shartiyya). He provides a precise criterion (ضابط) for the categorical proposition, which will be essential for understanding syllogistic reasoning in logic. This classification mirrors how jurists distinguish between unconditional rulings and conditional rulings in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition's extremes
Find the subject and predicate (or the two parts) in the statement. For example, in 'Zayd is standing', the extremes are 'Zayd' and 'standing'.
2
Check if extremes resolve into singulars
Verify that each extreme is a singular term or a phrase that functions as a single term (ma fi quwwat al-mufrad). For instance, 'the creator of the heavens' functions as a singular even though it is a phrase.
The text gives three kinds of 'what is in the power of the singular', but the passage cuts off.
3
Check if judgment is unconditional
Ensure the judgment is not dependent on a condition (e.g., no 'if', 'when'). If the truth of the statement depends on a condition, it is conditional.
4
Classify as categorical or conditional
If both conditions are met, the proposition is categorical; otherwise, it is conditional. For example, 'Man is animal' is categorical; 'If the sun rises, then it is day' is conditional.
5
Practice with Islamic examples
Test the classification on examples from kalam or fiqh. 'The world is contingent' is categorical; 'If someone prays, then he must face the qibla' is conditional.

فصل في مباديء التصديقات
اعلم أن مبادىء التصديقات هي القضايا وأحكامها .
والقضية في الاصطلاح هي التصديق»، وقد تقدم إيضاحه (۱) ، وتسمى: «القضية» و«الخبر»، و«التصديق»، وقد قدمنا أنه هو ما يعبر عنه في المعاني بالإسناد الخبري، وفي النحو بالجملة الاسمية أو الفعلية .
واعلم أن القضايا بالتقسيم الأول تنقسم إلى قسمين لا ثالث لهما، وهما القضية الحملية، والقضية الشرطية .

Chapter on the principles of assents. Know that the principles of assents are propositions and their judgments. The proposition in terminology is the assent, and its clarification has preceded, and it is called 'al-qadiyya', 'al-khabar', and 'al-tasdiq', and we have already stated that it is what is expressed in meanings by predicative attribution, and in grammar by the nominal or verbal sentence. And know that propositions, by the first division, are divided into two divisions with no third: the categorical proposition and the conditional proposition. The criterion of the categorical proposition is two things: First: that its two extremes resolve into two singulars, or what is in the power of singulars. Second: that the judgment in it is not conditional on anything. Its examples: 'Zayd is standing', 'Amr is sitting', 'Man is animal', and the like; for each of these examples, its two extremes resolve into two singulars, and the judgment in them is not conditional. And what is in the power of the singular is three kinds: First: that the subject is singular and the predicate is a sentence in the power of the singular...
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a definitional classification within the epistemology of logic, introducing the principles of assents (mabadi' al-tasdiqat). The author states that these principles are propositions (qadaya) and their judgments (ahkam). He defines the proposition terminologically as the assent (tasdiq), a concept already explained earlier. The proposition is also called 'al-qadiyya', 'al-khabar', or 'al-tasdiq', and it corresponds to what is expressed in meaning as predicative attribution (isnad khabari) and in grammar as a nominal or verbal sentence. Then the author gives the first division of propositions into two exhaustive kinds: categorical (hamliyya) and conditional (shartiyya). He provides two criteria for a categorical proposition: (1) its two extremes (subject and predicate) resolve into singular terms or what has the force of singulars, and (2) the judgment is not conditional on anything. Examples include 'Zayd is standing', 'Amr is sitting', and 'Man is animal' – each is formed from two singulars and the judgment is absolute, not dependent on a condition. He then begins to explain 'what is in the power of the singular' (ma fi quwwat al-mufrad), giving three kinds, and starts with the first: when the subject is singular and the predicate is a sentence in the power of a singular. The passage thus sets up the foundational distinction for syllogistic reasoning, analogous to how fiqh scholars distinguish between categorical and conditional rulings.

Propositions: Definition and Division into Categorical and Conditional
Author defines propositions and divides them into categorical and conditional types with criteria and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any proposition as categorical or conditional based on whether its parts resolve into singular terms or sentences and whether the judgment is conditional.

Core Idea
Every proposition is either categorical (unconditional) or conditional (dependent on a condition).

In logic, propositions are the building blocks of reasoning. This passage gives the first major division: into categorical (hamliyya) and conditional (shartiyya). Understanding this split is essential because arguments are built from propositions, and their logical behaviour depends on this classification. We will learn the two criteria for each type and how to identify 'in the force of a singular'.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the two criteria for categorical propositions
Read the passage's definition: first, each of its two parts (subject and predicate) must resolve into a singular term or something functioning like a singular; second, the judgment is not conditional (it asserts directly). Example: 'Zayd is standing' – both 'Zayd' and 'standing' are singular terms.
The 'singular term' (mufrad) is a simple word or phrase that does not form a complete sentence by itself.
2
Understand 'in the force of a singular'
Some sentences behave like singular terms because they can be reduced to a simple term. Study the three examples: subject singular with predicate sentence-like (Zayd's father is standing), subject sentence-like with predicate singular, and both sentence-like. Recognise that these are still categorical because the judgment is unconditional.
The predicate 'is standing his father' is reducible to 'father is standing'.
3
Learn the two criteria for conditional propositions
Now read the conditional definition: first, the two parts resolve into complete sentences (antecedent and consequent), not into singulars. Second, the judgment is conditional (dependent on the condition). Example: 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists'.
The connective tool (like 'if') is part of the structure; when removed, each part becomes a full sentence.
4
Apply the test to examples
Take each proposition given in the passage and decide if it is categorical or conditional. For categoricals, check whether each part is a singular or in force of a singular. For conditionals, check that both parts are sentences and the judgment is conditional.
Remember: The key difference is not syntactic complexity but whether the judgment is unconditional.

Source Text

واعلم أن القضايا بالتقسيم الأول تنقسم إلى قسمين لا ثالث لهما، وهما القضية الحملية، والقضية الشرطية .

الأول: أن يكون الموضوع مفردًا والمحمول جملة في قوة المفرد كقولك: زيد قائم أبوه ؛ لأن «قائم أبوه» في قوة «قائم الأب»، وقولك: «خير الذكر لا إله إلا الله» ؛ فإنه في قوة «خير الذكر كلمة لا إله إلا الله» .
الثاني : عكسه، وهو أن يكون الموضوع جملة في قوة المفرد والمحمول مفرداً ، كقولك : لا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله كنز من كنوز الجنة .
الثالث: أن يكون كل من الموضوع والمحمول جملة في قوة المفرد، كقولك : زيد عالم نقيض زيد ليس بعالم ؛ لأنه في قوة « قضية زيد عالم نقيضُ قضية زيد ليس بعالم » .
فتحصل أن الأقسام أربعة : مفردان، وأقسام ما في قوة المفرد الثلاثة المذكورة بأمثلتها آنفا .
وسميت حملية للحكم بمحمولها على أفراد موضوعها .
وأما القضية الشرطية فضابطها أمران :
الأول : أن ينحلّ طرفاها إلى جملتين، أعني أنه إن أزيلت أداة الربط في المتصلة، أو أداةُ العناد في المنفصلة بين طرفيها يصير كل من طرفيها - أعني مقدَّمَها وتاليها - جملة مستقلة .
والثاني : أن الحكم فيها معلّق .
فقولك مثلاً : لو كانت الشمس طالعة لكان النهار موجودا.
Chapter on the principles of assents. Know that the principles of assents are the propositions and their judgments. And the proposition in terminology is the assent, and its clarification has preceded. It is also called: 'the proposition', 'the report', and 'the assent'. We have stated that it is what is expressed in semantics as predicative attribution, and in grammar as the nominal or verbal sentence. And know that propositions by the first division are divided into two divisions with no third: the categorical proposition and the conditional proposition. And the definition of the categorical proposition is two matters: First: its two parts resolve into two singular terms, or what is in the force of two singulars. Second: the judgment in it is not dependent on anything. And its example: 'Zayd is standing', 'Amr is sitting', 'Man is an animal', and the like; for each of these examples its two parts resolve into two singulars, and the judgment in it is not dependent. And what is in the force of a singular has three divisions: First: that the subject is singular and the predicate is a sentence in the force of a singular, like your saying: 'Zayd's father is standing'; because 'is standing his father' is in the force of 'father is standing', and your saying: 'The best of remembrance is there is no god but Allah'; for it is in the force of 'The best of remembrance is the phrase there is no god but Allah'. Second: its opposite, which is that the subject is a sentence in the force of a singular and the predicate is singular, like your saying: 'There is no power and no strength except with Allah is a treasure from the treasures of Paradise'. Third: that both the subject and predicate are a sentence in the force of a singular, like your saying: 'Zayd is a scholar is the contradictory of Zayd is not a scholar'; because it is in the force of 'The proposition Zayd is a scholar is the contradictory of the proposition Zayd is not a scholar'. So it follows that the divisions are four: two singulars, and the three divisions of what is in the force of a singular mentioned with their examples earlier. And it is called categorical because of the judgment with its predicate upon the individuals of its subject. As for the conditional proposition, its definition is two matters: First: its two parts resolve into two sentences, meaning that if the connective tool in the conjunctive, or the tool of incompatibility in the disjunctive, is removed between its two parts, each of its two parts—namely its antecedent and its consequent—becomes an independent sentence. And second: the judgment in it is dependent. For example, your saying: 'If the sun is shining, then the daytime exists.'
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage opens a new section on the principles of assents (mabadi' al-tasdiqat). The author first establishes that these principles are propositions (qadaya) and their judgments. He then defines a proposition terminologically as an 'assent' (tasdiq) and notes it is also called 'report' (khabar) and 'assent' itself. He clarifies that in semantics it corresponds to predicative attribution, and in grammar to a complete sentence (nominal or verbal). The core of the passage is the first division of propositions into two and only two types: categorical (hamliyya) and conditional (shartiyya). For categorical propositions, he gives two defining characteristics: (1) each of the two parts (subject and predicate) resolves into a singular term or something in the force of a singular, meaning they are conceptually simple or reducible to a simple term; (2) the judgment is not conditional—it asserts directly without any 'if' or 'either'. Examples include simple statements like 'Zayd is standing' and 'Man is an animal'. The author then expands on what is meant by 'in the force of a singular' (ma fi quwwat al-mufrad). He enumerates three cases: (a) subject singular, predicate a sentence that functions like a single term (e.g., 'Zayd's father is standing' where 'his father' is part of the predicate); (b) reverse, subject a sentence-like term and predicate singular (e.g., 'There is no power and no strength except with Allah' is a treasure); (c) both subject and predicate are sentence-like terms (e.g., 'Zayd is a scholar is the contradictory of Zayd is not a scholar'). Adding the standard case of two singulars, we get four total divisions under categorical. The name 'categorical' (hamliyya) comes from the judgment (hukm) of the predicate upon the individuals of the subject. Then the author turns to conditional propositions. Their two characteristics: (1) the two parts resolve into full sentences (i.e., antecedent and consequent), not singular terms; (2) the judgment is conditional—it depends on a condition. He gives the example of a conjunctive conditional (mutasila): 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists'. The passage thus provides a clear, systematic classification of propositions foundational to logic.

قضية
حملية
شرطية
ما في قوة المفرد
مفرد

Key Takeaway
A proposition is either categorical (with singular parts and unconditional judgment) or conditional (with sentence parts and conditional judgment); this division is exhaustive and foundational for logical analysis.

Three Types of Compound Subject/Predicate in Categorical Propositions
Author enumerates three types of compound subject/predicate in categorical propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the four divisions of a categorical proposition a given sentence belongs to, based on whether the subject and predicate are single terms or phrases equivalent to single terms.

Core Idea
In categorical propositions, subjects and predicates can be sentences that function as single terms.

In classical logic, categorical propositions are not limited to simple terms like 'Zayd is standing'. They can involve whole sentences that logically behave like a single term. This lesson explains the three ways this happens, resulting in four total divisions of categorical propositions.

Guided Path
1
Recall the basic structure
Start with the simplest form: both subject and predicate are single terms (مفرد). This is the first division. For example, 'Zayd is standing' has a single term subject and predicate.
Most categorical propositions in logic textbooks assume this form.
2
Identify the first type: predicate as a phrase
In the first type, the predicate is a grammatically complete sentence that functions as a single term. Example: 'Zayd is such that his father is standing' – the predicate 'his father is standing' is equivalent to 'the father of Zayd is standing' and acts like a single predicate.
The Arabic example 'زيد قائم أبوه' is a common illustration.
3
Identify the second type: subject as a phrase
In the second type, the subject is a sentence equivalent to a single term, while the predicate is a single term. Example: 'There is no power and no strength except with Allah is a treasure from the treasures of Paradise' – the subject 'لا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله' is a phrase functioning as a single term.
This is the opposite (عكسه) of the first type.
4
Identify the third type: both as phrases
In the third type, both subject and predicate are sentences equivalent to single terms. Example: 'Zayd is knowledgeable is the opposite of Zayd is not knowledgeable' – both 'Zayd is knowledgeable' and 'Zayd is not knowledgeable' are propositions that act as single terms.
Logical relationships can be expressed this way.
5
Apply the classification
Given any categorical proposition, determine its division by checking each part: Is the subject a مفرد or in قوة المفرد? Same for the predicate. The four divisions are: (1) both مفرد, (2) subject مفرد and predicate in قوة المفرد, (3) subject in قوة المفرد and predicate مفرد, (4) both in قوة المفرد.
Memorize the three compound types to cover all possibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المفرد كقولك: زيد قائم أبوه ؛ لأن (قائم (أبوه) في قوة (قائم الأب)، وقولك: (خير الذكر لا إله إلا الله) ؛ فإنه في قوة (خير الذكر كلمة لا إله إلا الله) . الثاني : عكسه، وهو أن يكون الموضوع جملة في قوة المفرد والمحمول مفرداً ، كقولك : لا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله كنز من كنوز الجنة) . الثالث: أن يكون كل من الموضوع والمحمول جملة في قوة المفرد، كقولك : زيد عالم نقيض زيد ليس بعالم)؛ لأنه في قوة ( قضية زيد عالم نقيضُ قضية زيد ليس بعالم) . فتحصل أن الأقسام أربعة : مفردان، وأقسام ما في قوة المفرد الثلاثة المذكورة بأمثلتها آنفا . وسميت حملية للحكم بمحمولها على أفراد موضوعها .
The first type: the predicate is a sentence equivalent to a single term, like your saying: 'Zayd is such that his father is standing' because 'standing (his father)' is equivalent to 'standing is his father', and your saying: 'The best of remembrance is "La ilaha illa Allah"' because it is equivalent to 'The best of remembrance is the word "La ilaha illa Allah"'. Second: its opposite, i.e. the subject is a sentence equivalent to a single term and the predicate is a single term, like your saying: 'There is no power and no strength except with Allah is a treasure from the treasures of Paradise'. Third: both subject and predicate are sentences equivalent to a single term, like your saying: 'Zayd is knowledgeable is the opposite of Zayd is not knowledgeable' because it is equivalent to 'the proposition "Zayd is knowledgeable" is the opposite of the proposition "Zayd is not knowledgeable"'. Hence it follows that the divisions are four: [both are] single terms, and the three types of what is equivalent to a single term mentioned with their examples above. And it was called categorical (hamliyya) because of the judgment by its predicate on individuals of its subject.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage classifies the forms of categorical propositions (حمليات) based on whether the subject and predicate are single terms (مفرد) or phrases that function as a single term (جملة في قوة المفرد). The author begins by stating the first case: the predicate is a phrase that resolves into a single term. Two examples are given: 'Zayd is such that his father is standing' where the predicate 'his father is standing' is equivalent to 'standing is his father'? Actually the example is 'زيد قائم أبوه' which means 'Zayd, his father is standing' – it is a sentence but in the logical analysis it functions like a single predicate. Similarly, the phrase 'خير الذكر لا إله إلا الله' is equivalent to 'the best of remembrance is the word La ilaha illa Allah'. The second case is the reverse: the subject is a phrase in power of a single term while the predicate is a single term. Example: 'لا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله كنز من كنوز الجنة' – the phrase 'لا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله' is the subject. The third case is when both subject and predicate are phrases equivalent to single terms. Example: 'زيد عالم نقيض زيد ليس بعالم' – the subject 'زيد عالم' and predicate 'نقيض زيد ليس بعالم' are both propositions but function as terms. The author then concludes that the total divisions are four: both are single terms (the basic case) plus these three. Finally, the passage explains why this type of proposition is called 'حملي' (categorical): because the predicate is predicated of individuals of the subject. This is a technical classification important for understanding the structure of propositions in logic, especially when dealing with nominalizations or complex expressions.

Key Takeaway
Categorical propositions can have subjects and predicates that are grammatically full sentences but function as single terms in logical analysis.
