Definition and Division of Conditional Propositions
Author defines the conditional proposition with two conditions and divides it into conjunctive and disjunctive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two defining conditions of a conditional proposition and classify a given conditional as conjunctive or disjunctive.

Core Idea
A conditional proposition is defined by two conditions: parts are full sentences and the judgment is suspended.

In this passage, the author establishes the precise definition of the conditional proposition with two essential constraints. Understanding these conditions is crucial for recognizing conditional statements in Islamic logic and distinguishing them from categorical ones.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
From the passage, extract the two 'ضابطان' (defining constraints): first, that the extremes resolve into two independent sentences when connectives are removed; second, that the judgment is suspended.
2
Apply to a conjunctive example
Take the example 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists.' Verify both conditions: remove 'if' and 'then' to get two sentences, and note that the existence of daytime depends on the sun's shining.
3
Apply to a disjunctive example
Take 'The number is either even or odd.' Remove 'either...or' to get two sentences, and note that the judgment of evenness is suspended on the negation of oddness.
4
Distinguish conjunctive from disjunctive
Conjunctive conditionals allow both parts to be true together and false together; disjunctive conditionals (as defined here) involve mutual exclusivity where one is true only if the other is false.
5
Classify a new example
Given a new conditional statement, determine if it is conjunctive or disjunctive by checking the possible truth values of its parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القضية الشرطية فضابطها أمران : الأول : أن ينحلّ طرفاها إلى جملتين، أعني أنه إن أزيلت أداة الربط في المتصلة، أو أداةُ العناد في المنفصلة بين طرفيها يصير كل من طرفيها - أعني مقدَّمَها وتاليها - جملة مستقلة . والثاني : أن الحكم فيها معلّق . فقولك مثلاً : (لو) كانت الشمس طالعة لكان النهار موجودا)، قضية شرطية متصلة ؛ لأنك لو أزلت أداة الربط بين مقدَّمها وهو الطرف المقترن بـ (لو)، وبين تاليها وهو الطرف المقترن باللام في المثال المذكور - ونعني بأداة الربط لفظ (لو) واللام - صار المقدَّم (كانت الشمس طالعة وهي قضية حملية ؛ لأنها مبتدأ وخبر، دخل عليها فعل ناسخ وكان التالي (النهار) (موجود) وهو قضية حملية؛ لأنه مبتدأ وخبر، مع أن الحكم بوجود النهار معلق على طلوع الشمس، وكل قضية كان الحكم فيها معلقا وكانت ينحل طرفاها إلى جملتين فهي القضية الشرطية . وكذلك لو قلت : العدد إما زوج وإما فرد) فهذه شرطية منفصلة، فلو زالت أداة العناد بين طرفيها وهي لفظة (إما) صار الطرف الأول (العدد زوج و الطرف الثاني ( العدد (فرد)، وكلاهما قضية حملية. ووجه تعليق الحكم في المنفصلة أن كون العدد - مثلاً - زوجًا معلقا على نفي الفردية عنه، كعكسه . واعلم أن القضية الشرطية تنقسم بالتقسيم الأول إلى قسمين : أحدثهما الشرطية المتصلة، والثاني : الشرطية المنفصلة . وإيضاح كل واحدة منهما أن الشرطية المتصلة هي التي يجتمع طرفاها في الوجود ويجتمعان في العدم، بمعنى أنهما يجوز عدمهما معا ، ويجوز وجودهما معا، واجتماعهما في الوجود واجتماعهما في العدم.
As for the conditional proposition, its defining constraints are two: First, that its two extremes resolve into two sentences, meaning that if the connective tool in the conjunctive, or the tool of opposition in the disjunctive, is removed between its two extremes, each of its two extremes – i.e. its antecedent and consequent – becomes an independent sentence. Second, that the judgment in it is suspended. For example, your saying: 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists' is a conjunctive conditional proposition, because if you remove the connective tool between its antecedent, which is the extreme connected by 'If' (law), and its consequent, which is the extreme connected by the lām in the mentioned example – and we mean by the connective tool the word 'law' and the lām – then the antecedent becomes 'the sun is shining', which is a categorical proposition because it is a subject and predicate with a copulative verb, and the consequent becomes 'daytime exists', which is a categorical proposition because it is a subject and predicate, while the judgment of the existence of daytime is suspended on the sun's shining. And every proposition whose judgment is suspended and whose extremes resolve into two sentences is the conditional proposition. Similarly, if you say: 'The number is either even or odd', this is a disjunctive conditional. If the tool of opposition between its two extremes, which is the word 'either' (immā), is removed, the first extreme becomes 'the number is even' and the second extreme becomes 'the number is odd', and both are categorical propositions. The aspect of the suspension of the judgment in the disjunctive is that the number being even, for example, is suspended on the negation of oddness from it, and likewise the opposite. Know that the conditional proposition is divided by the first division into two parts: one is the conjunctive conditional, and the second is the disjunctive conditional. Clarification of each of them: the conjunctive conditional is that whose two extremes can co-exist and can be co-absent, meaning that both can be absent together and both can exist together, and their co-existence in existence and their co-existence in non-existence.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage defines the conditional proposition (الشرطية) with two essential conditions. The first condition is that its two parts—the antecedent (مقدم) and consequent (تالي)—must each be a complete sentence when the connective tool is removed. For conjunctive conditionals like 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists', removing the tool 'law' and the lām yields two independent categorical propositions: 'the sun is shining' and 'daytime exists'. The second condition is that the judgment (hukm) is suspended or conditional (معلق) on something. In the conjunctive example, the existence of daytime is dependent on the sun's shining. For disjunctive conditionals like 'The number is either even or odd', removing the tool 'immā' yields 'the number is even' and 'the number is odd', both categorical. The suspension here is mutual: evenness depends on negation of oddness, and vice versa. The passage then divides the conditional into two types: conjunctive (متصلة) and disjunctive (منفصلة). It clarifies that the conjunctive conditional is characterized by the possibility of both parts being true together and both false together—i.e., they are compatible in truth and falsity. This is in contrast to the disjunctive, which typically has parts that cannot be both true nor both false (متنافيان), though the definition of disjunctive is not fully given here. The passage ends with the beginning of the explanation of the conjunctive. This is a foundational classification used in syllogistic and dialectical reasoning.

Key Terms
قضية شرطية
متصلة
منفصلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Its subject must be a single term
B) Its two extremes must be connectable
C) The judgment in it is suspended (معلق)
D) It must contain the word 'if'

Key Takeaway
A conditional proposition is defined by two constraints: resolvability into two independent sentences and suspension of the judgment; it divides into conjunctive (compatible) and disjunctive (exclusive).

The Conditional Proposition: Definition and Types
Author defines conditional proposition and divides it into connected and disconnected.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a conditional proposition and classify it as connected or disconnected based on the relationship between its parts.

Core Idea
Conditional propositions resolve into two categorical sentences; type depends on whether the parts can coincide in truth or are mutually exclusive.

In this passage, the author defines what a conditional proposition is and then divides it into two primary types: connected and disconnected. Understanding this division is crucial because each type has different logical properties. We will work through the definition and then practice distinguishing between the two types using the author's examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining feature of a conditional proposition
Look for a judgment that is suspended on a condition. The author says: 'every proposition whose judgment is conditioned and whose two parts resolve into two sentences is the conditional proposition.' The key is that removing the conditional tool (like 'law' or 'imma') reveals two independent categorical propositions.
The condition is expressed by tools like 'if' (law) or 'either...or' (imma).
2
Check the relationship between the two parts after removing the tool
For a connected conditional (muttasila), both parts can be true together and false together. For a disconnected conditional (munfasila), the parts are mutually exclusive: they cannot both be true and cannot both be false. The author gives the example of 'the number is either even or odd' as disconnected.
The connected conditional is called 'connected' because the parts are connected in their truth and falsehood; the disconnected is called 'disconnected' because they are separated by exclusion.
3
Apply the classification to new examples
Take a statement like 'If the prayer is fajr, then it is two rak'ats.' Is the antecedent 'the prayer is fajr' and consequent 'it is two rak'ats'? Can both be true together? Yes. Can both be false? Yes, if it is not fajr and not two rak'ats. So it is connected. Now take 'Either the prayer is fajr or it is maghrib.' This is disconnected because it cannot be both fajr and maghrib, and it cannot be neither (assuming it is one of the five prayers).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قضية شرطية متصلة ؛ لأنك لو أزلت أداة الربط بين مقدَّمها وهو الطرف المقترن بـ (لو)، وبين تاليها وهو الطرف المقترن باللام في المثال المذكور - ونعني بأداة الربط لفظ (لو) واللام - صار المقدَّم (كانت الشمس طالعة وهي قضية حملية ؛ لأنها مبتدأ وخبر، دخل عليها فعل ناسخ وكان التالي (النهار) (موجود) وهو قضية حملية؛ لأنه مبتدأ وخبر، مع أن الحكم بوجود النهار معلق على طلوع الشمس، وكل قضية كان الحكم فيها معلقا وكانت ينحل طرفاها إلى جملتين فهي القضية الشرطية .
وكذلك لو قلت : العدد إما زوج وإما فرد) فهذه شرطية منفصلة، فلو زالت أداة العناد بين طرفيها وهي لفظة (إما) صار الطرف الأول (العدد زوج و الطرف الثاني ( العدد (فرد)، وكلاهما قضية حملية. ووجه تعليق الحكم في المنفصلة أن كون العدد - مثلاً - زوجًا معلقا على نفي الفردية عنه، كعكسه .
واعلم أن القضية الشرطية تنقسم بالتقسيم الأول إلى قسمين : أحدهما الشرطية المتصلة، والثاني : الشرطية المنفصلة . وإيضاح كل واحدة منهما أن الشرطية المتصلة هي التي يجتمع طرفاها في الوجود، ويجتمعان في العدم، بمعنى أنهما يجوز عدمُهما معا، ويجوز وجودهما معا، واجتماعهما في الوجود واجتماعهما في العدم هو معنى الاتصال فسميت متصلة لاتصال طرفيها في كونهما موجودين، واتصالهما في كونهما معدومين . فقولك : ( لو كانت الشمس طالعة لكان النهار موجودًا) يجتمع طرفاها في الوجود فتكون الشمس طالعة والنهار موجوداً، ويجتمعان في العدم فتكون الشمس ليست بطالعة والنهار ليس بموجود، كما يقع في زمن الليل واجتماعهما في الوجود والعدم هو معنى الاتصال الذي سميت بسببه متصلة .
واعلم أن الشرطية المتصلة تنقسم إلى قسمين وهما الشرطية المتصلة اللزومية والشرطية المتصلة الاتفاقية، وتحقيق الفرق بينهما لا يستغني عنه طالب علم . وقد ارتبك خلق كثير من أهل العلم في تحقيق معنى الشرط والجزاء في نحو ( لو لم يخف الله لم يعصه) ونحوها من القضايا ؛ بسبب عدم الفرق بين اللزومية والاتفاقيه، ولو فرقوا بينهما لم يرتبكوا .
وإيضاح الفرق بينهما هو أن اللزومية لابد أن يكون اتصال مقدّمها
A conditional connected proposition: because if you remove the connective tool between its antecedent (which is the term paired with 'law') and its consequent (which is the term paired with the lam in the mentioned example)—and we mean by the connective tool the word 'law' and the lam—then the antecedent becomes 'The sun is rising' (which is a categorical proposition because it is subject and predicate with a copulative verb added), and the consequent becomes 'The day exists' (which is a categorical proposition because it is subject and predicate), while the judgment of the existence of the day is conditioned upon the rising of the sun. And every proposition whose judgment is conditioned and whose two parts resolve into two sentences is the conditional proposition. Similarly, if you say: 'The number is either even or odd'—this is a disconnected conditional. So if the tool of disjunction between its two parts, which is the word 'either', is removed, the first part becomes 'The number is even' and the second part becomes 'The number is odd', and both are categorical propositions. And the aspect of the conditional judgment in the disconnected proposition is that the number being even, for example, is conditioned upon the negation of oddness from it, and vice versa. Know that the conditional proposition is divided by the first division into two types: one is the connected conditional, and the second is the disconnected conditional. And the explanation of each of them is that the connected conditional is that whose two parts are both possible in existence and both possible in non-existence, meaning that it is possible for both to be non-existent together and possible for both to exist together. And their coexistence in existence and their coexistence in non-existence is the meaning of connectedness. So it is called connected due to the connection of its two parts in being existent and their connection in being non-existent. For your statement: 'If the sun were rising, then the day would be existent'—its two parts coincide in existence (the sun rising and the day existent) and coincide in non-existence (the sun not rising and the day not existent), as occurs at nighttime. And their coexistence in existence and non-existence is the meaning of connectedness because of which it is called connected. Know that the connected conditional is divided into two types: the necessary connected conditional and the coincidental connected conditional. And the verification of the difference between them is something a student of knowledge cannot dispense with. And many learned people have fallen into confusion in verifying the meaning of the condition and the result in statements like 'If he did not fear Allah, he would not disobey Him' and similar propositions, due to not differentiating between the necessary and the coincidental. And if they had differentiated between them, they would not have been confused. And the clarification of the difference between them is that the necessary [conditional] requires that the connection of its antecedent
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definition and classification of conditional propositions (al-qadaya al-shartiyya). The author begins by demonstrating that a conditional proposition, such as 'if the sun is rising, then the day exists', reduces to two simple categorical propositions when the conditional connective (law and lam) is removed. The antecedent becomes 'the sun is rising' and the consequent becomes 'the day exists', both of which are categorical sentences with subject and predicate. The key is that the judgment (hukm) of the consequent is suspended on the condition of the antecedent. This is the defining feature of a conditional proposition: a judgment that is conditional (muta'alliq) and whose two parts resolve into two independent sentences. The author then gives an example of a disconnected conditional (shartiyya munfasila): 'the number is either even or odd'. Removing the tool of disjunction ('imma') leaves two categorical propositions. The conditional aspect here is that being even is contingent on not being odd, and vice versa. Next, the author divides the conditional proposition into two primary types: connected (muttasila) and disconnected (munfasila). The connected conditional is characterized by the possibility of both parts being true together and both false together—they coincide in existence and non-existence. This is exemplified by the sun-day example: when the sun rises, the day exists; when the sun does not rise, the day does not exist. This simultaneous truth and falsehood is the essence of 'connectedness' (ittisal). Finally, the author subdivides the connected conditional into two subtypes: necessary (luzumiyya) and coincidental (ittifaqiyya). The necessary type involves an essential connection between antecedent and consequent, while the coincidental type involves a mere accidental conjunction. The author warns that many scholars have erred by confusing these two, especially in interpreting religious-legal conditionals like 'If he did not fear Allah, he would not disobey Him', which is a necessary connection (fear of Allah implies non-disobedience). The text breaks off while explaining that in the necessary conditional, the connection of the antecedent must be such that... (the conclusion is missing due to the missing page).

شرطية متصلة
شرطية منفصلة

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a disconnected conditional (shartiyya munfasila)?
A) If the sun rises, then the day exists.
B) Either the number is even or it is odd.
C) If a person is a scholar, then he is respected.
D) If it rains, then the ground becomes wet.

Key Takeaway
A conditional proposition has a conditioned judgment; its parts are categorical sentences, and it is either connected (parts can both be true/false) or disconnected (mutually exclusive).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Consider the Quranic statement: 'If you fear (from a debtor) that he is in difficult circumstances, then defer (the debt) until ease' (2:280). Identify the antecedent and consequent. Is this a connected or disconnected conditional? Explain why.
?
2. Give an original example from fiqh of a disconnected conditional (e.g., 'Either the contract is valid or it is void'). Classify it and justify.

Connected Conditionals: Necessary vs. Coincidental
Author subdivides connected conditional into necessary and coincidental, warning of common confusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish necessary from coincidental connected conditionals and explain the interpretive confusion they can cause.

Core Idea
Not all connected conditionals are necessary; some are coincidental, and failing to differentiate leads to errors in understanding religious texts.

The author now subdivides the connected conditional into two types: luzumiyya (necessary) and ittifaqiyya (coincidental). He warns that many scholars have erred by not distinguishing them, especially when interpreting statements like 'If he did not fear Allah, he would not disobey Him.' We will unpack the difference and see why it matters for both logic and Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Recall the nature of the connected conditional
In a connected conditional, both antecedent and consequent can be true together and false together. For the necessary type, there is an essential, causal connection: the truth of the antecedent necessitates the truth of the consequent. For the coincidental type, the connection is accidental; there is no necessary entailment.
The author says the necessary conditional 'requires that the connection of its antecedent...' (text breaks off, but the idea is that the antecedent must imply the consequent).
2
Apply the criteria to the example given
The author mentions 'If he did not fear Allah, he would not disobey Him.' In this case, the fear of Allah necessarily implies non-disobedience. It is not a mere coincidence; the connection is logical and essential. So it is luzumiyya. If it were coincidental, there would be no necessary link, e.g., 'If the door is open, then the scholar is in the house'—the door could be open without the scholar being present.
This example shows that many scholars mistakenly treated it as coincidental, leading to confusion.
3
Identify the practical importance
In Islamic theology and jurisprudence, understanding the type of condition is crucial for deriving implications. A necessary condition in a Quranic verse (e.g., 'If they turn away, then only you are responsible for delivery' — 16:82) must be interpreted as a necessary consequence, not a mere coincidence.
The author states that verifying the difference is something a student of knowledge cannot dispense with.

Implicative vs Coincidental Conditionals
Definition and division of the connected conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an implicative (lāzimiyya) and a coincidental (ittifāqiyya) conditional proposition using the three types of connection (ʿaqlī, sharʿī, ʿādī).

Core Idea
A conditional must be tested for real connection—intellectual, legal, or customary—not assumed.

This passage addresses a common source of confusion in Islamic dialectic: mistakenly treating a coincidental conditional as if it were implicative. The author clarifies that a true implicative conditional has a necessary bond between antecedent and consequent, whether the bond is based on reason, divine law, or custom. A coincidental conditional lacks any such bond; the two parts merely happen to be true together.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of the connected conditional
Read the opening sentences: the connection means both parts are true together and false together. Identify the example of sun and day. Notice that this connection in both existence and non-existence is the defining feature of 'connected'.
The example 'If the sun is risen, the day exists' shows coexistence and co-absence.
2
Distinguish the two types: lāzimiyya and ittifāqiyya
The author stresses the importance of distinguishing them. For lāzimiyya, there must be a necessitating factor (cause, necessary condition) linking antecedent and consequent. For ittifāqiyya, no factor exists; the conjunction is accidental.
Many scholars err here, especially with statements like 'If he did not fear Allah, he would not disobey Him'.
3
Analyze the three categories of necessary connection
The implicative connection can be intellectual (e.g., human implies animal), legal (e.g., zenith implies prayer), or customary (e.g., water implies plant). Recognize that each category provides a real, non-accidental link.
In each case, the antecedent necessitates the consequent in existence and non-existence.
4
Apply the test to a potentially confusing example
Take the statement 'If he did not fear Allah, he would not disobey Him'. Determine whether the connection between lack of fear and lack of disobedience is necessary or coincidental using the three categories. Consider: is there an intellectual, legal, or customary bond?
The author suggests this is a common stumbling block; the connection may be coincidental, as fear does not always prevent disobedience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العدم هو معنى الاتصال فسميت متصلة لاتصال طرفيها في كونهما موجودين، واتصالهما في كونهما معدومين. فقولك: (لو كانت الشمس طالعة لكان النهار موجودًا) يجتمع طرفاها في الوجود فتكون الشمس طالعة والنهار موجوداً، ويجتمعان في العدم فتكون الشمس ليست بطالعة والنهار ليس بموجود، كما يقع في زمن الليل واجتماعهما في الوجود والعدم هو الاتصال الذي سميت بسببه متصلة. واعلم أن الشرطية المتصلة تنقسم إلى قسمين وهما الشرطية المتصلة اللزومية والشرطية المتصلة الاتفاقية، وتحقيق الفرق بينهما لا يستغني عنه طالب علم. وقد ارتبك خلق كثير من أهل العلم في تحقيق معنى الشرط والجزاء في نحو (لو لم يخف الله لم يعصه) ونحوها من القضايا؛ بسبب عدم الفرق بين اللزومية والاتفاقية، ولو فرقوا بينهما لم يرتبكوا. وإيضاح الفرق بينهما هو أن اللزومية لابد أن يكون اتصال مقدمها بتاليها في الوجود والعدم لموجب يقتضي ذلك، ككون أحدهما سببًا للآخر والآخر مسببا له، أو كون أحدهما ملزوما للآخر والآخر لازما له، وبذلك الموجب المقتضي للارتباط بينهما في الوجود والعدم سميت لزومية. وسواء كان ذلك الارتباط بينهما المذكور عقليا أو شرعيا أو عاديا. ومثال العقلي عندهم كلما كان هذا إنسانًا كان حيوانا؛ لأن الحيوان عندهم جزء الإنسان الذي هو أعم منه. والعقل يمنع الكل عن جزئه، فكونه إنسانًا ملزوم لكونه حيوانا وكونه حيوانا لازم لكونه إنسانًا، وذلك اللزوم عقلي. ومثال الشرعي قولك: كلما زالت الشمس وجبت صلاة الظهر وكلما وجد شهر رمضان وجب الصوم؛ لأن كلاً من زوال الشمس ووجود رمضان سبب شرعي للعبادة المذكورة من صلاة أو صيام. ومثال العادي قولك: كلما لم يكن ماء لم يكن نبات؛ لأن الله أجرى العادة بأن وجود الماء سبب لوجود النبات، وعدمه سبب لعدمه. وأما الشرطية المتصلة الاتفاقية فضابطها أنها لا ربط بين مقدمها وتاليها، أي لا علاقة بينهما أصلاً، لا عقلاً ولا شرعًا ولا عادة، ولكن اتفق في الخارج أن صدق كل منهما مع صدق الأخرى، أي اجتمعا في الوجود، مع أنه لا أثر لنفي أحدهما - لو فرض - في نفي الآخر.
Non-existence is the meaning of connection, so it is called 'connected' because its two sides are connected in both being existent and both being non-existent. Your statement: 'If the sun were risen, then the day would exist' — its two sides come together in existence, so the sun is risen and the day exists, and they come together in non-existence, so the sun is not risen and the day does not exist, as occurs in night-time; and their coming together in existence and non-existence is the connection due to which it is called 'connected'. Know that the connected conditional is divided into two types: the implicative connected conditional and the coincidental connected conditional, and verifying the difference between them is indispensable for the student of knowledge. Many scholars have become confused in verifying the meaning of the antecedent and consequent in examples like 'If he did not fear Allah, he would not disobey Him' and similar propositions, due to the failure to distinguish between the implicative and the coincidental. Had they distinguished between them, they would not have been confused. Clarifying the difference between them is that the implicative must have a connection between its antecedent and consequent in existence and non-existence due to a necessitating factor, such as one being a cause of the other and the other being an effect, or one being a necessary condition for the other and the other being a necessary consequence; and due to that necessitating factor for the connection between them in existence and non-existence, it is called 'implicative'. Whether that mentioned connection between them is intellect-based, legal, or customary. An example of the intellect-based is: 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal'; because the animal according to them is a part of the human, which is more general than him. Intellect prevents the whole from its part, so being a human necessitates being an animal, and being an animal is necessary for being a human, and that implicative connection is intellect-based. An example of the legal is your statement: 'Whenever the sun has passed its zenith, the noon prayer becomes obligatory' and 'Whenever the month of Ramadan occurs, fasting becomes obligatory'; because each of the sun's passage and the occurrence of Ramadan is a legal cause for the mentioned worship, whether prayer or fasting. An example of the customary is your statement: 'Whenever there is no water, there is no plant'; because Allah has established the custom that the existence of water is a cause for the existence of plants, and its non-existence is a cause for their non-existence. As for the coincidental connected conditional, its criterion is that there is no connection between its antecedent and consequent, i.e., no relation between them at all, neither intellect-based, nor legal, nor customary, but it coincidentally happens in reality that each is true along with the truth of the other, i.e., they come together in existence, while the negation of one has no effect — if assumed — on the negation of the other.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage defines the connected conditional proposition (sharṭiyya muttaṣila) and then divides it into two types: implicative (lāzimiyya) and coincidental (ittifāqiyya). The author begins by explaining the term 'connected' (muttaṣila): it refers to the connection of the two sides (antecedent and consequent) in both existence and non-existence. For instance, the example 'If the sun is risen, then the day exists' shows that both occur together in reality and both are absent together at night. This is the 'connection' that gives the conditional its name. Next, the author asserts the importance of distinguishing between the two types, criticizing many scholars who confuse them, especially in interpreting a statement like 'If he did not fear Allah, he would not disobey Him.' Such confusion arises precisely from failing to recognize whether the connection is implicative or merely coincidental. The implicative conditional (lāzimiyya) requires a real connection: the antecedent must necessitate the consequent (or vice versa) in both existence and non-existence, due to a necessitating factor. This factor can be intellect-based (ʿaqlī), legal (sharʿī), or customary (ʿādī). For an intellect-based example, 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal' — the relation is that humanity entails animality because animal is part of the definition of human. For a legal example, 'Whenever the sun has passed its zenith, the noon prayer becomes obligatory' — the sun's passage is a legal cause for obligation. For a customary example, 'Whenever there is no water, there is no plant' — God's custom links water and plant life. The coincidental conditional (ittifāqiyya) lacks any such connection. There is no intellect-based, legal, or customary link; it is merely a coincidence that both happen to be true together in the external world. Negating one has no bearing on the truth of the other. This distinction is crucial for correctly analyzing conditional statements in dialectical reasoning.

Key Terms
شرطية متصلة (sharṭiyya muttaṣila): A conditional proposition whose two parts (antecedent and consequent) are connected in existence and non-existence due to some relation. [Note: In some earlier logic texts, muttaṣila is simply the conditional as opposed to munfaṣila; here it is specifically the connected type.]
لزومية (lāzimiyya): An implicative conditional where antecedent and consequent have a real necessary connection, whether intellect-based, legal, or customary. [Note: Stable term across the tradition.]
اتفاقية (ittifāqiyya): A coincidental conditional with no necessary connection; the truth of antecedent and consequent together is merely accidental. [Note: Stable term.]
مقدم (muqaddam): The antecedent part of a conditional proposition. [Note: Stable.]
تالي (tālī): The consequent part of a conditional proposition. [Note: Stable.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of an implicative conditional (sharṭiyya lāzimiyya) based on legal (sharʿī) connection?
A) Whenever the sun is risen, the day exists.
B) Whenever the sun has passed its zenith, the noon prayer becomes obligatory.
C) Whenever there is no water, there is no plant.
D) Whenever the sky is clear, the grass is green.

Key Takeaway
The validity of reasoning with conditionals depends on correctly identifying whether the connection is necessary (lāzimiyya) or merely coincidental (ittifāqiyya).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

(b) 'Whenever a human exists, an animal exists.' (c) 'Whenever there is no water, there is no plant.'

Classification of Conditional Propositions: Necessary vs. Accidental
Author defines necessary and accidental conditionals, subdividing necessary into rational, legal, and customary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between necessary (lizumiyya) and accidental (ittifaqiyya) conditional propositions by identifying the type of connection (rational, legal, or customary) or its absence.

Core Idea
Not every 'if' hides a real link—conditionals can be purely coincidental.

Conditional propositions are common in Islamic reasoning, from fiqh rulings to Quranic verses. But not all conditionals are created equal. Some express a genuine connection—whether logical, legal, or customary—while others merely happen to be true together. This lesson shows you how to classify conditionals based on the nature of the link between the antecedent and consequent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the antecedent and consequent
Read the conditional proposition and isolate the 'if' part (muqaddam) and the 'then' part (tali). In Arabic, these are often introduced by 'كلما' or 'إن' or 'لو'.
For example, in 'كلما زالت الشمس وجبت صلاة الظهر', the antecedent is 'زالت الشمس' and the consequent is 'وجبت صلاة الظهر'.
2
Check for a causal or logical connection
Ask: Is there a reason the antecedent leads to the consequent? Does the truth of one necessitate the truth of the other? If yes, it is a necessary conditional (lizumiyya). If not, it is accidental (ittifaqiyya).
3
Determine the type of necessity
If the connection exists, classify it as rational ('aqli), legal (shar'i), or customary ('adi). Rational: based on logic, e.g., part-whole. Legal: based on divine law, e.g., cause-effect in worship. Customary: based on Allah's habit in nature.
Examples from passage: Rational (human→animal), Legal (zenith→prayer), Customary (no water→no plant).
4
Confirm accidental by lack of any connection
If no rational, legal, or customary link exists, the conditional is accidental. The simultaneous truth is coincidental. The Quranic examples illustrate this.
Example: 'كلما كانت الشمس طالعة كان الإنسان ناطقا'—no relation between sunrise and speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بتاليها في الوجود والعدم لموجب يقتضي ذلك، ككون أحدهما سببًا للآخر والآخرِ مُسَبَّبا له ، أو كون أحدهما ملزوما للآخر والآخر لازما له، وبذلك الموجب المقتضي للارتباط بينهما في الوجود والعدم سميت لزومية . عاديًا . وسواء كان ذلك الارتباط بينهما المذكور عقليا أو شرعيًا أو ومثال العقلي عندهم كلما كان هذا إنسانًا كان حيوانا)؛ لأن الحيوان عندهم جزء الإنسان الذي هو أعم منه . انفكاك منه . والعقل يمنع الكل عن جزئه، فكونه إنسانًا ملزوم لكونه حيوانا وكونه حيوانا لازم لكونه إنسانًا، وذلك اللزوم عقلي . ومثال الشرعي قولك : كلما زالت الشمس وجبت صلاة الظهر) و(كلما وُجد شهر رمضان وجب الصوم)؛ لأن كلاً من زوال الشمس و وجود رمضان سبب شرعي للعبادة المذكورة من صلاة أو صيام . ومثال العادي قولك : كلما) لم يكن ماء لم يكن نبات)؛ لأن الله أجرى العادة بأن وجود الماء سبب لوجود النبات، وعدمه سبب لعدمه. وأما الشرطية المتصلة الاتفاقية فضابطها أنها لا ربط بين مقدمها وتاليها ، أي لا علاقة بينهما أصلاً ، لا عقلاً ولا شرعًا ولا عادة، ولكن اتفق في الخارج أن صدق كل منهما مع صدق الأخرى، أي اجتمعا في أنه لا أثر لنفي أحدهما - لو فرض ـ في نفي الآخر ولا عدده؛ إذ لا علاقة بينهما أصلاً كقولك كلما كانت الشمس طالعة كان الإنسان ناطقا فلا علاقة أصلاً بين طلوع الشمس وبين نطق الإنسان . ومن أمثلتها في القرآن قوله - تعالى -: ﴿ لَوْ كُنتُمْ فِي بُيُوتِكُمْ لَبَرَزَ الَّذِينَ كُتِبَ عَلَيْهِمُ الْقَتْلُ إِلَى مَضَاجِعِهِمْ ﴾ [آل عمران/ ١٥٤]؛ لأن كينونتهم في بيوتهم وبروزهم إلى مضاجعهم لا علاقة بينهما، ولا يستلزم أحدهما الآخر ولا عدمه. وكقوله - تعالى -: ﴿ قُل لَّوْ كَانَ الْبَحْرُ مِدَادًا لِكَلِمَاتِ رَبِّي لَنَفِدَ الْبَحْرُ قَبْلَ أَن تَنفَدَ كَلِمَتُ رَبِّي ﴾ [الكهف/ ١٠٩] ؛ لأن كون البحر مدادًا لها لا علاقة له بنفادها ولا عدمه. وكقوله - تعالى -: ﴿ وَإِن تَدْعُهُمْ إِلَى الْهُدَى فَلَن يَهْتَدُوا إِذا أَبدا ) [الكهف/ ٥٧]؛ لأن دعاءه إياهم إلى الهدى لا علاقة بينه وبين عدم اهتدائهم . ومن هذا القبيل قولهم : (لو لم يخف الله لم يعصه)؛ لأن عدم خوفه من الله لا أثر له في عدم عصيانه . بل قد يكون سببًا لعصيانه فيما يظهر للناظر . وقد نبهنا في كتابنا (أضواء البيان) (۱) على غلط الزمخشري (٢) .
...in existence and non-existence due to a cause that necessitates that, like one being a cause for the other and the other being caused by it, or one being a necessary concomitant of the other and the other being a necessary concomitant of it. By that cause necessitating the connection between them in existence and non-existence, it is called 'necessary' (lizumiyya). And it may be customary. Whether that mentioned connection between them is rational, legal, or customary. And an example of the rational according to them is: 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal'; because the animal according to them is part of the human, which is more general than it. [There is] no separation from it. And reason prevents the whole from its part. So being human is a necessary concomitant of being animal, and being animal is a necessary concomitant of being human. That necessary connection is rational. And an example of the legal is your saying: 'Whenever the sun has passed its zenith, the noon prayer becomes obligatory' and 'Whenever the month of Ramadan occurs, fasting becomes obligatory'; because each of the sun's passing the zenith and the occurrence of Ramadan is a legal cause for the mentioned act of worship, whether prayer or fasting. And an example of the customary is your saying: 'Whenever there is no water, there is no plant'; because Allah has made it customary that the existence of water is a cause for the existence of plants, and its non-existence is a cause for its non-existence. As for the accidental conditional, its defining characteristic is that there is no connection between its antecedent and its consequent, i.e., no relationship at all between them, neither rational, nor legal, nor customary. But it so happens externally that each is true along with the other's truth, i.e., they coincide such that there is no effect of negating one—if assumed—on negating the other, nor on its nonexistence; since there is no relationship at all between them. Like your saying: 'Whenever the sun is rising, the human is speaking.' There is no relationship at all between the rising of the sun and the speech of the human. And among its examples in the Quran is His saying—Exalted is He: 'If you had been in your houses, those who were decreed to be killed would have gone out to their places of lying in death' [Al-Imran 154]; because their being in their houses and their going out to their places of lying in death have no relationship between them, and neither implies the other nor its non-existence. And like His saying—Exalted is He: 'Say, if the sea were ink for the words of my Lord, the sea would be exhausted before the words of my Lord are exhausted' [Al-Kahf 109]; because the sea being ink for them has no relationship to their exhaustion nor to its non-exhaustion. And like His saying—Exalted is He: 'And if you call them to guidance, they will never be guided, then, ever' [Al-Kahf 57]; because his calling them to guidance has no relationship between it and their non-guidance. And of this type is their saying: 'If he did not fear Allah, he would not disobey Him'; because his lack of fear of Allah has no effect on his non-disobedience. Rather, it may be a cause for his disobedience, as appears to the observer. And we have pointed out in our book 'Adwa' al-Bayan' (1) the error of al-Zamakhshari (2).
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage classifies conditional propositions (shartiyya muttasila) into two main types: necessary (lizumiyya) and accidental (ittifaqiyya). The passage begins mid-sentence, explaining that a necessary conditional has a connection between antecedent and consequent in existence and non-existence due to a cause. This cause can be either one being a cause for the other, or one being a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the other. The connection can be rational (aqli), legal (shar'i), or customary (adi). For rational connection, the example given is 'Whenever this is human, it is animal', because ‘animal’ is part of the definition of ‘human’ and reason prevents the whole from being separated from its part. For legal connection, examples are ‘Whenever the sun passes its zenith, the noon prayer becomes obligatory’ and ‘Whenever Ramadan occurs, fasting becomes obligatory’ because these are legal causes (asbab) for the obligations. For customary connection, the example is ‘Whenever there is no water, there is no plant’, because Allah's custom is that water causes plant life.

The passage then defines the accidental conditional (ittifaqiyya) as one where there is no connection at all—neither rational, legal, nor customary—between the antecedent and consequent. They merely happen to both be true externally. An example is ‘Whenever the sun is rising, the human is speaking’. The author then provides several Quranic examples: verse 3:154 where being in houses and going out to die have no relation; verse 18:109 where the sea being ink has no relation to the exhaustion of Allah's words; and verse 18:57 where calling to guidance has no relation to lack of guidance. Finally, a non-Quranic example is given: ‘If he did not fear Allah, he would not disobey Him’—the lack of fear does not cause non-disobedience. The author also notes that he has corrected al-Zamakhshari's error on this point in his own work 'Adwa' al-Bayan'.

Key Terms
شرطية متصلة
لزومية
اتفاقية
عقلي
شرعي
عادي

Key Takeaway
Conditional propositions may have a necessary connection (rational, legal, or customary) or no connection at all (accidental); recognizing this prevents misinterpretation in Islamic reasoning.

Distinguishing Coincidental from Implicative Conditionals
Author critiques Zamakhshari and Abu Hayyan's misinterpretation of a Quranic conditional as implicative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a Quranic conditional is coincidental (ittifaqiyya) or implicative (luzumiyya) by analyzing the real cause of its consequent.

Core Idea
Not every Quranic conditional implies causation; some are coincidental, with the true cause unstated.

Misinterpreting a Quranic conditional as causal can lead to theological error. In this critique, the author exposes a mistake by Zamakhshari and Abu Hayyan in treating Q. 18:57 as implicative (luzumiyya). This lesson will teach you to spot the difference between an implicative and a coincidental conditional, a crucial distinction in Tafsir and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the antecedent and consequent
Locate the part after 'in' (if) as the antecedent (shart) and the part after 'fa' as the consequent (jaza'). In Q. 18:57: 'If you call them to guidance' is the antecedent; 'they will never be guided' is the consequent.
2
Test the antecedent as a plausible cause
Ask: Could the antecedent genuinely cause the consequent? Here, does calling someone to guidance cause them to never be guided? That seems contradictory; a call to guidance should guide, not misguide.
If yes, the conditional may be luzumiyya; if no, it is likely ittifaqiyya.
3
Search for an external hidden cause
Consider what the real cause of the consequent might be. The author explains that the real cause of the disbelievers' lack of guidance is Allah's eternal will, not the Prophet's call.
In a coincidental conditional, the cause of the consequent is external and not expressed in the statement.
4
Classify the conditional
If the antecedent is not the real cause, the conditional is coincidental (ittifaqiyya). If the antecedent is the real cause, it is implicative (luzumiyya). Apply this to the verse: it is coincidental.
This classification affects interpretation: do not read causation where none exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأبي حيان في آية (وَإِن تَدْعُهُمْ إِلَى الْهُدَى فَلَن يَهْتَدُوا إِذا أَبَدًا) فقد ظنا هذه الشرطية في هذه الآية لزومية؛ حيث زعما أنه جعل فيها سبب عدم اهتدائهم هو دعوته لهم إلى الهدى والمعنى أن الكفار عندهما جعلوا ما يكون سببًا للاهتداء سببًا لانتفائه، وكل ذلك غلط؛ لأن الشرطية المذكورة في الآية اتفاقية لا ربط أصلاً بين مقدمها وتاليها. واعلم أن الشرط الحقيقي في الاتفاقية ليس هو المذكور مع الجزاء، بل سبب الجزاء شيء آخر غير مذكور معه في الشرطية المذكورة. كقولهم (لو لم يخف الله لم يعصه) فعدم العصيان الذي هو الجزاء في مثل هذا المثل سببه غير مذكور معه فليس سببه عدم الخوف الذي هو الشرط في المثال المذكور، ولكنه شيء آخر لم يذكر، وهو تعظيمه الله ومحبته له المانعة من معصيته له، ولو لم يكن خائفا. وكذلك قوله - تعالى -: ﴿ فَلَن يَهْتَدُوا إِذَا أَبَدًا) سببه الحقيقي غير مذكور معه، فليس هو قوله: ﴿وَإِن تَدْعُهُمْ إِلَى الْهُدَى) كما ظنه الزمخشري وأبو حيان وغيرهما، بل سببه هو إرادة الله - تعالى - عدم اهتدائهم على وفق ما سبق في علمه أزلا. وكذلك قوله - تعالى -: لَوْ كُنتُمْ فِي بُيُوتِكُمْ لَبَرَزَ الَّذِينَ كُتِبَ عَلَيْهِمُ الْقَتْلُ إِلَى مَضَاجِعِهِمْ؛ لأن سبب بروزهم إلى مضاجعهم شيء آخر غير مذكور في الآية، وهكذا.
And [regarding] Abu Hayyan on the verse 'And if you call them to guidance, they will never be guided'—they both thought this conditional in this verse is implicative, claiming that He made the cause of their not being guided their being called to guidance, meaning that in their view the disbelievers made what is a cause of guidance a cause of its absence. All of that is wrong, because the conditional mentioned in the verse is coincidental; there is no connection at all between its antecedent and its consequent. Know that the real condition in the coincidental is not the one mentioned with the consequent, rather the cause of the consequent is something else not mentioned with it in the conditional. Like their saying 'If he did not fear God, he would not disobey Him'—the non-disobedience which is the consequent in such an example has an unmentioned cause, so its cause is not the absence of fear which is the condition in the mentioned example, but something else not mentioned, namely his glorification of God and love for Him which prevents him from disobeying Him, even if he were not fearful. Likewise His saying, Exalted is He, 'Then they will never be guided'—its real cause is not mentioned with it, so it is not His saying 'And if you call them to guidance' as Zamakhshari, Abu Hayyan, and others thought; rather its cause is the will of God, Exalted is He, that they not be guided in accordance with what preceded in His knowledge from eternity. Likewise His saying, Exalted is He, 'If you had been in your houses, those for whom killing was prescribed would have gone forth to their deathbeds'—because the cause of their going forth to their deathbeds is something else not mentioned in the verse. And so on.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a critique of a specific logical error made by two exegetes, Zamakhshari and Abu Hayyan, in interpreting a Quranic verse. The author argues that they mistakenly treated the conditional in the verse as implicative (luzumiyya), i.e., believing that the antecedent (the call to guidance) is the cause of the consequent (the lack of guidance). In fact, the author asserts, the conditional is coincidental (ittifaqiyya), meaning there is no genuine causal connection between antecedent and consequent. The author explains that in a coincidental conditional, the real cause of the consequent is not stated in the conditional itself; rather it is something external. He illustrates with the example 'If he did not fear God, he would not disobey Him': the consequent 'does not disobey' is not caused by the absence of fear but by glorification and love of God. Similarly, in the Quranic verse, the real cause of the disbelievers' failure to be guided is not the Prophet's call but Allah's eternal will from pre-eternity. A second Quranic example is given about staying in houses and going forth to deathbeds: the cause of their going forth is not their staying home but something else unstated. The author thus defends the view that many Quranic conditionals are coincidental, not implicative, and criticizes those who read a causal link where there is none.

Key Terms
شرطية اتفاقية
شرطية لزومية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Quranic verse 'If you call them to guidance, they will never be guided', the correct logical classification is:
A) Implicative conditional, because the call causes their lack of guidance.
B) Coincidental conditional, because the real cause is Allah's eternal will, not the call.
C) Implicative conditional, because the verse shows cause and effect.
D) Coincidental conditional, because there is no logical relationship at all.

Key Takeaway
Many Quranic conditionals are coincidental (ittifaqiyya); the antecedent is not the real cause of the consequent.

Truth Criterion for Conditional Propositions
Author explains truth criterion for conditionals based on connection correctness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the truth of a conditional proposition by assessing its internal connection (rabṭ), independent of the truth of its components.

Core Idea
A conditional's truth is about the connection, not about the truth of its parts.

In Islamic logic, a conditional proposition like 'If A then B' is evaluated by the soundness of the implication between A and B, not by whether A or B are factually true. This passage establishes that crucial criterion, which is fundamental for logical analysis in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify antecedent and consequent
Find the two parts of the conditional: the antecedent (al-muqaddam) after 'if' and the consequent (al-tālī) after 'then'.
2
Isolate the connection (rabṭ)
The connection is the logical relation expressed by the conditional connective. It is what makes the conditional a single proposition.
Do not focus on the truth of each part individually.
3
Assess correctness of the connection
Determine whether the antecedent genuinely implies the consequent. This depends on the context (e.g., logical necessity, theological doctrine, juristic rule).
The author says truth follows correctness of the connection.
4
Apply the criterion
If the connection is correct, the conditional is true (even if both parts are false). If the connection is incorrect, it is false (even if both parts are true).
The source emphasizes that truth/falsity apply only to the connection.
5
Avoid the common mistake
Do not conflate the truth of a conditional with the truth of its components. The author explicitly warns about this.
Remove the connective tool to see the parts' truth separately.

Warnings: First: Know that the truth of the matter is that truth and falsity in the conditional conjunctive proposition are only according to the correctness of the connection between the antecedent and the consequent and its incorrectness. If the connection is correct, it is true; if the connection is incorrect, it is false. And because truth and falsity only apply to the connection between the antecedent and the consequent, it is valid for it to be true while both of its parts are false if the connective tool is removed.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage provides a crucial criterion for evaluating the truth value of conditional propositions (al-shartiyya al-muttasila). The author states that truth and falsity are determined solely by the correctness of the connection (rabṭ) between the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tālī). If the connection holds, the conditional is true; if not, it is false. This means that the truth of a conditional does not depend on the truth of its components individually. For example, the conditional 'If the world is eternal, then it has no creator' might be considered true in logic if the connection (i.e., a supposed implication) is deemed correct, even if the antecedent and consequent are both false in reality. This principle is critical for logical analysis: a conditional can be true despite false parts. The author emphasizes this point by noting that if you remove the connective tool (like 'if'), the truth of the parts would matter separately, but within the conditional, only the connection counts. This underscores that logic is concerned with form and connection, not factual content.

Truth Conditions of Conjunctive Conditionals
Author explains truth conditions of conditionals using Quranic examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the truth value of a conjunctive conditional by assessing whether the logical connection between its antecedent and consequent is sound, independent of the truth of the parts.

Core Idea
The truth of a conditional lies in the connection, not the parts.

In this passage, the author clarifies a fundamental principle of conditional logic: the truth of a conjunctive conditional (al-shartiyya al-muttasila) is determined solely by the soundness of the implication between antecedent and consequent. Many students mistakenly think a conditional is true only if both parts are true or false in a certain way, but the author uses powerful Quranic examples—like the verse about gods in the heavens—to show that even if both antecedent and consequent are false, the conditional can be utterly true if the connection is valid. This principle has direct applications in Islamic theology (kalām) and legal theory (uṣūl al-fiqh), where conditionals are used to draw necessary conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional's parts
Locate the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tālī) in the conditional. In 'If Zayd were a stone, he would be inanimate,' the antecedent is 'Zayd is a stone' and the consequent is 'Zayd is inanimate.'
Be careful of the 'if...then' structure; sometimes it is implicit.
2
Assess the connection (rabṭ)
Ask: If the antecedent were true, would the consequent necessarily be true? If yes, the connection is sound; if no, it is unsound. In the stone example, if Zayd were a stone, he would indeed be inanimate, so the connection is sound.
Ignore whether the antecedent is actually true; only consider the hypothetical.
3
Ignore the truth of the parts
Note that the actual truth values of antecedent and consequent are irrelevant. In the stone example, both are false (Zayd is not a stone nor inanimate), yet the conditional is true because the connection is sound.
This is a common point of confusion.
4
Classify the conditional
If the connection is necessary (luzūmiyya), the conditional is implicative (e.g., the Quranic example). If the connection is accidental (ittifāqiyya), the conditional removes imagined incompatibility (e.g., the hypocrites' case).
The passage distinguishes these two types.
5
Test with a contrasting example
Consider a conditional with a false connection: 'If the sky is blue, then 2+2=5.' The antecedent could be true, but the consequent false, so the connection is unsound; the conditional is false. This verifies the rule.
Use Islamic examples in the application.

Among the reasons necessitating that a conditional be accidental is the removal of what occurs in the imagination of incompatibility between two propositions, so by the accidental conjunctive proposition it is shown that there is no incompatibility between them. For example, the disbelievers used to imagine that their being in their houses was incompatible with their emergence to their resting places, and they thought that it would save them from killing, as He - exalted - mentioned about them in His saying: 'They say: If we had any part in the matter, we would not have been killed here' [Al 'Imran 154]. If the connection is sound, it is true; if the connection is unsound, it is false. And because truth and falsehood only apply to the connection between the antecedent and consequent, it is possible for it to be true even while its two parts are false if the connecting tool is removed. So His saying - exalted -: 'If there were in them gods besides Allah, they would have been corrupted' [Al-Anbiya 22] is a conjunctive implicative conditional of utmost truth, even though if you removed the connecting tool between its two parts, each of the two parts would be a false proposition. Then the first part after removing the connection becomes: 'There were in them gods besides Allah' - and this is a false proposition; glorified and exalted is He above having another god with Him, great indeed. And the second part in the mentioned example becomes: 'they would have been corrupted' i.e., the heavens and the earth, and it too is a false proposition. And like your saying: 'If Zayd were a stone, he would be inanimate' - this is an implicative conditional true despite the falsity of its two parts, because Zayd was not a stone and was not inanimate. And some scholars said: The locus of truth and falsehood in the conjunctive conditional is only the consequent, which is the result, so it is what admits truth and falsehood according to them. And the condition, which is the antecedent, is a restriction in it. And they claimed that this is the view of the Arabic scholars. And the correct view, as it appears, is that the linguists agree with the logicians that truth and falsehood in the mentioned conditional only apply to the connection between its two parts; because of its truth with the falsity of its two parts as we exemplified, and also because of its truth with the falsity of one of them and the truth of the other if the connection is removed, as in your saying: 'If Zayd were in the sky, he would not escape death' - this is a true conditional, and its consequent, which is 'he would not escape death', is true, and its antecedent, which is his being in the sky, is false. Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the truth conditions for conjunctive conditional propositions (al-shartiyya al-muttasila). The author begins by giving two Quranic examples of 'accidental' conditionals (ittifāqiyya): the verses about the hypocrites imagining that staying home would prevent death. These conditionals serve to remove the imagined incompatibility between two propositions; they are true because the connection is absent, not because the parts are true. Then the author states the core thesis: truth and falsehood in a conditional depend solely on the soundness of the logical connection (rabṭ) between antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tālī). A conditional is true if the connection is sound, false otherwise. To illustrate, he provides a powerful Quranic example: 'If there were in them gods besides Allah, they would have been corrupted' (Q. 21:22). This conditional is considered extremely true, yet both its antecedent ('there were gods besides Allah') and consequent ('they would have been corrupted') are false when considered as independent propositions. Similarly, the artificial example 'If Zayd were a stone, he would be inanimate' is true despite both parts being false. The author then reports an alternative view from 'some scholars' (identified as grammarians) who argue that truth and falsehood reside only in the consequent, with the antecedent merely a condition. The author refutes this by noting that linguists actually agree with the logicians: the truth pertains to the connection, as shown by cases where the consequent is true and the antecedent false (e.g., 'If Zayd were in the sky, he would not escape death' — the connection is sound, but the antecedent is false and the consequent true). Finally, the second notification simply names the first part of the conditional as the 'antecedent' (muqaddam), though the text cuts off here. Throughout, the passage guards against the common misunderstanding that the truth of a conditional is determined by the truth of its components.

Truth in Connected Conditionals is the Connection, Not the Parts
Author explains that truth in conditionals is in the connection, not the parts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the locus of truth in a connected conditional (shartiyya muttasila luzumiyya) and distinguish it from mistaken views that locate truth in the parts.

Core Idea
A conditional's truth lies in the necessary connection between antecedent and consequent, not in the truth of either part.

In logic, it is crucial to understand what makes a conditional statement true. The author uses Quranic and everyday examples to show that a conditional can be true even when both its parts are false. He then critiques a view that assigns truth to the consequent alone, affirming that the connection (rabt) is the true bearer of truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
The passage discusses connected conditionals of necessity (shartiyya muttasila luzumiyya). Recognize that these conditionals assert a necessary relation between antecedent and consequent.
The term 'luzumiyya' indicates the implication is necessary, not accidental.
2
Examine the author's main examples
Look at the Quranic verse (21:22) and the Zayd-as-stone example. Note that each part independently is false, yet the conditional is true. This shows truth cannot reside solely in the parts.
If you remove the conditional connective, both parts become false propositions.
3
Understand the erroneous view
Some grammarians claim truth and falsehood apply only to the consequent (tali), treating the antecedent as a mere restriction. Recognize why this fails: the Quranic example has a false consequent but the conditional remains true.
The author later refutes this view.
4
Grasp the correct doctrine
The correct view is that truth attaches to the connection (rabt) between antecedent and consequent. The third example (Zayd in sky) shows a true conditional with a true consequent but false antecedent, further supporting the connection view.
Thus, the conditional's truth is independent of the truth of its components.

His statement - Exalted is He -: 'If there were in them gods besides Allah, they would have been ruined' [Al-Anbiya 22] is a connected conditional of necessity, extremely true, while if you removed the conjunction between its two sides, each of the two sides would be a false proposition. Thus the first side becomes upon removing the connector: 'There were in them gods besides Allah' — and this is a false proposition; glory be to Him and exalted above there being with Him a god — greatly exalted. And the second side becomes in the mentioned example: 'they would have been ruined' i.e. the heavens and the earth, and it is also a false proposition. And like your saying: 'If Zayd were a stone, he would be an inanimate object' — this is a necessary conditional, true while the two sides are false, because Zayd was not a stone and was not an inanimate object. And some scholars said: The locus of truth and falsehood in the connected conditional is only the consequent which is the apodosis; it is what admits truth and falsehood according to them, and the condition which is the antecedent is a restriction in it. And they claimed this is the position of the Arabic grammarians. And the correct view, as it appears, is that the linguists agree with the logicians that truth and falsehood in the mentioned conditional only apply to the connection between its two sides; because of its truth with falsehood of both sides as we exemplified, and also its truth with falsehood of one and truth of the other if the connection were removed, as your saying: 'If Zayd were in the sky, he would not have escaped death' — this conditional is true, while its consequent which is 'he would not have escaped death' is true, and its antecedent which is his being in the sky is false.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage defines the truth condition for connected conditionals (shartiyya muttasila) of the necessary type (luzumiyya). The author begins with a Quranic example: 'If there were in them gods besides Allah, they would have been ruined' (21:22). He notes that this conditional is extremely true, yet if you remove the conditional connective (law) and consider each part independently, both parts are false (since there are no gods besides Allah, and the heavens and earth were not ruined). This demonstrates that truth cannot reside solely in the parts. He then gives a second example: 'If Zayd were a stone, he would be an inanimate object' — again, both parts false but the conditional is true because the connection (luzum) holds. Next, he reports a view from 'some scholars' (grammarians) who claim that truth and falsehood apply only to the consequent (tali/جزاء), treating the antecedent as a mere restriction. The author rejects this, stating that the correct view is that truth pertains to the connection (al-rabṭ) between antecedent and consequent. He supports this with a third example: 'If Zayd were in the sky, he would not have escaped death' — here the consequent is true (since no one escapes death), the antecedent false, yet the conditional is true because the connection is sound. Thus, the passage establishes the standard logical doctrine that a conditional's truth is a function of the implication, not the truth of its components.

Identifying Muqaddam and Tali in Conditionals
Author explains the terminology 'muqaddam' and 'tali' for connected vs disjunctive conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the parts of a connected conditional and a disjunctive conditional by identifying which part is called muqaddam (antecedent) and which tali (consequent).

Core Idea
In connected conditionals, the order of muqaddam and tali is fixed by logical implication; in disjunctives, the order is free.

Conditional propositions are fundamental to logical reasoning in Islamic scholarship, appearing in arguments from kalam to usul al-fiqh. To analyse these conditionals correctly, we must first name their parts precisely. In this lesson, we examine the terminology for the two parts of a conditional — muqaddam (antecedent) and tali (consequent) — and see how this naming differs between connected and disjunctive conditionals.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two parts in a connected conditional
In a sentence like 'If this is a human, then it is an animal', identify which part is muqaddam and which is tali. The part after 'if' is the antecedent (muqaddam), and the part after 'then' is the consequent (tali). This order is fixed by logical dependency.
2
Compare with a disjunctive conditional
Take 'The number is either even or odd'. Note that flipping the order (odd or even) does not change the meaning. The parts are not dependent on each other.
This illustrates the freedom of order in disjunctives.
3
Identify the disagreement among scholars
The text mentions two opinions: some say disjunctives also have muqaddam and tali, others say they do not because neither part is ordered upon the other. Understand the reasoning for each.
The author presents this as a difference of view, not settled.
4
Apply the test of reversibility
For a connected conditional, reversing renders it false (except special cases). For a disjunctive, reversing is fine. This test shows the difference in logical structure.
The author gives the example: 'If this were a human, it would be an animal' is true, but the reverse is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التنبية الثاني : اعلم أن الطرف الأول من طرفي الشرطية يسمى مقدما، والثاني يسمى تاليا . وهذا في المتصلة لا خلاف فيه . وقيل في المنفصلة أيضًا كذلك وقيل لا يسمى جزاها مقدما وتاليا؛ لأنها لم يكن أحد طرفيها مرتباً على الآخر، فالتقديم والتأخير فيها موكول إلى اختيار المتكلم . فلو قلت : العدد إما زوج وإما فرد فلا فرق بين قولك : العدد إما فرد وإما زوج، فلك أن تقدم من الطرفين ما شئت وتؤخره . بخلاف المتصلة؛ فإنه لو أُخر المقدم لم تصدق لزوما، ولو صدقت في بعض الصور الخصوص المادة فقولك : لو كان هذا إنسانًا لكان حيوانا صحيح، ولو عكست فقلت: لو كان هذا حيوانًا لكان إنسانًا كان كاذبًا. فتبين أن كونه إنسانًا مقدم وكونه حيوانًا تال على الحقيقة بخلاف المنفصلة كما مثلنا .
The second warning: Know that the first of the two sides of a conditional is called antecedent (muqaddam), and the second is called consequent (tali). This is for the connected conditional, and there is no disagreement about it. And it was said for the disjunctive conditional also likewise; and it was said that its two parts are not called antecedent and consequent, because one of its sides is not dependent on the other, so ordering and delaying in it is entrusted to the choice of the speaker. If you say: 'The number is either even or odd', there is no difference between your saying: 'The number is either odd or even', so you may advance whichever of the two sides you wish and delay it. Unlike the connected conditional; for if the antecedent is delayed, it would not be true as a necessity, even if it might be true in certain specific material cases. So your saying: 'If this were a human, it would be an animal' is correct; and if you reversed and said: 'If this were an animal, it would be a human' it would be false. Thus it is shown that its being a human is antecedent and its being an animal is consequent in reality, unlike the disjunctive conditional as we have exemplified.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage defines the technical terminology for the parts of conditional propositions. The author states that for connected conditionals (shartiyya muttasila), the first part is called muqaddam (antecedent) and the second called tali (consequent), unanimously. For disjunctive conditionals (shartiyya munfasila), there is disagreement: some apply the same terms, but others say that since the two parts are not ordered by implication (one not dependent on the other), the speaker is free to present either part first. The author illustrates this with the example 'The number is either even or odd' — flipping the order changes nothing. In contrast, a connected conditional like 'If this is a human, then it is an animal' cannot be reversed; reversing would make it false, because the implication only holds in that direction (humans are necessarily animals, but not vice versa). However, the author notes an exception: in some specific material cases where the relationship is equivalence (like between human and rational), reversal might hold, but that is not the norm. The key point is that in connected conditionals, the logical order is fixed, while in disjunctive, it is not.

Key Terms
مقدم (muqaddam): The first part of a conditional proposition; in connected conditionals it is the antecedent (the 'if' clause). [Note: Stable across texts.]
تالي (tali): The second part of a conditional proposition; in connected conditionals it is the consequent (the 'then' clause). [Note: Stable.]
منفصلة (munfasila): A disjunctive conditional, where the two parts are alternatives (either/or) and not necessarily ordered. [Note: Some later texts divide munfasila into subtypes like mani'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following correctly describes the naming of parts in a disjunctive conditional according to some scholars?
A) The first part is always muqaddam and the second always tali.
B) The parts are not called muqaddam and tali because neither depends on the other.
C) The parts are named based on their truth value.
D) The parts are named based on the speaker's intention.

Key Takeaway
The naming of the parts of a conditional depends on whether the conditional is connected (fixed order) or disjunctive (free order).

The Condition of Mutual Exclusivity in Disjunctive Conditionals
Author states the condition for disjunctive conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the essential condition (ʿinād) for a disjunctive conditional and distinguish it from conjunctive or inclusive disjunction.

Core Idea
Disjunctive conditionals require mutual exclusivity between their two parts.

In this passage, the author lays down the fundamental rule for disjunctive conditionals (shartiyya munfaṣila): the two sides must be mutually incompatible. This condition, called ʿinād or tanāfur, ensures that the disjunction is exclusive – the two parts cannot both be true. Understanding this condition is crucial for correctly evaluating arguments that rely on disjunctive syllogisms, a common form in Islamic legal reasoning and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Locate the term 'al-shartiyya al-munfaṣila' in the Arabic. Recognize that this is a disjunctive conditional, as opposed to a conjunctive or hypothetical conditional.
Disjunctive conditionals use 'either...or' (إما... أو).
2
Extract the essential condition
The author states that there must be 'ʿinād' between the two sides. Understand that ʿinād means mutual opposition or incompatibility.
The author equates ʿinād with tanāfur – both mean the impossibility of the two parts being true together.
3
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the Islamic legal maxim: 'Either an act is obligatory (wājib) or prohibited (ḥarām).' These two cannot be true simultaneously for the same act, so they form a valid disjunctive conditional with ʿinād.
If the two parts could both be true (e.g., 'either it is sunnah or mubāḥ'), the disjunction would be inclusive, not exclusive, and would not satisfy the condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشرطية المنفصلة فضابطها أنها لابد أن يكون بين طرفيها عناد في الجملة . واعلم أن المراد بالعناد هنا والتنافرِ شيء واحد، وهو تنافي الطرفين، واستحالة اجتماعهما والعناد المذكور بين الطرفين هو
As for the disjunctive conditional, its rule is that there must be between its two sides some incompatibility (inad). And know that what is meant by inad here and tanafur is one thing: the mutual incompatibility of the two sides and the impossibility of their conjunction. And the mentioned inad between the two sides is...
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage defines the essential condition for a disjunctive conditional (shartiyya munfasila): the two parts must be mutually exclusive. The author uses the terms 'inad and tanafur synonymously to indicate that the two sides cannot be true together. This is a fundamental logical requirement for exclusive disjunction, as opposed to conjunction or inclusive disjunction.

Key Terms
الشرطية المنفصلة
العناد
التنافر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A disjunctive conditional is valid only if its two parts are mutually exclusive (ʿinād), meaning they cannot be true together.

Classification of Disjunctives: The Three Types of Opposition
Author classifies disjunctives into three types and defines the true disjunctive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of disjunctive conditionals based on opposition in existence and non-existence, and identify examples of the true disjunctive (man'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw) in Islamic logical arguments.

Core Idea
The true disjunctive is the only one where exactly one side is true.

In this passage, the author lays out the rational classification of disjunctive conditionals. Understanding these three types is crucial for correctly analyzing disjunctive arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of opposition
The author divides opposition (العناد) between disjunct sides into three: opposition in both existence and non-existence, in existence only, and in non-existence only. Mark these clearly.
2
Define the first type: man'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw
When opposition is in both existence and non-existence, the sides cannot be true together nor false together. This is the true disjunctive conditional. Note the two conditions: no conjunction (jam') and no emptiness (khuluw).
3
Recognize the logical requirement for sides
The two sides must be either actual contradictories (النقيضان) or a thing and the equivalent of its contradictory (مساوي نقيضه). This ensures the disjunction is exclusive and exhaustive.
4
Analyze the examples provided
The author gives: 'the number is either even or not even' (contradictories) and 'the number is either even or odd' (equivalent to contradictory). Verify that in both cases exactly one holds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتقسيم العقلي الصحيح يحصر العناد المذكور في ثلاثة أقسام لا رابع لها: الأول: أن يكون في الوجود والعدم معاً. الثاني: أن يكون في الوجود فقط. الثالث: أن يكون في العدم فقط. فإن كان العناد بين طرفيها في الوجود والعدم معا، بمعنى أن طرفيها لا يمكن اجتماعهما في الوجود ولا في العدم، فلا يوجدان معا ولا يُعدمان معًا، بل لابد من وجود أحدهما وعدم الآخر، فهي المعروفة بالشرطية المنفصلة الحقيقية، وتسمى مانعة الجمع والخُلُوِّ معا، ولا تتركب إلا من النقيضين أو من الشيء ومساوي نقيضه، ومثالها في النقيضين قولك: العدد إما زوج أو ليس بزوج، ومثالها في الشيء ومساوي نقيضه: العدد إما زوج وإما فرد؛ لأن لفظة (فرد) مساوية لـ ليس بزوج، وهي نقيض (العدد زوج).
And the correct rational division confines the mentioned opposition to three divisions, no fourth: first: that it is in existence and non-existence together. second: that it is in existence only. third: that it is in non-existence only. For if the opposition between its two sides is in existence and non-existence together, meaning that its two sides cannot coexist in existence nor in non-existence, so they are not both existent nor both non-existent, but rather one must exist and the other be non-existent, then it is the known true disjunctive conditional, and it is called the preventer of conjunction and emptiness together, and it is composed only of contradictories or of a thing and the equivalent of its contradictory. Its example in contradictories is your saying: the number is either even or not even, and its example in the thing and the equivalent of its contradictory: the number is either even or odd; because the term 'odd' is equivalent to 'not even', and it is the contradictory of 'the number is even'.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage defines the three-way classification of disjunctive conditionals based on the type of opposition (العناد) between the two sides. The author begins by stating that the correct rational division limits opposition in disjunctives to three types, with no fourth: 1) opposition in both existence and non-existence, 2) opposition in existence only, 3) opposition in non-existence only. Then he elaborates the first type: if the opposition is in both existence and non-existence, then the two sides cannot coexist nor both be absent; one must exist and the other must not. This is called the true disjunctive conditional (الشرطية المنفصلة الحقيقية) and also 'man'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw' (preventer of conjunction and emptiness). It is composed only of actual contradictories (النقيضين) or of a thing and the equivalent of its contradictory (مساوي نقيضه). The author gives two examples: for contradictories, 'the number is either even or not even'; for a thing and the equivalent of its contradictory, 'the number is either even or odd', explaining that 'odd' is equivalent to 'not even', which is the contradictory of 'even'. This shows that the two sides are mutually exclusive and exhaustive.

Key Terms
العناد
مانعة الجمع والخُلُوّ
النقيضان

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The number is either even or odd.

Key Takeaway
The true disjunctive (man'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw) is defined by mutual exclusivity and exhaustiveness, requiring its sides to be contradictories or equivalent.
