Structural Condition of Preventing Conjunction Only
Author defines the compositional rule for man'at al-jam' faqat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structural condition (akhass min al-naqid) that defines a disjunctive preventing conjunction only.

Core Idea
It is composed of a proposition and a subcontrary, not a contradictory.

In the previous study of disjunctives, we encountered the 'true disjunctive' that prevents both conjunction and emptiness. Now, we examine a more nuanced type: the disjunctive that prevents conjunction only. The author provides a precise structural condition for this type, relating to the relationship between its two sides. Learning this condition is essential for correctly constructing and evaluating arguments that involve non-exhaustive alternatives.

Guided Path
1
Locate the core structural rule
Find the sentence in the Arabic where the author specifies the compositional requirement: 'ولا تتركب إلا من قضية وأخص من نقيضها'. This is the defining feature of this disjunctive type.
2
Understand 'Akhass min al-Naqid'
Contrast this with a true disjunctive, which uses the absolute contradictory (al-naqid). 'Akhass min al-naqid' means a subcontrary or a more specific term. For example, 'black' is more specific than the contradictory of 'white' (which is 'not-white').
3
Apply the rule to the example given
The author's example is 'The body is either white or black'. Check if 'black' is more specific than the contradictory of 'white'. Yes, it is. This fulfills the structural condition.
4
Construct an original Islamic example
Think of a pair from Fiqh, such as 'wajib' and 'mandub'. Is 'mandub' more specific than 'not-wajib'? Yes. Can an act be both simultaneously? No. Can it be neither? Yes, if it is mubah, makruh, or haram. This fits the structure perfectly.
This contrasts with a true disjunctive like 'halal' vs. 'haram', where an act must be one or the other.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان العناد بين طرفيها في الوجود فقط فهي مانعة الجمع المجوزة للخلو، ولا تتركب إلا من قضية وأخص من نقيضها، كقولك: (الجسم إما أبيض وإما أسود)، فهذه شرطية منفصلة مانعة جمع؛ لأنه يستحيل اجتماع طرفيها في الوجود، بأن يكون الجسم الواحد أبيض أسود في وقت واحد من جهة واحدة، ولكنها تُجوز الخُلُو من الطرفين؛ لأنها لا عناد بين طرفيها في العدم، فيجوز أن يكون الجسم غير أبيض وغير أسود؛ لكونه أحمر أو أصفر مثلاً. وجواز عدم طرفيها معا هو معنى كونها لا عناد بين طرفيها في العدم، بل هما مصطحبان فيه، لانعدام كليهما.
And if the opposition between its two sides is in existence only, then it is a preventer of conjunction permitting emptiness, and it is composed only of a proposition and something more specific than its contradictory, like your saying: (The body is either white or black). This is a disjunctive conditional preventing conjunction because it is impossible for its two sides to coexist in existence, that a single body be white and black at one time from one aspect, but it permits emptiness from both sides because there is no opposition between its two sides in non-existence; so it is permissible that the body be neither white nor black, because it is, for example, red or yellow. And the permissibility of both sides being non-existent is the meaning of there being no opposition between its two sides in non-existence; rather they are together in it, due to the non-existence of both.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage explains the second type of disjunctive conditional, where the opposition is only in existence. The author terms it 'man'at al-jam' al-mujawwiza li al-khuluw' (preventer of conjunction permitting emptiness). The condition for such a disjunctive is that one side is a proposition and the other is more specific than its contradictory (أخص من نقيضها). The example given is 'the body is either white or black'. The two sides cannot be simultaneously true (a body cannot be both white and black at the same time and in the same respect), but they can both be false (a body may be red, yellow, etc.). This 'permitting emptiness' means both sides can be absent together; there is no opposition in non-existence. The passage clarifies that this permissibility is what it means for there to be no opposition in non-existence—both sides are concurrent in non-existence.

Key Terms
أخص من النقيض
مانعة الجمع المجوزة للخلو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It must be composed of a proposition and something more specific than its contradictory.
C) It must be composed of two universals.
D) It must be composed of a universal and a particular.

Key Takeaway
The structural key to this disjunctive is that one side is a subcontrary, not a contradictory, allowing for both to be false.

The Logical Space of Non-Existence in Disjunctives
Author explains the permissibility of non-existence (khuluw) in this disjunctive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the concept of 'khuluw' (emptiness) in a disjunctive and give an Islamic science example where both sides are false.

Core Idea
The absence of opposition in non-existence creates space for multiple possibilities beyond the two stated.

The previous lesson focused on the structure of the 'man'at al-jam' faqat' disjunctive. Now we explore its most important logical consequence: the permissibility of both sides being false simultaneously (khuluw). The author clarifies that the two sides have no opposition in non-existence, meaning they can be absent together. This 'empty' space is where intermediate possibilities reside.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key phrase about non-existence
Find the sentence: 'لا عناد بين طرفيها في العدم'. This phrase establishes that the opposition between the two sides is limited to existence only.
2
Understand 'concurrent in non-existence'
The author says 'هما مصطحبان فيه لانعدام كليهما'. This means the two sides coincide in non-existence. When one is false, the other is not necessarily true; both can be false together.
3
Trace the implication in the author's example
Apply this to 'white vs. black'. If a body is not white and not black, where is it? It is in an intermediate state (red, yellow). The disjunctive explicitly permits this.
4
Compare with the true disjunctive
In a true disjunctive (man'at al-jam' wal-khuluw), the two sides are exhaustive and exclusive. If one is false, the other must be true. Here, both can be false. This is the key difference.
Example: 'The number is either even or odd.' Here, khuluw is impossible.
5
Apply to a Kalam theological distinction
Consider the distinction between 'eternal (qadim)' and 'originated (muhdath)'. This is a true disjunctive. But what about 'black' vs. 'white' in the context of divine attributes? This is preventing conjunction only.
Misidentifying the type leads to theological paradoxes.

Source Text

And the permissibility of both sides being non-existent is the meaning of there being no opposition between its two sides in non-existence; rather they are together in it, due to the non-existence of both.
Source pages p. 77

This passage explains the second type of disjunctive conditional, where the opposition is only in existence. The author terms it 'man'at al-jam' al-mujawwiza li al-khuluw' (preventer of conjunction permitting emptiness). Disjunctive Preventing Emptiness Only (Inclusive Or)
Author defines the third type: disjunctive where opposition is in non-existence only.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a disjunctive that prevents emptiness (both sides cannot be false) but permits conjunction (both can be true), and distinguish it from other disjunctives.

Core Idea
In the inclusive disjunctive, both sides can be true but never both false.

The author now introduces the third type of disjunctive conditional, where opposition is only in non-existence. This type, called 'man'at al-khuluw al-mujawwiza li al-jam', is the logical equivalent of the inclusive 'or'. Understanding its truth conditions is essential for correctly evaluating propositions in Islamic scholarly debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the truth condition pattern
Notice the key property: the two sides cannot both be false (emptiness prevented) but can both be true (conjunction permitted). This is opposite to the previous type.
2
Examine the composition
The disjunctive is composed of a proposition and something more general than its contradictory (أعم من النقيض). For example, 'not white' is more general than 'black' because 'not white' includes black, red, yellow, etc.
3
Test the example
Consider the example: 'The body is either not white or not black.' Check if both can be true: a red body is both not white and not black. Check if both can be false: if it is false that the body is not white, then it is white, which implies it is not black, so 'not black' is true. Hence both cannot be false.
Use the principle of double negation: نفي النفي إثبات (negation of negation is affirmation).
4
Distinguish from other types
Compare with the true disjunctive (cannot both be true and cannot both be false) and the preventer of conjunction (cannot both be true but can both be false). The inclusive disjunctive is the only one where both can be true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان العناد بين طرفيها في العدم فقط فهي مانعة الخلو المجوزة للجمع عكس التي قبلها، ولا تتركب إلا من قضية وأعم من نقيضها، كقولك: (الجسم إما غير أبيض وإما غير أسود)، فهذه شرطية منفصلة مانعة خلو مجوّزة جمع، فلا يمكن اجتماع طرفيها في العدم البتة، ولكن يمكن اجتماعهما في الوجود؛ إذ لا عناد بينهما في الوجود، وإنما العناد بينهما في العدم. فطرفا هذه المنفصلة اللذان هما (غير أبيض) و(غير أسود) يجوز اجتماعهما في الوجود فنقول: (هذا الجسم غير أبيض وغير أسود)، وأنت صادق؛ لكونه أحمرًا أو أصفرًا مثلاً. ولكن لا يمكن اجتماعهما في العدم بحال، بل إذا عُدم أحدهما لزم وجود الآخر ضرورة؛ لأنك لو حكمت بنفي (غير الأبيض) فقد جزمت بأنه أبيض؛ لأن نفي النفي إثبات، وإذا جزمت بأنه أبيضُ لزم ضرورة حصول الطرف الثاني الذي هو غير أسود؛ لأن الأبيض غير أسود.
And if the opposition between its two sides is in non-existence only, then it is a preventer of emptiness permitting conjunction, the opposite of the previous one, and it is composed only of a proposition and something more general than its contradictory, like your saying: (The body is either not white or not black). This is a disjunctive conditional preventing emptiness permitting conjunction, so it is absolutely impossible for its two sides to coexist in non-existence, but it is possible for them to coexist in existence, because there is no opposition between them in existence, but rather the opposition between them is in non-existence. So the two sides of this disjunctive, which are (not white) and (not black), it is permissible for them to coexist in existence, so we say: (This body is not white and not black), and you are truthful, because it is, for example, red or yellow. But they cannot coexist in non-existence at all; rather if one of them is non-existent, the existence of the other necessarily follows, because if you judge the negation of (not white), you have asserted that it is white, because the negation of a negation is affirmation, and if you assert that it is white, then the existence of the second side, which is (not black), necessarily follows, because white is not black.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage describes the third type of disjunctive conditional, where opposition is only in non-existence. The author calls it 'man'at al-khuluw al-mujawwiza li al-jam' (preventer of emptiness permitting conjunction), which is the opposite of the previous type. It is composed of a proposition and something more general than its contradictory (أعم من نقيضها). The example is 'the body is either not white or not black'. The two sides are 'not white' and 'not black'. They cannot both be false together (emptiness prevented) because if 'not white' is false, then the body is white, which implies it is not black (i.e., 'not black' is true). This is proven by the principle that the negation of a negation is affirmation (نفي النفي إثبات). However, both sides can be true simultaneously: a body can be both not white and not black (e.g., red). Thus the disjunctive permits conjunction (both true) but prevents emptiness (both false). The passage provides a logical demonstration: if one denies 'not white', that affirms 'white', and 'white' necessarily entails 'not black' (since white and black are exclusive in some sense). Therefore, the two sides cannot both be absent.

Man'at al-Khuluw: The Exhaustive Non-Exclusive Disjunction
Author defines mani'at al-khuluw (exclusive disjunction permissive of combination) with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a mani'at al-khuluw disjunction, distinguish it from mani'at al-jam', and validate its truth conditions in Islamic logical examples.

Core Idea
Exhaustive but not exclusive: at least one must be true, but both can be true.

We have seen disjunctions that prohibit both sides from being true (mani'at al-jam'). Now we meet its opposite: the disjunction that allows both to be true but prevents both from being false. This type is crucial in dialectical reasoning where exhaustive options do not entail mutual exclusivity. The author illustrates it with the example 'The body is either not-white or not-black', showing that both negations can simultaneously hold (for a red body) while they cannot both be false.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sides of the disjunction
Find the two alternatives in the Arabic text – here, 'non-white' and 'non-black' are the two sides. Note that both are negations of properties.
2
Determine where the opposition lies
The author says opposition is only in non-existence: the two sides cannot both be absent. Check if it is possible for both to be false. If you negate 'non-white', you affirm 'white', which implies 'non-black' (since white is not black). So both cannot be false simultaneously.
3
Check if both sides can be true
The passage gives the example: a red body is both non-white and non-black. Therefore both sides are true. This confirms the disjunction is 'permissive of combination' (mujawwizat li al-jam').
4
Apply the principle of double negation
The reasoning uses the logical rule that the negation of a negation is an affirmation. This allows us to deduce that if one side is false, the other must be true. This is the 'exclusive of emptiness' (man'at al-khuluw) aspect.
5
Contrast with the previous type
Recall that the previous type (mani'at al-jam') prohibits both being true but allows both being false. This new type is the reverse: allows both true but prohibits both false. Understanding this contrast is key to classifying disjunctions correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان العناد بين طرفيها في العدم فقط فهي مانعة الخلو المجوزة للجمع عكس التي قبلها، ولا تتركب إلا من قضية وأعم من نقيضها ، كقولك : (الجسم إما غير أبيض وإما غير أسود)، فهذه شرطية منفصلة مانعة خلو مجوّزة جمع، فلا يمكن اجتماع طرفيها في العدم البتة، ولكن يمكن اجتماعهما في الوجود؛ إذ لا عناد بينهما في الوجود، وإنما العناد بينهما في العدم. فطرفا هذه المنفصلة اللذان هما (غير أبيض) و(غير أسود) يجوز اجتماعهما في الوجود فنقول : (هذا الجسم غير أبيض وغير أسود)، وأنت صادق ؛ لكونه أحمرًا أو أصفرًا مثلاً . ولكن لا يمكن اجتماعهما في العدم بحال، بل إذا عُدم أحدهما لزم وجود الآخر ضرورة؛ لأنك لو حكمت بنفي (غير الأبيض) فقد جزمت بأنه أبيض؛ لأن نفي النفي إثبات، وإذا جزمت بأنه أبيضُ لزم ضرورة حصول الطرف الثاني الذي هو غير أسود)؛ لأن الأبيض غير أسود قطعا كما هو ضرورة.
If the opposition between its two sides is only in non-existence, then it is the exclusive disjunction that is permissive of combination, the opposite of the previous one. It is composed only of a proposition and something more general than its negation, like your saying: 'The body is either not-white or not-black'. This is a disjunctive conditional, exclusive of emptiness, permissive of combination. So it is not possible for its two sides to come together in non-existence at all, but it is possible for them to come together in existence, because there is no opposition between them in existence; the opposition is only between them in non-existence. The two sides of this disjunction, which are 'not-white' and 'not-black', may come together in existence, so we say: 'This body is not-white and not-black', and you are truthful, because it is red or yellow for example. But they cannot come together in non-existence in any case. Rather, if one of them is negated, the existence of the other necessarily follows, because if you judge the negation of 'not-white', you have affirmed that it is white, because the negation of a negation is an affirmation. And when you affirm that it is white, the second side, which is 'not-black', necessarily follows, because white is certainly not-black, as is necessary.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage defines the third type of disjunction: mani'at al-khuluw al-mujawwizat li al-jam'. The term 'man'at al-khuluw' means that the two sides cannot both be absent—at least one must be true. But it is 'mujawwizat li al-jam'' meaning it permits both to be present, i.e., both can be true simultaneously. This is different from the previous type (mani'at al-jam') which prohibits both being true but permits both being false. The author gives an example: 'The body is either not-white or not-black'. These two can both be true (a red body is both not-white and not-black), but they cannot both be false. If it is false that it is not-white, then it is white, and whiteness entails not-black. Hence the disjunction is exhaustive but not exclusive. The logical reasoning is based on the principle that the negation of a negation is an affirmation. This type of disjunction is useful in dialectic where we want to exhaust possibilities without incompatibility.

Six Types of Categorical Propositions
Author enumerates the six divisions of categorical propositions by quality and quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify any categorical proposition into one of the six types based on quantity and quality.

Core Idea
Categorical propositions are of six types: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative, singular affirmative, singular negative.

In this passage, the author from the classical logical tradition lays out the foundational classification of categorical propositions. These six types serve as the basic building blocks for constructing syllogisms. Understanding them is the first step to analyzing arguments in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject's quantity
Determine whether the subject term is universal (general) or particular (singular). If the subject is a general term like 'human', it is universal. If it is a specific individual like 'Zayd', it is singular/particular.
Unquantified propositions (no quantifier) are treated as particular.
2
Check the quantifier (if universal)
If the subject is universal, see if it is preceded by a universal quantifier (every, all, no) or a particular quantifier (some). If there is no quantifier, it is unquantified but considered particular.
Universal quantifiers: every, all, none. Particular quantifier: some.
3
Determine quality: affirmative or negative
Check whether the proposition asserts (affirmative) or denies (negative) the predicate of the subject. Words like 'is', 'are' indicate affirmative; 'is not', 'no' indicate negative.
In Arabic, 'ليس' indicates negative.
4
Combine quantity and quality
Combine the quantity (universal/particular/singular) with the quality (affirmative/negative) to get one of the six types. For example, universal + affirmative = universal affirmative.
Singular propositions are also considered particular but are named 'personal' (shakhsiyya).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمرين : إما أن يكون جزئيا، وإما أن يكون كليا . وإن كان موضوعها كليًا فله خمس حالات : الأولى : أن يسور بسور كلي إيجابي . الثانية : أن يسور بسور كلي سلبي . الثالثة : أن يسور بسور جزئي إيجابي . الرابعة : أن يسور بسور جزئي سلبي . الخامسة : أن يهمل من السور، وهذه الخامسة بمنزلة الجزئية . فأقسام ما موضوعُها كلّي أربعة في الحقيقة؛ لأن المهملة في قوة الجزئية . وإن كان موضوعها جزئيًا فهي التي تسمى شخصية ومخصوصة، ولها حالتان؛ لأنها تكون موجبة نحو زيد) قائم) و(هند جميلة)، وسالبة نحو (زيد ليس بقائم) و (هند ليست بجميلة) . فتحصل أن الحملية باعتبار الكيف والكم ستة أقسام : ١ - الأول : كلية موجبة نحو كل إنسان حيوان) و(جميع الإنسان حيوان) ، و ( عامة الإنسان حيوان، ونحو ذلك . ٢ - الثاني : كلية سالبة، نحو (لا شيء من الإنسان بحجر)، و(لا واحد من الإنسان بفرس)، ونحو ذلك ٣ - الثالث : جزئية موجبة ، نحو (بعض الحيوان إنسان). ٤ - الرابع : جزئية سالبة ، نحو (بعض الحيوان ليس بإنسان. ٥ - الخامس : شخصية موجبة، نحو (زيد قائم) . ٦ - السادس : شخصية سالبة : نحو (زيد ليس بقائم) .
Two matters: either it is particular, or it is universal. And if its subject is universal, it has five states: First: it is quantified with a universal affirmative quantifier. Second: it is quantified with a universal negative quantifier. Third: it is quantified with a particular affirmative quantifier. Fourth: it is quantified with a particular negative quantifier. Fifth: it is left unquantified, and this fifth is in the position of the particular. So the divisions of that whose subject is universal are four in reality; because the unquantified is in the strength of the particular. And if its subject is particular, it is called singular and specific, and it has two states: because it is affirmative, like 'Zayd is standing' and 'Hind is beautiful', and negative, like 'Zayd is not standing' and 'Hind is not beautiful'. So it results that categorical propositions, in terms of quality and quantity, are six divisions: 1. First: universal affirmative, like 'Every human is an animal', and 'All humans are animals', and 'Generally humans are animals', and so on. 2. Second: universal negative, like 'No human is a stone', and 'Not one human is a horse', and so on. 3. Third: particular affirmative, like 'Some animal is human'. 4. Fourth: particular negative, like 'Some animal is not human'. 5. Fifth: singular affirmative, like 'Zayd is standing'. 6. Sixth: singular negative, like 'Zayd is not standing'.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage defines the six types of categorical propositions (al-ḥamliyya) based on quantity (kullī/juz'ī/shakhṣī) and quality (ījābī/salbī). The author first explains the possibilities for the subject: if the subject is universal, it may be quantified universally affirmative, universally negative, particularly affirmative, particularly negative, or unquantified (muhmala). The unquantified is treated as particular. Thus universal-subject propositions are effectively four types. If the subject is particular (singular), it is called shakhṣiyya (personal) and has two forms: affirmative and negative. Combining these gives six distinct types. The author provides examples from everyday Arabic, such as 'Every human is an animal' (universal affirmative) and 'Zayd is standing' (singular affirmative). These examples are clear and illustrate the logical forms. The student should memorize this sixfold division.

Every categorical proposition can be classified into one of six types using quantity (universal/particular/singular) and quality (affirmative/negative).

Two Important Notes on Categorical Propositions
Author explains why logicians count eight types but only six are real, and defines subject/predicate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why logicians count eight proposition types but only six are real, and define the subject of a categorical proposition as referring to individuals, not the common concept.

Core Idea
Unquantified propositions are just particular; the subject means the actual individuals, not the abstract essence.

In this lesson, we examine two crucial clarifications about categorical propositions. The first corrects a common misconception about the number of proposition types: why unquantified propositions do not count as a separate type. The second clarifies what logicians mean by 'subject' — a point that is often misunderstood but critical for interpreting categorical statements correctly.

Guided Path
1
Understand the logicians' eight-type division
Logicians sometimes count eight types of categorical propositions by quantity and quality: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative, plus unquantified affirmative and unquantified negative (e.g., 'A human is an animal'). Recognize that this addition is what creates eight.
The author states this is the custom of logicians.
2
See why unquantified reduces to particular
The author argues that unquantified propositions are effectively particular. For example, 'A human is an animal' does not mean all humans, but some human. Therefore, they do not form a separate division; the true count is six.
The author says the unquantified is 'in the strength of the particular' (fī quwwat al-juzʾiyya).
3
Define the subject as individuals
The second notice defines the subject of a categorical proposition: it means all the external individuals that fall under the title (ʿunwān), not the common concept (al-qadr al-mushtarak). This applies whether we consider actual existence (khārijiyya) or estimated existence (ḥaqīqiyya).
This distinction is crucial for evaluating propositions like 'Every human is an animal' — the subject refers to individual humans, not the concept humanity.
4
Apply the distinction to external vs real propositions
Recognize that 'external' propositions consider actually existing individuals, while 'real/essential' propositions consider individuals even if none exist (e.g., if all humans perished, 'human' still refers to mentally posited individuals). The subject definition holds for both.
This shows the logical analysis is not dependent on actual existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيهات : الأول : اعلم أنه جرى عرف المنطقيين في التقسيم المذكور أن القضايا باعتبار الكم والكيف ثمانية ؛ لأنهم يزيدون على الستة التي ذكرنا المهملة الموجبة ، نحو (الإنسان حيوان)، والمهملة السالبة نحو (ليس الحيوان بإنسان، وإنما جعلناها ستة لا ثمانية لأن المهملة في قوة الجزئية، لا قسم زائد ،عليها فصارت القضايا باعتبار الكم والكيف ستا على التحقيق كما ذكرنا . التنبيه الثاني : وهو مهم جدا، وهو أن تعلم أن المراد بالموضوع مناف للمراد [ بالمحمول ] في القضية الحملية؛ لأن المراد بالموضوع جميع أفراده الخارجية الداخلة تحت العنوان، سواء اعتبرنا الوجود الخارجي؛ لكونها خارجية، أو اعتبرنا تقدير الوجود؛ لكونها [حقيقية ] ، ولا يراد بالموضوع القدر المشترك، الذي هو
Notices: First: Know that the custom of logicians in the mentioned division is that propositions in terms of quantity and quality are eight, because they add to the six we mentioned the unquantified affirmative, like 'A human is an animal', and the unquantified negative, like 'An animal is not a human'. But we made them six, not eight, because the unquantified is in the strength of the particular, not an additional division over it. So propositions in terms of quantity and quality are six upon investigation as we mentioned. The second notice, and it is very important: that you know that the intended meaning of subject is opposed to the intended meaning of predicate in the categorical proposition; because by subject is meant all its external individuals falling under the title, whether we consider external existence because they are external, or we consider estimation of existence because they are real/essential, and by subject is not meant the common concept, which is...
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage contains two important clarifications (tanbīhāt) about categorical propositions. The first warning explains why some logicians count eight types instead of six: they include unquantified (muhmala) propositions as separate. The author insists that unquantified propositions are effectively particular, so the correct number is six. For example, 'A human is an animal' (unquantified) means 'Some human is an animal', not all. The second notice is crucial: the subject of a categorical proposition refers to all the external individuals that fall under its term, whether we consider them as actually existing (external propositions) or as mentally posited (real/essential propositions). It does not refer to the common concept (al-qadr al-mushtarak) that is the shared meaning. In other words, the subject denotes the set of individuals, not the abstract essence. This distinction affects how we interpret propositions like 'Every human is an animal' — the subject 'human' means all individual humans, not the concept 'humanity'. The predicate is then affirmed or denied of those individuals.

Key Takeaway
Categorical propositions are six types, not eight, and the subject means the actual individuals, not the common concept.

The Six Types of Categorical Propositions and Why Indefinite Counts as Particular
Author enumerates the six types and argues against adding indefinite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the six types of categorical propositions in Arabic logical texts and explain why indefinite propositions are not a separate type.

Core Idea
Categorical propositions are six, not eight: indefinite propositions collapse into particulars.

In this passage, the author completes the enumeration of categorical propositions by quantity and quality. He lists the last three types and then addresses a common practice among logicians of counting eight types by including indefinite propositions. The author argues that indefinite propositions are logically equivalent to particular propositions, so they do not warrant a separate category. This distinction is crucial for analyzing arguments in Islamic sciences like fiqh and kalam, where precise quantification matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types listed
The passage explicitly gives particular negative, singular affirmative, and singular negative. Recognize their Arabic names: قضية جزئية سالبة, قضية شخصية موجبة, قضية شخصية سالبة.
2
Recall the first three types
The passage assumes you know the first three: universal affirmative (كلية موجبة), universal negative (كلية سالبة), and particular affirmative (جزئية موجبة). List all six in order.
3
Understand the indefinite proposition
Indefinite (مهملة) propositions lack a quantifier, e.g., 'Human is animal' (الإنسان حيوان). Recognize that they are grammatically indefinite but logically ambiguous.
The author says indefinite is 'in the strength of' (في قوة) the particular, meaning it is treated as particular in logical analysis.
4
Apply the equivalence principle
Test the equivalence: 'Human is animal' is normally understood as 'Some human is animal' in logical contexts. Why would it not be universal? Because without a quantifier, it does not assert all humans.
In Arabic discourse, indefinite often implies some, but not necessarily all. The logician treats it as particular to avoid unwarranted universality.
5
Distinguish between grammatical and logical types
Grammatically, there are indefinite statements. Logically, they reduce to particulars. The author insists on six types because the indefinite does not add a new logical quantity.
This reflects a key principle: logical form is about quantification, not merely grammar.

4. Fourth: particular negative, e.g., 'Some animal is not human.' 5. Fifth: singular affirmative, e.g., 'Zaid is standing.' 6. Sixth: singular negative, e.g., 'Zaid is not standing.' Remarks: First: Know that the custom of the logicians in the mentioned division is that propositions, in terms of quantity and quality, are eight; because they add to the six we mentioned the indefinite affirmative, e.g., 'Human is animal,' and the indefinite negative, e.g., 'Animal is not human.' But we made them six, not eight, because the indefinite is equivalent to the particular; it is not an additional division. Thus propositions, in terms of quantity and quality, become six upon investigation, as we mentioned.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a classification passage. The author lists three of the six types of categorical propositions: particular negative, singular affirmative, and singular negative. (The first three types—universal affirmative, universal negative, and particular affirmative—were presumably listed earlier.) Then the author makes a remark about the standard classification. Logicians often count eight types by including indefinite (المهملة) affirmative and indefinite negative as separate. However, the author argues that indefinite propositions are equivalent to particular propositions in logical force. For example, 'Human is animal' (indefinite) is understood as 'Some human is animal.' Therefore, they are not a distinct type. Thus the correct count is six: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative, singular affirmative, singular negative. This distinction is important in Islamic logic because indefinite propositions are used in everyday discourse but are treated as particular in formal reasoning.

Subject vs. Predicate in Categorical Propositions
Author distinguishes subject and predicate reference in categorical propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the referent of the subject (external individuals) from that of the predicate (mental quiddity) in a categorical proposition, and identify a natural proposition.

Core Idea
Subject points to external individuals; predicate points to the mental common core.

In this passage, the author clarifies a crucial semantic distinction between the subject and predicate in a categorical proposition (qaḍiyya ḥamliyya). Misunderstanding this distinction leads to false judgments or tautologies. We will explore how the subject refers to all its external individuals, while the predicate refers only to the universal mental meaning shared by its individuals. A special case—the natural proposition—is also introduced.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms in the example
Take the proposition 'Every human is an animal' (kull insān ḥayawān). The subject is 'human' (insān) and the predicate is 'animal' (ḥayawān).
2
Determine what the subject refers to
The author says the subject (mawḍū‘) refers to all external individuals falling under it—here, every individual human like Zaid and Amr, whether actually existing or merely possible.
This includes both external existence (khārijiyya) and presumed existence (ḥaqīqiyya).
3
Determine what the predicate refers to
The predicate (maḥmūl) does not refer to external individuals like horses or mules. Instead, it refers to the absolute mental quiddity—the common core (al-qadr al-mushtarak) shared by all animals: animality.
If it referred to individuals, the judgment would be false or tautological.
4
Test the consequences of confusion
If the predicate 'animal' meant external animals, then 'Every human is an animal' would claim that each human is a horse or mule (false). Or if it meant 'human', it would be tautology (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil).
5
Recognize the natural proposition
When the subject itself is taken as the mental common core (not external individuals), the proposition is called natural (ṭabī‘iyya). Example: 'Animal is a genus, human is a species.'
In such propositions, the subject does not denote external individuals but the universal essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التنبيه الثاني : وهو مهم جدا، وهو أن تعلم أن المراد بالموضوع مناف للمراد [بالمحمول] (٢) في القضية الحملية؛ لأن المراد بالموضوع جميع أفراده الخارجية الداخلة تحت العنوان، سواء اعتبرنا الوجود الخارجي؛ لكونها خارجية، أو اعتبرنا تقدير الوجود؛ لكونها [حقيقية] (٤) ، ولا يراد بالموضوع القدر المشترك، الذي هو المعنى الذهني، بخلاف المحمول ؛ فإنه لا يقصد به الأفراد الخارجية، وإنما يراد به مطلق الماهية الذهنية التي هي القدر المشترك للأفراد.

بين ذلك قولك : كل إنسان حيوان قضية حملية موضوعها (الإنسان)، ومحمولها (الحيوان)، فالموضوع الذي [هو] (١) (الإنسان) يراد به أفراده الداخلة في لفظه ، فكل فرد من أفراد الإنسان محكوم عليه بأنه حيوان، بخلاف المحمول الذي هو (الحيوان) في هذا المثال ؛ فإنما يراد به معناه الذهني، الذي هو القدر المشترك بين أفراده، ولا يجوز أن تقصد أفراده ؛ لأنك لو قصدت أفراد الحيوان كالفرس والبغل كنت حاكماً على الإنسان بأنه فرس أو بغل، وقد قدمنا أن الحكم على المباين بمباينه إيجابًا أنه كاذب. أو الحكم على الشيء بنفسه، وهو تحصيل الحاصل، إن قصدت بالحيوان المحمول على (الإنسان) الإنسان.

وإن قصدت بالموضوع القدر المشترك، الذي هو الحقيقة الذهنية، سُمّيت القضية طبيعية كقولك : الحيوان جنس والإنسان نوع، وهكذا.
Second remark: And it is very important. It is that you know that what is intended by the subject differs from what is intended by the predicate in the categorical proposition. Because what is intended by the subject is all of its external individuals falling under the term, whether we consider external existence because they are external, or we consider the presumption of existence because they are real. And what is intended by the subject is not the common core (al-qadr al-mushtarak), which is the mental meaning. Unlike the predicate: it is not intended to refer to external individuals; rather, what is intended by it is the absolute mental quiddity, which is the common core of individuals. This is clarified by your saying: 'Every human is an animal' is a categorical proposition, its subject is 'human', its predicate is 'animal'. So the subject, which is 'human', is intended to mean its individuals that fall under its expression; thus every individual of human is judged to be an animal. Unlike the predicate, which is 'animal' in this example: it is intended only to mean its mental meaning, which is the common core among its individuals, and it is not permissible to intend its individuals because if you intended the individuals of animal like horse and mule, you would be judging that human is a horse or a mule, and we have previously stated that judging a thing to be something distinct from it affirmatively is false. Or it would be judging a thing to be itself, which is tautology (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil), if you intended by 'animal' predicated on 'human' that it is human. And if you intended by the subject the common core, which is the mental reality, the proposition is called natural (ṭabī‘iyya), like your saying: 'Animal is a genus, and human is a species,' and so on.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and clarificatory passage. The author makes a crucial distinction between the subject and predicate in a categorical proposition (qaḍiyya ḥamliyya). The subject is intended to refer to all its external individuals (afrād khārijiyya) that fall under the term. This includes both propositions about actually existing individuals (khārijiyya) and propositions about possible existents (ḥaqīqiyya). The predicate, on the other hand, is not intended to refer to external individuals but to the universal mental meaning (al-māhiyya al-dhihniyya) which is the common core (al-qadr al-mushtarak) among its individuals. The author illustrates with the example 'Every human is an animal' (kull insān ḥayawān). The subject 'human' means all individual humans (e.g., Zaid, Amr). The predicate 'animal' does not mean individual animals like horses or mules; if it did, the proposition would falsely claim that each human is a horse or mule. Alternatively, if the predicate were taken to mean exactly the same as the subject (i.e., 'human'), the proposition would be tautological (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil). The author then notes a special case: when the subject itself is taken as the mental common core (al-qadr al-mushtarak), the proposition is called natural (ṭabī‘iyya), as in 'Animal is a genus, human is a species.' In such propositions, the subject does not refer to external individuals but to the universal essence. This distinction is foundational in Islamic logic for understanding the semantics of categorical propositions and avoiding fallacies of equivocation.

قضية طبيعية
قدر مشترك

Distinguishing the Quantifier from the Subject
Author clarifies that the quantifier indicates the extent of the subject, not the subject itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical subject and the quantifier in any categorical proposition and explain their distinct roles.

Core Idea
The quantifier is not the subject; it only specifies quantity.

In logic, the quantifier (like 'every' or 'some') is often mistakenly thought to be part of the subject. This lesson clarifies their separation, which is essential for accurate proposition analysis and syllogistic reasoning. Mastering this distinction helps in usul al-fiqh when analyzing general and particular rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quantifier
Look for words like 'kull' (every/all) or 'ba'd' (some) at the start of the proposition. These are quantifiers.
2
Find the annexed noun
The quantifier is annexed to a noun (e.g., 'insan' in 'kullu insan'). That noun is the logical subject (mawdu').
3
Separate quantifier from subject
Understand that 'kull' or 'ba'd' is not part of the subject term; it indicates how many individuals of the subject are being judged.
4
Verify by parsing
Even if the quantifier is grammatically parsed as mubtada', logically it is not the subject. The subject is the noun that follows.
This prevents misinterpreting the structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التنبيه الثالث : اعلم أن السور الذي هو لفظة (كل) أو (بعض) ونحوهما ليس هو الموضوع، وإنما هو بيان للقدر المحكوم عليه من أفراد الموضوع، فقولك : (كل إنسان حيوان) أو (بعض الإنسان حيوان) الموضوع فيها المضاف إليه، الذي هو الإنسان، وأما السور الذي لفظة (كل) أو (بعض) وإن أعربت مبتداً فليست هي هو الموضوع، وإنما هي لفظ مبين للكمية المحكوم عليها من أفراد الموضوع : هل هي جميعها أو بعضها؟
The third warning: Know that the quantifier, which is the word 'every' or 'some' and the like, is not the subject. Rather, it is an indication of the quantity of the individuals of the subject that are judged. So when you say 'Every human is an animal' or 'Some human is an animal', the subject in them is the annexed noun, which is 'human'. As for the quantifier, which is the word 'every' or 'some', even if it is parsed as an initial subject (mubtada'), it is not the subject; rather, it is a word that clarifies the quantity of the individuals of the subject that are judged: whether they are all of them or some of them.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage defines the role of the quantifier (sawr) in categorical propositions. The author, Adud al-Din al-Iji (or his commentator), emphasizes that the quantifier—words like 'every' (kull) or 'some' (ba'd)—is not itself the logical subject (mawdu') of the proposition. Instead, it is an indicator that specifies which portion of the subject's individuals are being judged. For example, in 'Every human is an animal', the subject is 'human' (the noun annexed to the quantifier), and 'every' merely shows that the predicate 'animal' applies to all humans. Similarly, in 'Some human is an animal', 'some' restricts the judgment to a part. The author warns against misinterpreting the quantifier's syntactical position: even if it is parsed as a mubtada' in Arabic grammar, logically it is not the subject. This distinction is crucial for understanding the structure of propositions in logic and for correctly analyzing syllogisms. In Islamic legal reasoning (usul al-fiqh), this helps clarify rulings: e.g., 'Every intoxicant is haram'—the subject 'intoxicant' includes all its individuals, and the quantifier 'every' asserts universality.

Universal and Particular Quantifiers in Conditional Propositions
Author illustrates universal and particular quantifiers in conditionals with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct universal and particular conditional propositions (both connected and disjunctive) and distinguish their affirmative and negative forms.

Core Idea
Quantifiers in conditionals modify the connection, not the subject; universal means always, particular means sometimes.

In categorical logic, quantifiers like 'all' and 'some' specify the extent of the subject. In conditional logic, quantifiers specify how often the conditional relation holds: universally (always) or particularly (sometimes). This lesson uses concrete examples from the text to show how universal and particular quantifiers work in both connected and disjunctive conditionals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quantifier expression
Locate the quantifier words in the Arabic: 'ليس البتة' for universal negative, 'قد يكون' for particular affirmative, 'قد لا يكون' for particular negative, and 'ليس كلما' / 'ليس دائما' for specific negative forms.
These quantifiers directly modify the conditional relation, not the subject.
2
Analyze universal negative connected conditional
Examine the example: 'ليس البتة إذا كان الشيء إنسانًا كان حجرًا.' This asserts that the implication 'if human then stone' is never true. Recognize that the quantifier 'ليس البتة' makes it a universal negative conditional.
The truth of such a statement depends on the logical relation between antecedent and consequent.
3
Analyze universal negative disjunctive conditional
Examine the example: 'ليس البتة إما أن يكون الشيء أبيض وإما أن يكون بارداً.' The author explains why it is false: there is no incompatibility between whiteness and coldness (e.g., snow). Understand that universal negative disjunctives require absolute incompatibility.
This shows that quantifiers interact with the nature of the disjunction.
4
Analyze particular affirmative examples
For particular affirmative: 'قد يكون إذا كان الشيء حيوانا كان إنسانا' (connected) and 'قد يكون إما أن يكون الشيء حيوانا وإما أن يكون فرسا' (disjunctive). Note that 'قد يكون' indicates that the conditional holds only in some cases.
The truth of the disjunctive is particular because the incompatibility holds only when the animal is not the horse.
5
Analyze particular negative examples
Study the particular negative examples: 'قد لا يكون إذا كان هذا حيوانا كان إنسانا' (connected) and 'قد لا يكون إما أن يكون الشيء حيوانا وإما أن يكون إنسانا' (disjunctive). Also note the alternative forms 'ليس كلما' and 'ليس دائما'.
These show that the conditional or disjunction fails in some cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثاله في المتصلة ليس البتة إذا كان الشيء إنسانًا كان حجرًا.
ومثاله في المنفصلة ليس البتة إما أن يكون الشيء أبيض وإما أن يكون بارداً؛ لأن الصدق فيها بحسب صدق العناد، وهو هنا كاذب؛ إذ لا عناد بين البياض والبرودة؛ لأن تباينهما تباين مخالفة، فيجوز اجتماعهما كالواقع في الثلج.

ومثالها متصلة: إن كان هذا حيواناً كان إنسانًا) ومثالها منفصلة: (إما أن يكون هذا حيوانًا وإما أن يكون فراسًا . والمهملة في قوة الجزئية كما تقدم.
وأما الشرطية المخصوصة: فهي الشرطية التي حكم فيها على وضع معين من الأوضاع الممكنة، أي: حال من الأحوال الممكنة. ومثالها متصلة: (إن جئتني الآن أكرمتك)، فتخصيص ربطها بالوقت الحاضر دون غيره من الأزمنة صيرها مخصوصة.
ومثالها منفصلة: زيد) الآن إما كاتب أو غير كاتب.
فتبين بما ذكرنا أن الشرطية كالحملية تنقسم إلى مخصوصة وكلية وجزئية ومهملة وكل واحدة منها تكون موجبة أو سالبة، فالأقسام ثمانية، وهي في الحقيقة ستة؛ لأن المهملة السالبة والمهملة الموجبة راجعتان إلى الجزئية لأنها في قوتها، فتصير الأقسام في الحقيقة ستة كما تقدم.
واعلم أن شمول الكلية الحملية إنما هو للأفراد الداخلة تحت لفظ
And its example in the connected [conditional] of universal negative: 'It is never the case that if something is a human, it is a stone.' And its example in the disjunctive of universal negative: 'It is never the case that something is either white or cold'; because truth in it depends on the truth of incompatibility, and here it is false, for there is no incompatibility between whiteness and coldness, because their distinction is a distinction of opposition, so their combination is permissible, as is the case with snow. And the example of the particular affirmative quantifier for both connected and disjunctive together: 'It may be'. As you say in the connected: 'It may be that if something is an animal, it is a human.' And as you say in the disjunctive: 'It may be that something is either an animal or a horse'; because in the state when the animal is not the horse, incompatibility holds, but considering the truth of 'animal' for the horse, incompatibility is invalidated, so it is valid that the truth of incompatibility is particular. And the example of the particular negative quantifier for both together: 'It may not be', and for the connected only: 'Not every time', and for the disjunctive only: 'Not always'. So we say in the connected: 'It may not be that if this is an animal, it is a human.' And you say in the disjunctive: 'It may not be that something is either an animal or a human'; because in some states it combines being human and animal, so there is no incompatibility. And you say in the connected: 'Not every time something is an animal, it is a horse.' And in the disjunctive: 'Not always is something either an animal or a horse.' And the indeterminate conditional is that whose connection or incompatibility is stripped of all quantifiers. And its example for the connected: 'If this is an animal, it is a human.' And its example for the disjunctive: 'Either this is an animal or it is a horse.' And the indeterminate is in strength of the particular, as previously mentioned. As for the particularized conditional, it is the conditional in which judgment is made on a specific situation among the possible situations, i.e., a circumstance among possible circumstances. Its example for the connected: 'If you come to me now, I will honor you.' So particularizing its connection to the present time rather than other times made it particularized. And its example for the disjunctive: 'Zayd is now either a writer or not a writer.' So it becomes clear from what we mentioned that the conditional, like the categorical, divides into particularized, universal, particular, and indeterminate, and each one of them can be affirmative or negative, so the divisions are eight, but in reality they are six, because the indeterminate negative and the indeterminate positive both revert to the particular because they are in its strength, so the divisions become in reality six, as previously. And know that the comprehensiveness of the universal categorical is only for the individuals falling under the expression...
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of conditional propositions (al-shartiyya) according to their quantifiers (sawar). It covers universal (kulli), particular (juz'i), indeterminate (muhmala), and particularized (makhsusa) forms, with examples from both connected (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila) conditionals. The author first illustrates the universal negative (saliba kulliyya) for both types: for the connected, 'It is never the case that if something is a human, it is a stone' — clearly false because an object cannot be both human and stone simultaneously. For the disjunctive, 'It is never the case that something is either white or cold' is also false because whiteness and coldness can coexist (as in snow), so their incompatibility (inad) is not absolute. The author then explains the particular affirmative (mujaba juz'iyya) using the quantifier 'qad yakun' (it may be). For the connected: 'It may be that if something is an animal, it is a human' — true in some cases (e.g., humans are animals) but not all. For the disjunctive: 'It may be that something is either an animal or a horse' — here the incompatibility holds when the animal is not a horse, but fails when the animal is a horse (since 'animal' applies to 'horse'), hence the truth of the disjunction is only particular. Next, the particular negative (saliba juz'iyya) is shown with 'qad la yakun' (it may not be). For the connected: 'It may not be that if this is an animal, it is a human' — true when the animal is not human. For the disjunctive: 'It may not be that something is either an animal or a human' — because a thing can be both (a human is an animal), so incompatibility fails in some cases. Additionally, there are exclusive quantifiers for each type: 'laysa kullama' (not every time) for connected, and 'laysa da'iman' (not always) for disjunctive. The indeterminate conditional (muhmala) is defined as stripped of all quantifiers, e.g., 'If this is an animal, it is a human' — its logical force is equivalent to the particular. The particularized conditional (makhsusa) is a judgment tied to a specific situation; e.g., 'If you come to me now, I will honor you' restricts the consequence to the present moment. The passage concludes by summarizing the divisions: eight in number (universal/particular/indeterminate/particularized, each affirmative and negative), but in reality six because the indeterminate affirmative and negative reduce to the particular. An additional note states that the universality of the categorical proposition pertains to individuals under the subject term, but the text is cut off here.

Key Terms
المتصلة (al-muttasila): A conditional proposition formed by a connection (talazum) between antecedent and consequent, e.g., 'If A then B'. [Note: No significant shift; used consistently across logic texts.]
المنفصلة (al-munfasila): A conditional proposition formed by incompatibility (inad) between two parts, e.g., 'Either A or B' (often exclusive). [Note: No significant shift.]
السور (al-sawr): The quantifier that specifies the extent of the truth of the conditional or disjunctive relation; e.g., 'always', 'never', 'sometimes', etc. [Note: In categoricals, sawr refers to subject quantity; in conditionals, it refers to the modal quantifier over the conditional relation.]
الجزئي (al-juz'i): The particular quantifier ('sometimes'), indicating the conditional holds in some cases but not all.
الكلي (al-kulli): The universal quantifier ('always' or 'never'), indicating the conditional holds in all cases (or none).
المهملة (al-muhmala): An indeterminate conditional without any quantifier; logically equivalent to the particular.
المخصوصة (al-makhsusa): A particularized conditional restricted to a specific situation (e.g., time), not a general law. [Note: Sometimes called 'shartiyya mu'ayyana' in later texts.]
العناد (al-'inad): Incompatibility between two propositions or terms, such that they cannot both be true (and sometimes not both false) in disjunctives. [Note: In disjunctives, 'inad' refers to the exclusive relation; in later logic, it may be distinguished from 'tanaqud' (contradiction).]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a universal negative connected conditional?
A) ليس البتة إذا كان الشيء إنسانًا كان حجرًا.
B) قد يكون إذا كان الشيء حيوانا كان إنسانًا.
C) ليس كلما كان الشيء حيوانا كان فرسا.
D) إما أن يكون هذا حيوانا وإما أن يكون فرسا.

Key Takeaway
Universal quantifiers in conditionals assert that the connection or incompatibility holds in all cases; particular quantifiers assert it holds in some cases.

Indeterminate and Particularized Conditionals
Author explains indeterminate and particularized conditionals with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish indeterminate conditionals from particularized conditionals and explain why indeterminate conditionals are logically equivalent to particular conditionals.

Core Idea
An indeterminate conditional is like a 'bare' conditional; it functions as a particular conditional in logical force.

Not all conditionals come with an explicit quantifier. Some are left indeterminate (muhmala), like 'If this is an animal, it is a human'. Others are restricted to a specific situation (makhsusa), like 'If you come now, I will honor you'. This lesson clarifies these two types and their logical behavior.

Guided Path
1
Identify the indeterminate conditional
Find the examples: 'إن كان هذا حيواناً كان إنسانًا' (connected) and 'إما أن يكون هذا حيوانًا وإما أن يكون فرسا' (disjunctive). Note the absence of any quantifier word.
The text calls these 'muhmala' (indeterminate).
2
Understand logical equivalence to particular
The author states that the indeterminate is in the strength of the particular. This means that without a quantifier, we cannot assume universality; logically, it is treated as 'sometimes true'.
This is a key principle: when a conditional is not quantified, it defaults to particular.
3
Identify the particularized conditional
Find the connected example: 'إن جئتني الآن أكرمتك' and the disjunctive: 'زيد الآن إما كاتب أو غير كاتب'. Notice the word 'الآن' which restricts to a specific time.
The conditional is restricted to a specific situation, not general.
4
Analyze the effect of particularization
The particularized conditional is not universal; it applies only in that specific situation. The text says 'تخصيص ربطها بالوقت الحاضر دون غيره من الأزمنة صيرها مخصوصة'.
This contrasts with indeterminate, which is open to any situation but not quantified.
5
Distinguish indeterminate from particularized
Indeterminate: no quantifier, defaults to particular. Particularized: quantifier is a specific situation (e.g., now). Both are non-universal, but particularized is more specific about the context.
This distinction is important for understanding the scope of conditional statements.

The Divisions of Conditional Propositions: Eight in Principle, Six in Reality
Author summarizes the classification of conditionals and reduction of forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the eight forms of conditional propositions and explain why the indeterminate forms reduce to particular, resulting in six distinct forms.

Core Idea
Conditional propositions come in eight logical forms, but indeterminate affirmatives and negatives collapse into particular ones, leaving six essential forms.

The author concludes his discussion by classifying all conditional propositions into eight types based on quantifier and polarity. However, because indeterminate conditionals are logically equivalent to particular ones, the effective number of distinct forms is six. This lesson consolidates the classification.

Guided Path
1
List the basic division
Start with the four quantifier types: universal, particular, indeterminate, particularized. Each can be affirmative or negative, giving 8.
These are the eight forms mentioned by the author.
2
Identify the indeterminate forms
The author states that the indeterminate affirmative and negative both revert to the particular. Why? Because logically, an unquantified conditional is equivalent to a particular conditional (it is not universal).
This reduction is based on logical force, not on syntactic form.
3
Apply the reduction
Remove the two indeterminate forms. The remaining are: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative, particularized affirmative, particularized negative. That's six.
Note: particularized remains distinct from particular because it specifies a situation.
4
Understand the note on categorical universality
The last sentence begins to compare with categorical propositions: 'واعلم أن شمول الكلية الحملية إنما هو للأفراد الداخلة تحت لفظ'. This is cut off but indicates a distinction in how universality applies.
The author likely means that categorical universal quantifies over individuals, while conditional universal quantifies over cases.

Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of conditional propositions (al-shartiyya) according to their quantifiers (sawar). It covers universal (kulli), particular (juz'i), indeterminate (muhmala), and particularized (makhsusa) forms, with examples from both connected (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila) conditionals. The author first illustrates the universal negative (saliba kulliyya) for both types: for the connected, 'It is never the case that if something is a human, it is a stone' — clearly false because an object cannot be both human and stone simultaneously. For the disjunctive, 'It is never the case that something is either white or cold' is also false because whiteness and coldness can coexist (as in snow), so their incompatibility (inad) is not absolute. The author then explains the particular affirmative (mujaba juz'iyya) using the quantifier 'qad yakun' (it may be). For the connected: 'It may be that if something is an animal, it is a human' — true in some cases (e.g., humans are animals) but not all. For the disjunctive: 'It may be that something is either an animal or a horse' — here the incompatibility holds when the animal is not a horse, but fails when the animal is a horse (since 'animal' applies to 'horse'), hence the truth of the disjunction is only particular. Next, the particular negative (saliba juz'iyya) is shown with 'qad la yakun' (it may not be). For the connected: 'It may not be that if this is an animal, it is a human' — true when the animal is not human. For the disjunctive: 'It may not be that something is either an animal or a human' — because a thing can be both (a human is an animal), so incompatibility fails in some cases. Additionally, there are exclusive quantifiers for each type: 'laysa kullama' (not every time) for connected, and 'laysa da'iman' (not always) for disjunctive. The indeterminate conditional (muhmala) is defined as stripped of all quantifiers, e.g., 'If this is an animal, it is a human' — its logical force is equivalent to the particular. The particularized conditional (makhsusa) is a judgment tied to a specific situation; e.g., 'If you come to me now, I will honor you' restricts the consequence to the present moment. The passage concludes by summarizing the divisions: eight in number (universal/particular/indeterminate/particularized, each affirmative and negative), but in reality six because the indeterminate affirmative and negative reduce to the particular. An additional note states that the universality of the categorical proposition pertains to individuals under the subject term, but the text is cut off here.

Key Terms
المتصلة (al-muttasila): A conditional proposition formed by a connection (talazum) between antecedent and consequent, e.g., 'If A then B'. [Note: No significant shift; used consistently across logic texts.]
المنفصلة (al-munfasila): A conditional proposition formed by incompatibility (inad) between two parts, e.g., 'Either A or B' (often exclusive). [Note: No significant shift.]
السور (al-sawr): The quantifier that specifies the extent of the truth of the conditional or disjunctive relation; e.g., 'always', 'never', 'sometimes', etc. [Note: In categoricals, sawr refers to subject quantity; in conditionals, it refers to the modal quantifier over the conditional relation.]
الجزئي (al-juz'i): The particular quantifier ('sometimes'), indicating the conditional holds in some cases but not all.
الكلي (al-kulli): The universal quantifier ('always' or 'never'), indicating the conditional holds in all cases (or none).
المهملة (al-muhmala): An indeterminate conditional without any quantifier; logically equivalent to the particular.
المخصوصة (al-makhsusa): A particularized conditional restricted to a specific situation (e.g., time), not a general law. [Note: Sometimes called 'shartiyya mu'ayyana' in later texts.]
العناد (al-'inad): Incompatibility between two propositions or terms, such that they cannot both be true (and sometimes not both false) in disjunctives. [Note: In disjunctives, 'inad' refers to the exclusive relation; in later logic, it may be distinguished from 'tanaqud' (contradiction).]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many distinct forms of conditional propositions exist after reduction?
A) 8
B) 6
C) 4
D) 2

Key Takeaway
Though conditionals can be classified into eight forms based on quantifier and polarity, the indeterminate forms are redundant, leaving six distinct logical forms.

The Fourfold Division of Conditional Propositions
Author explains the divisions of conditional propositions into universal, particular, indefinite, and specified.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any conditional proposition as specified, universal, particular, or indefinite based on its quantifier and context.

Core Idea
Conditional quantifiers range over situations, not individuals—a key difference from categoricals.

In the previous lesson, we learned how categorical propositions are divided by quantity. The author now extends this analysis to conditional propositions, showing that they too can be universal, particular, indefinite, or even specified to a concrete situation. Understanding these divisions is key to handling conditional arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the connector type
Determine if the conditional is conjunctive (متصلة) or disjunctive (منفصلة). This affects how quantifiers apply.
Conjunctive uses 'if...then'; disjunctive uses 'either...or'.
2
Look for explicit quantifiers
Search for words like 'كلما' (whenever) for universal, 'ليس دائما' (not always) for negative universal. If absent, it may be indefinite or specified.
3
Check for specification to time/situation
If the conditional refers to a specific time (e.g., 'now') or a named situation, it is specified (مخصوصة). Otherwise, consider it indefinite.
Example: 'If you come now, I will honor you' is specified.
4
Distinguish universal vs. particular scope
Remember: universal conditionals cover all possible situations; particular cover some situations. The scope is not over individuals.
This is a key difference from categoricals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتقول في المتصلة : ليس) كلما كان الشيء حيوانا كان فرسا) . وفي المنفصلة : ليس دائما إما أن يكون الشيء حيوانا وإما أن يكون فرسا) . والشرطية المهملة هي التي تجرّد ربطها أو عنادها عن جميع الأسوار. ومثالها متصلة : إن كان هذا حيواناً كان إنسانًا) ومثالها منفصلة : (إما أن يكون هذا حيوانًا وإما أن يكون فرسًا . والمهملة في قوة الجزئية كما تقدم. وأما الشرطية المخصوصة : فهي الشرطية التي حكم فيها على وضع معين من الأوضاع الممكنة، أي: حال من الأحوال الممكنة. ومثالها متصلة : (إن جئتني الآن أكرمتك)، فتخصيص ربطها بالوقت الحاضر دون غيره من الأزمنة صيرها مخصوصة . ومثالها منفصلة : زيد) الآن إما كاتب أو غير كاتب . فتبين بما ذكرنا أن الشرطية كالحملية تنقسم إلى مخصوصة وكلية وجزئية ومهملة وكل واحدة منها تكون موجبة أو سالبة، فالأقسام ثمانية، وهي في الحقيقة ستة ؛ لأن المهملة السالبة والمهملة الموجبة راجعتان إلى الجزئية لأنها في قوتها، فتصير الأقسام في الحقيقة ستة كما تقدم. واعلم أن شمول الكلية الحملية إنما هو للأفراد الداخلة تحت لفظ الموضوع، وشمول الجزئية الحملية إنما هو لبعض الأفراد المذكورة، وأما الشرطية الكلية فشمولها بحسب الأوضاع والأحوال لا الأفراد، وكذلك الشرطية الجزئية، فشمولها لبعض الأوضاع والأحوال لا الأفراد، وقد قدمنا أمثلة الجميع .
And you say in the conjunctive: 'Not every time something is an animal it is a horse.' And in the disjunctive: 'Not always either something is an animal or it is a horse.' The indefinite conditional is that whose connection or disconnection is stripped of all quantifiers. Its example conjunctive: 'If this is an animal, it is a human.' And its example disjunctive: 'Either this is an animal or it is a horse.' The indefinite has the force of the particular, as previously. As for the specified conditional: it is the conditional in which judgment is passed on a specific situation among possible situations, i.e., a state among possible states. Its example conjunctive: 'If you come to me now, I will honor you,' so specifying its connection to the present time rather than other times made it specified. And its example disjunctive: 'Zaid is now either a writer or a non-writer.' So it is clear from what we have mentioned that the conditional, like the categorical, is divided into specified, universal, particular, and indefinite, and each of them is affirmative or negative, so the divisions are eight, but in reality they are six, because the negative indefinite and the positive indefinite revert to the particular since they have its force, so the divisions become in reality six as previously. And know that the scope of the universal categorical is only over the individuals falling under the subject term, and the scope of the particular categorical is only over some of the mentioned individuals. As for the universal conditional, its scope is according to situations and states, not individuals, and likewise the particular conditional, its scope is over some situations and states, not individuals, and we have already presented examples of all.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage defines the various types of conditional propositions (al-shartiyya) and their quantifier classifications, paralleling the earlier discussion of categorical propositions. The author begins by giving examples of negative universal conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals: 'Not every time something is an animal is it a horse' and 'Not always either something is an animal or a horse.' These show that conditionals can be negated universally. Next, the indefinite conditional (al-muhmala) is defined as that which strips its connector (for conjunctives) or its disjunctor (for disjunctives) of all quantifiers (sawar). Examples: conjunctive 'If this is an animal, it is a human' and disjunctive 'Either this is an animal or a horse.' The author emphasizes that the indefinite has the force of the particular (juz'iyya), meaning it is treated as a particular proposition in logical reasoning, not as a universal. Then the specified conditional (al-makhsusa) is introduced: it is a conditional where the judgment is tied to a specific possible situation or state. Example conjunctive: 'If you come to me now, I will honor you'—the specification to 'now' makes it specified. Disjunctive example: 'Zaid is now either a writer or a non-writer.' After these definitions, the author summarizes that conditionals, like categoricals, are divided into four types: specified, universal, particular, and indefinite. Each type can be affirmative or negative, giving eight types. However, in reality, there are only six, because both the positive and negative indefinite are considered equivalent to the particular (since the indefinite has the force of the particular). Therefore, the effective divisions are six: specified affirmative, specified negative, universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. Finally, the author clarifies a key difference in the scope of quantifiers between categoricals and conditionals. Universal categoricals range over individuals falling under the subject term, while universal conditionals range over situations and states (awda' wa ahwal). Similarly, particular categoricals range over some individuals, whereas particular conditionals range over some situations. This distinction is crucial for understanding how conditionals function in logical arguments, especially in the Islamic legal and theological context where conditionals often express rulings contingent on specific conditions.

شرطية منفصلة
مهملة
مخصوصة
كلية
جزئية

The Indefinite Conditional and Its Particular Force
Author explains the divisions of conditional propositions into universal, particular, indefinite, and specified.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert an indefinite conditional into a particular conditional and explain its logical force.

Core Idea
The indefinite conditional has the logical force of the particular — a crucial rule for correct inference.

The author explicitly states that the indefinite (مهملة) has the force of the particular (جزئية). This means that an unconditional statement like 'If this is an animal, it is a human' is logically equivalent to 'For some situations, if it is an animal, it is a human.' This insight prevents overgeneralization in reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify an indefinite conditional
Look for a conditional with no quantifier and no specification to a particular time or situation.
Example: 'If this is an animal, it is a human' is indefinite.
2
Recall the rule: indefinite = particular
The passage states: 'المهملة في قوة الجزئية' (the indefinite has the force of the particular).
This is a fixed rule in Islamic logic.
3
Rephrase as a particular conditional
Rewrite the indefinite as 'Sometimes/fot some cases, if A then B' or 'For some possible situations, A implies B'.
This clarifies that it does not apply universally.
4
Contrast with a universal conditional
Compare: 'كلما كان الشيء حيواناً كان إنساناً' (Whenever something is an animal, it is a human) is universal false. The indefinite 'إن كان هذا حيواناً كان إنساناً' is particular true for some cases (e.g., a human).
This shows that indefinite is weaker.

The Reduction from Eight to Six Effective Types
Author explains the divisions of conditional propositions into universal, particular, indefinite, and specified.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the six effective divisions of conditional propositions and explain why the indefinite is merged into particular.

Core Idea
Although conditional propositions have 8 nominal types (4 quantities × 2 qualities), only 6 are logically distinct because indefinite collpses into particular.

After classifying conditionals into four types and noting that each can be affirmative or negative, the author points out that the indefinite, whether affirmative or negative, reduces to the particular. Thus, the effective divisions are six. This reduction is important for simplifying the logical system.

Guided Path
1
List the four quantity types
Specified (مخصوصة), Universal (كلية), Particular (جزئية), Indefinite (مهملة).
2
Multiply by two qualities
Each type can be affirmative or negative, giving 8 combinations.
8 = 4 × 2
3
Identify the indefinite as non-distinct
The passage says the indefinite has the force of the particular, so both affirmative and negative indefinite are equivalent to their particular counterparts.
Thus indefinite affirmative = particular affirmative; indefinite negative = particular negative.
4
Remove indefinite to get six
Since indefinite adds no new logical category, the effective types are: specified affirmative, specified negative, universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative.
Total = 6
5
Verify with examples
Check that each of the six types can be instantiated with real conditionals from Islamic texts.
Example: 'ليس كلما كان الشيء حيوانا كان فرسا' is universal negative.

The author emphasizes that the indefinite has the force of the particular (juz'iyya), meaning it is treated as a particular proposition in logical reasoning, not as a universal. Then the specified conditional (al-makhsusa) is introduced: it is a conditional where the judgment is tied to a specific possible situation or state. Example conjunctive: 'If you come to me now, I will honor you'—the specification to 'now' makes it specified. Disjunctive example: 'Zaid is now either a writer or a non-writer.' After these definitions, the author summarizes that conditionals, like categoricals, are divided into four types: specified, universal, particular, and indefinite. Each type can be affirmative or negative, giving eight types. However, in reality, there are only six, because both the positive and negative indefinite are considered equivalent to the particular (since the indefinite has the force of the particular). Therefore, the effective divisions are six: specified affirmative, specified negative, universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. Finally, the author clarifies a key difference in the scope of quantifiers between categoricals and conditionals. Universal categoricals range over individuals falling under the subject term, while universal conditionals range over situations and states (awda' wa ahwal). Similarly, particular categoricals range over some individuals, whereas particular conditionals range over some situations. This distinction is crucial for understanding how conditionals function in logical arguments, especially in the Islamic legal and theological context where conditionals often express rulings contingent on specific conditions.

Key Terms
كلية
جزئية
مهملة
مخصوصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because specified conditionals are rare
B) Because indefinite merges into particular
C) Because universal and particular are the same
D) Because negative conditionals are excluded

Key Takeaway
The eight nominal divisions of conditionals reduce to six effective ones because indefinite conditionals are logically equivalent to particular conditionals.
