External vs. Real Propositions
Author explains the division of propositions into external and real.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between external (kharijiyya) and real (haqiqiyya) propositions and classify any given categorical proposition based on the existence requirement.

Core Idea
Some propositions require existence of subjects; others do not.

In logic, not all propositions make the same existence claim. Some assume the subject actually exists, others allow hypothetical existence. This distinction is crucial for handling empty classes and universal statements in Islamic theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the criterion
The author defines two types based on whether the truth of the proposition requires the external existence of the subject's individuals.
2
Identify the subject term
In any proposition, locate the subject (mawḍū‘) and its concept (concept of the subject).
3
Check existence requirement
Determine if the proposition considers actual existence (in past, present, or future) or is content with assuming existence.
The 'three times' are past, present, and future.
4
Classify the proposition
If actual existence is considered, it is external (khārijiyya); if not, it is real (ḥaqīqiyya).
5
Apply to examples
Test with 'Every human is animal' (external) and 'Every impossible thing is not a creator' (real).
The source's own example and a typical kalam example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه : اعلم أن من المقرر في فن المنطق أن من أنواع تقسيم القضايا تقسيمها إلى قضية خارجية وقضية حقيقية، وإيضاح الفرق بينهما أن كل قضية اعتبر في صدق عنوانها على مفهومه الذي هو حقيقة الموضوع وجود الأفراد الخارجية في أحد الأزمنة الثلاثة فهي المسماة بالخارجية؛ لوجود أفراد موضوعها في الخارج، وأما إن لم يُعتبر وجودها في الخارج بل اكتفي فيها بتقدير الوجود مع كونه غير واقع بالفعل فهي المسماة بالحقيقة . ومثال الخارجية كل إنسان حيوان؛ لأن العنوان الذي هو (الإنسان) في هذا المثال معتبر في صدقه وجود أفراده في الخارج، كزيد وعمرو .
Note: Know that it is established in the art of logic that among the types of division of propositions is their division into external proposition and real proposition. The clarification of the difference between them is that every proposition in whose truth the existence of external individuals in one of the three times is considered for the truth of its title upon its concept, which is the reality of the subject, it is called external, because of the existence of the individuals of its subject in the external world. As for when the existence of individuals in the external world is not considered, but rather one is content with assuming existence even though it is not actually occurring, then it is called real. An example of the external is 'Every human is an animal'; because the title 'human' in this example is considered for its truth the existence of its individuals in the external world, like Zayd and Amr.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage explains a critical distinction in the logic of propositions: the division into external (خارجية) and real (حقيقية) propositions. The author defines an external proposition as one where the truth of the statement requires that the individuals of the subject actually exist externally at some time (past, present, or future). For example, the proposition 'Every human is an animal' is external because human individuals like Zayd and Amr actually exist in the external world. In contrast, a real proposition does not require actual external existence; it suffices to assume or suppose existence, even if unrealized. This distinction is important in Islamic logic for handling propositions about non-existent subjects (e.g., 'Every impossible entity is not a creator') or class statements where the subject term may be empty. The category of real propositions allows logical statements to hold regardless of the existential status of the subject. The author's example 'Every human is an animal' is a straightforward case: since humans exist, the proposition is external. The concept of 'three times' (الأزمنة الثلاثة) refers to past, present, and future, so the existence requirement is not limited to the present moment. This division is fundamental to understanding quantification and truth conditions in categorical propositions.

Key Terms
قضية خارجية
قضية حقيقية

Key Takeaway
The truth of an external proposition depends on the actual existence of its subject's individuals, while a real proposition does not require such existence.

External and Real Propositions: The Criterion and Examples
Author explains and illustrates the division into external and real propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between external (kharijiyya) and real (haqiqiyya) propositions based on whether the subject term requires actual existence of its individuals in one of the three times.

Core Idea
A proposition's truth may or may not require actual existence of its subject's referents.

In logic, propositions are divided into two types: external and real. The key difference lies in whether the subject term must actually exist in the past, present, or future for the proposition to be true. This distinction is essential for evaluating statements about non-existent or hypothetical entities, which often appear in Islamic theological and legal discussions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the criterion
Read the passage carefully. For an external proposition, the truth of the subject term (al-'unwan) requires that at least one individual of that subject actually exists in the past, present, or future. For a real proposition, only a hypothetical assumption of existence is needed—actual existence is not required.
2
Identify the subject term
In any given proposition, identify the subject (the thing being talked about). Ask: Does this subject have actual instances in the external world? If yes, it is likely external; if not, it is real.
Examples: 'Every human is an animal' – 'human' has actual instances (Zaid, 'Amr). 'The 'anqa' is a bird' – 'anqa' has no actual instances.
3
Test the criterion with examples
Apply the criterion to the examples given in the passage: (1) 'Every human is an animal' is external because humans exist. (2) 'The 'anqa' is a bird' is real because the 'anqa' is a mythical creature. (3) 'Safar is a beast' is real for the same reason.
The passage also quotes poetry mentioning 'safar' to illustrate its non-existence.
4
Recognize the poetic summary
The author summarizes the distinction in an arjuzah (didactic poem). The key line: when existence (in any time) is considered, it is external; when existence is not considered, it is real. Memorize this rule.
The poem condenses the definition into a memorable form.

Caution: Know that it is established in the art of logic that among the types of division of propositions is dividing them into external proposition and real proposition. The clarification of the difference between them is that every proposition in which, for the truth of its 'unwan upon its concept (which is the reality of the subject), the existence of external individuals in one of the three times is considered, it is called external due to the existence of the individuals of its subject externally. As for that in which their existence externally is not considered, but rather sufficed with the assumption of existence while it is not actual, it is called real. An example of the external is 'Every human is an animal', because the 'unwan which is 'human' in this example, its truth requires the existence of its individuals externally, like Zaid and 'Amr. An example of the real is your saying 'The 'anqa' is a bird, strange in shape, long-necked, hunting children and small cattle', because the 'anqa' is a bird imagined by the Arabs and there is no existence of any individual of it externally at all; therefore they used as a proverb for that which is lost and cannot be hoped for at all, saying 'the 'anqa' flew away with him'. Examples of the real: the definition of safar as 'a beast in the stomach of a human that bites on his cartilage when he is hungry'—and the sharāsif are the roots of the ribs—because safar is something imagined by the Arabs with no existence externally. From it is the saying of A'sha Bahila: 'And he who does not wait for what is in the pot, nor does safar bite on his cartilage.' The saying 'the 'anqa' is a bird' and 'safar is a beast'—in the truth of their 'unwan upon their subject, external existence is not considered, but rather the assumption of existence is sufficient, because if the mentioned bird and beast were supposed to exist externally, the name would apply to them. And I have indicated in my versification in the art of logic the difference between external and real with my saying: 'Every proposition, according to what they mentioned, in the truth of its 'unwan, existence of its subject is considered, in the present, past, or future—so it is external, as is customary. And where existence is not considered, the real is established.'
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage introduces and illustrates the distinction between two types of propositions: external (kharijiyya) and real (haqiqiyya). The author begins by stating that this is a standard division in logic. The key criterion is whether, for the truth of the subject term's application, one requires actual existence of at least one individual in the past, present, or future. If yes, it is an external proposition; if not, and one merely assumes existence hypothetically, it is a real proposition.

The author provides clear examples. 'Every human is an animal' is external because 'human' has actual individuals like Zaid and 'Amr who exist or existed. The proposition is true because of those real individuals. In contrast, 'The 'anqa' is a bird of strange shape' is a real proposition: the 'anqa' is a mythical creature that never existed, but the proposition is still meaningful because it describes the imagined concept; if one were to suppose it existed, the description would apply. The same applies to 'safar', a mythical beast said to bite a hungry person's ribs. The author also quotes a line of poetry from A'sha Bahila to show the cultural use of 'safar'. Finally, the author summarizes the distinction in a didactic poem (arjuzah) he composed for logic: when existence (in any time) is considered for the truth of the subject, it is external; when existence is not considered, it is real (haqiqi). This classification is fundamental for understanding how propositions relate to reality and is especially important in discussions of logical validity and truth conditions.

Real Propositions: Truth Without External Existence
Author defines real propositions using mythical creatures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define a real proposition (ḥaqīqiyya) and identify it using the criterion of presumed existence.

Core Idea
Real propositions can be true even if their subjects never existed.

In Islamic logic, not all propositions require their subjects to actually exist. The author introduces real propositions using mythical creatures like the Anqa and Safar, which are imagined but not real. Understanding this distinction helps you correctly evaluate universal statements in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition's subject
Look for the term about which something is being said. In the example 'The Anqa is a bird,' the subject is 'the Anqa.'
2
Determine if the subject exists externally
Check if the subject has actual existence in the outside world at any time (past, present, or future). The Anqa is a mythical bird with no external existence.
3
Check if truth depends on existence
If the subject does not exist, ask: Would the predicate be true of it if it did exist? For the Anqa, if it existed, it would indeed be a bird, so the statement is true.
4
Classify as real or external
If the statement does not require actual existence, it is a real proposition. If it does require existence, it is external.
The author's definition: a real proposition is one where presumed existence suffices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثال الحقيقية قولك : (العنقاء طائر عجيب الشكل طويل العنق يصطاد الصبيان وصغار البقر ) ؛ لأن العنقاء طائر تتخيله العرب ولا وجود لفرد من أفراده في الخارج البتة، ولذلك كانوا يضربون المثل للمفقود الذي لا طمع في وجوده بحال بقولهم: (طارت به العنقاء) . أمثلة الحقيقية تعريف الصَّفَر بأنه (دابة في جوف الإنسان تعض على شراسيفه إذا جاع، والشراسيف أصول الأضلاع؛ لأن الصفر شيء تتخيله العرب مع أنه لا وجود له في الخارج، ومنه قول أعشى باهلة : ومن لا يتأرى لما في القدر يرقبه ولا يعض على شرسوفه الصفر قولهم (العنقاء طائر، والصفر (دابة لم يعتبر في صدق عنوانه على موضوعه الوجود الخارجي، بل اكتفي بتقدير الوجود؛ لأن الطائر والدابة المذكورين لو فُرض وجودهما في الخارج لصدق عليهم الاسم، وقد أشرت في أرجوزتي في فن المنطق إلى الفرق بين الخارجية والحقيقة بقولي : كل قضية على ماذكروا في صدق عنوان لها يعتبرُ وجود موضوع لها في الحال أو المضي أو الاستقبال فخارجية على المعهود وحيث لا اعتبار بالوجودِ بل كان بالتقدير للوجود مع كون الوجود خارجًا قد لا يقع فهي الحقيقية والفرق أضـا بينهمــا بـأنـه لـو فـرضــا الحصر للألوان في السواد في خارج والغير ذو انفقادِ فقولك البياض لون فنّدوا بحسب الأول إذ لا يوجد وكذبه لفقده في الخارج مع اعتبارنا الوجود الخارجي وصادق بالاعتبار الثاني كون البياض أحد الألوانِ إذ فيه معنى كلُّ ما لو وجدا كان بياضًا فبحيث لو بدا وجوده يكون لونا فعلـم الصدق مع كون البياض منعدم وصدقُ حصر اللون بالسواد بحسَب الأول أمر باد وكذبه بحسب الثاني اتضح فما أبيح افعل ودع مالم يُبح انتهى محل الغرض من الأرجوزة المذكورة.
An example of a real proposition is your saying: 'The Anqa is a bird of strange shape, long neck, preying on boys and young cows'; because the Anqa is a bird imagined by the Arabs and there is absolutely no individual of its kind in external existence. Therefore they used it as a parable for something lost which there is no hope of its existence at all, saying: 'The Anqa carried it away.' Examples of the real: the definition of Safar as 'an animal in man's belly that bites his cartilage when he is hungry'—the cartilage are the roots of the ribs; because Safar is something the Arabs imagine with no existence externally. From it is the saying of A'sha of Bahila: 'Who does not wait for what is in the pot, nor does Safar bite his cartilage.' They say: 'The Anqa is a bird, and Safar is an animal' – external existence was not considered in the truth of the predicate applying to its subject; rather they were content with presuming existence, because if the mentioned bird and animal were assumed to exist externally, the name would be true of them. And I have indicated in my poem on the art of logic the difference between the external and the real by my saying: 'Every proposition, according to what they mentioned, for the truth of its predicate, the existence of its subject is considered in the present, past, or future; so it is external on the usual way. And where existence is not considered, rather it is by presumption of existence, while existence externally may not occur, then it is the real. And the difference between them is that if we were to imagine the restriction of colors to black externally, and the other is nonexistent, then your saying 'Whiteness is a color' is refuted according to the first [view] since it does not exist; and its falsity due to its lack externally, while we consider external existence. And it is true according to the second consideration that whiteness is one of the colors, since in it is the meaning: everything that if it existed would be whiteness. So if its existence appears, it would be a color. Thus the truth is known despite whiteness being nonexistent. And the truth of restricting color to black according to the first [view] is a clear matter, and its falsity according to the second is evident. So do what is permitted and leave what is not permitted.' This ends the intended purpose of the mentioned poem.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates the difference between external (خارجية) and real (حقيقية) propositions. The author first gives examples: the statement 'The Anqa is a bird...' and the definition of Safar as a mythical creature. These are real propositions because their subject has no external existence, yet the predicate can be truly affirmed as long as the subject is conceivable. The author then quotes his own didactic poem which systematically explains: an external proposition requires actual existence of the subject at some time (present, past, or future); a real proposition dispenses with actual existence and only requires that if the subject existed, the predicate would apply. The poem further illustrates with the example of 'Whiteness is a color'—this is false as an external proposition (since whiteness may not exist externally) but true as a real one (since if whiteness existed, it would be a color). Conversely, 'All colors are black' is false in both senses. The lesson emphasizes that the truth-value of a proposition can depend on the type of proposition (external vs. real).

Key Terms
الحقيقية
الخارجية
تقدير الوجود

Key Takeaway

The Systematic Rule: External vs. Real Propositions
Author contrasts external and real propositions using a didactic poem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule from the author's poem to classify any proposition as external or real.

Core Idea
The poem provides a clear decision procedure: if existence is required, it's external; if only presumed, it's real.

The author encapsulates the distinction in a didactic poem, which not only summarizes the rule but also illustrates it with the example of 'Whiteness is a color.' This lesson unpacks the poem and shows how it refines your ability to classify propositions accurately in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem's key line
The poem states: 'Every proposition... for the truth of its predicate, the existence of its subject is considered in the present, past, or future — so it is external. And where existence is not considered, rather it is by presumption of existence... then it is the real.'
2
Apply to a proposition: check subject existence
Determine if the subject is required to have existed at some time (past, present, or future). If yes, it's external.
Example: 'The sky is blue' requires the sky to exist, so external.
3
If no existence required, test presumed existence
Even if the subject does not exist, ask: Would the predicate be true if it existed? If yes, it's real.
Example: 'Whiteness is a color' – whiteness may not exist, but if it did, it would be a color, so real.
4
Practice with the whiteness example
The poem uses 'Whiteness is a color' to show that the same proposition can be false as external (since whiteness may be absent) but true as real (if whiteness existed, it would be a color).
This highlights that truth depends on the type of proposition assumed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثال الحقيقية قولك : (العنقاء طائر عجيب الشكل طويل العنق يصطاد الصبيان وصغار البقر ) ؛ لأن العنقاء طائر تتخيله العرب ولا وجود لفرد من أفراده في الخارج البتة، ولذلك كانوا يضربون المثل للمفقود الذي لا طمع في وجوده بحال بقولهم: (طارت به العنقاء) . أمثلة الحقيقية تعريف الصَّفَر بأنه (دابة في جوف الإنسان تعض على شراسيفه إذا جاع، والشراسيف أصول الأضلاع؛ لأن الصفر شيء تتخيله العرب مع أنه لا وجود له في الخارج، ومنه قول أعشى باهلة : ومن لا يتأرى لما في القدر يرقبه ولا يعض على شرسوفه الصفر قولهم (العنقاء طائر، والصفر (دابة لم يعتبر في صدق عنوانه على موضوعه الوجود الخارجي، بل اكتفي بتقدير الوجود؛ لأن الطائر والدابة المذكورين لو فُرض وجودهما في الخارج لصدق عليهم الاسم، وقد أشرت في أرجوزتي في فن المنطق إلى الفرق بين الخارجية والحقيقة بقولي : كل قضية على ماذكروا في صدق عنوان لها يعتبرُ وجود موضوع لها في الحال أو المضي أو الاستقبال فخارجية على المعهود وحيث لا اعتبار بالوجودِ بل كان بالتقدير للوجود مع كون الوجود خارجًا قد لا يقع فهي الحقيقية والفرق أضـا بينهمــا بـأنـه لـو فـرضــا الحصر للألوان في السواد في خارج والغير ذو انفقادِ فقولك البياض لون فنّدوا بحسب الأول إذ لا يوجد وكذبه لفقده في الخارج مع اعتبارنا الوجود الخارجي وصادق بالاعتبار الثاني كون البياض أحد الألوانِ إذ فيه معنى كلُّ ما لو وجدا كان بياضًا فبحيث لو بدا وجوده يكون لونا فعلـم الصدق مع كون البياض منعدم وصدقُ حصر اللون بالسواد بحسَب الأول أمر باد وكذبه بحسب الثاني اتضح فما أبيح افعل ودع مالم يُبح انتهى محل الغرض من الأرجوزة المذكورة.
An example of a real proposition is your saying: 'The Anqa is a bird of strange shape, long neck, preying on boys and young cows'; because the Anqa is a bird imagined by the Arabs and there is absolutely no individual of its kind in external existence. Therefore they used it as a parable for something lost which there is no hope of its existence at all, saying: 'The Anqa carried it away.' Examples of the real: the definition of Safar as 'an animal in man's belly that bites his cartilage when he is hungry'—the cartilage are the roots of the ribs; because Safar is something the Arabs imagine with no existence externally. From it is the saying of A'sha of Bahila: 'Who does not wait for what is in the pot, nor does Safar bite his cartilage.' They say: 'The Anqa is a bird, and Safar is an animal' – external existence was not considered in the truth of the predicate applying to its subject; rather they were content with presuming existence, because if the mentioned bird and animal were assumed to exist externally, the name would be true of them. And I have indicated in my poem on the art of logic the difference between the external and the real by my saying: 'Every proposition, according to what they mentioned, for the truth of its predicate, the existence of its subject is considered in the present, past, or future; so it is external on the usual way. And where existence is not considered, rather it is by presumption of existence, while existence externally may not occur, then it is the real. And the difference between them is that if we were to imagine the restriction of colors to black externally, and the other is nonexistent, then your saying 'Whiteness is a color' is refuted according to the first [view] since it does not exist; and its falsity due to its lack externally, while we consider external existence. And it is true according to the second consideration that whiteness is one of the colors, since in it is the meaning: everything that if it existed would be whiteness. So if its existence appears, it would be a color. Thus the truth is known despite whiteness being nonexistent. And the truth of restricting color to black according to the first [view] is a clear matter, and its falsity according to the second is evident. So do what is permitted and leave what is not permitted.' This ends the intended purpose of the mentioned poem.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates the difference between external (خارجية) and real (حقيقية) propositions. The author first gives examples: the statement 'The Anqa is a bird...' and the definition of Safar as a mythical creature. These are real propositions because their subject has no external existence, yet the predicate can be truly affirmed as long as the subject is conceivable. The author then quotes his own didactic poem which systematically explains: an external proposition requires actual existence of the subject at some time (present, past, or future); a real proposition dispenses with actual existence and only requires that if the subject existed, the predicate would apply. The poem further illustrates with the example of 'Whiteness is a color'—this is false as an external proposition (since whiteness may not exist externally) but true as a real one (since if whiteness existed, it would be a color). Conversely, 'All colors are black' is false in both senses. The lesson emphasizes that the truth-value of a proposition can depend on the type of proposition (external vs. real).

Key Terms
الحقيقية
الخارجية
تقدير الوجود
الأرجوزة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) true as external, false as real
B) false as external, true as real
C) true in both
D) false in both

Key Takeaway
The truth of a proposition depends on whether we interpret it as external (requiring actual existence) or real (presuming existence).

Experimental Self-Evident (Tajribi)
Author defines the experimental type of self-evident proposition based on repeated observation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the experimental self-evident proposition and identify its defining characteristics from repeated observation.

Core Idea
Experimental self-evident propositions yield certainty through uniform repeated observation, even without knowing the cause.

We have already covered the first two types of obvious self-evident propositions. The third type, the experimental (tajribi), is crucial because it grounds much of our empirical knowledge. This lesson unpacks its definition and demonstrates how repeated observations produce certainty about causal relationships, even when the exact cause remains unknown.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find what is being defined in the first sentence. The phrase 'النوع الثالث من أنواع البديهي الجلي: التجريبي' tells us the subject is the experimental type of obvious self-evident proposition.
The definiendum is 'experimental self-evident' (التجريبي).
2
Extract the full definition (الضابط)
The author provides the definition: 'كل قضية يحكم العقل فيها بثبوت المحمول للموضوع بواسطة تجريب ومشاهدات متكررة مفيدة للعلم بأن هذا الوقوع المتكرر على نمط واحد من غير تخلف لابد له من سبب'. Break this down: it is a proposition where the mind judges the predicate's belonging to the subject by means of repeated experimentation and observations, which give knowledge that the uniform repeated occurrence must have a cause.
Note the key components: (1) a proposition, (2) mind's judgment, (3) via experimentation, (4) repeated observations, (5) resulting in knowledge of a necessary cause.
3
Understand the epistemic claim
The passage states that this process 'يفيد العلم' (gives knowledge) and 'يحصل الجزم' (attains certainty). So experimental propositions are not merely probable but certain, because of the uniformity and lack of exceptions.
This certainty is based on the repeated occurrence, not on knowing the cause's essence.
4
Analyze the examples provided
Look at each example: water quenches thirst, bread satiates, fire burns, scammony purges bile, oxymel soothes it. For each, verify that it matches the definition: repeated observations confirm the uniform result, and we infer a cause (even if unknown).
Notice that the cause (sabab) is inferred, but its reality may be unknown.
5
Apply the definition to a new example
Test a new statement: 'Honey heals wounds.' Does it qualify as experimental self-evident? It requires repeated observation of healing without exception, leading to certainty of a cause.
This step reinforces the conditions: uniformity and no exceptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث من أنواع البديهي الجلي: التجريبي، وضابطه أنه كل قضية يحكم العقل فيها بثبوت المحمول للموضوع بواسطة تجريب ومشاهدات متكررة مفيدة للعلم بأن هذا الوقوع المتكرر على نمط واحد من غير تخلف لابد له من سبب، وإن لم يكن عالمًا بحقيقة هذا السبب. وهذا القسم واضح ومن أمثلته قولك: الماء يُروي، والخبز يشبع، والنار محرقة، والسقمونيا مسهل للصفراء، والسكنجبيل مسكن لها وهكذا لأنها قضايا علمت بالتجريب، وتكرر المشاهدة لترتب المسبب فيها على السبب فحصل الجزم بها بواسطة ذلك التكرر المذكور.
The third type of the obvious self-evident is the experimental: its definition is that it is every proposition in which the mind judges the attribution of the predicate to the subject by means of experimentation and repeated observations that give knowledge that this repeated occurrence in a uniform manner without exception must have a cause, even if one does not know the reality of this cause. This category is clear, and examples include your saying: water quenches thirst, bread satiates, fire burns, scammony purges bile, oxymel soothes it, and so on, because these are propositions known by experimentation and repeated observation of the effect following the cause, so certainty about them is attained by means of that repeated observation.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage defines the third type of obvious self-evident proposition (badihi jali): the experimental (tajribi). The author gives its definition: it is a proposition where the mind judges the predicate belongs to the subject based on repeated experimentation and observation, which yields certain knowledge that this repeated uniform occurrence must have a cause, even if the exact nature of that cause is unknown. He then provides examples from common experience: water quenches thirst, bread fills hunger, fire burns, scammony purges bile, and oxymel soothes it. These are known through repeated observation of the effect following the cause, leading to certainty.

Key Terms
التجريبي (al-tajribi): A self-evident proposition learned through repeated experience that reveals a causal connection. [Note: Stable term; used in later logic for empirical generalization.]
السبب (al-sabab): The cause of the repeated phenomenon, whose exact nature may be unknown. [Note: In kalam, sabab often refers to a secondary cause; here it is the cause inferred from regular occurrence.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Repeated observations of uniform occurrences leading to certainty about a cause.
C) Direct sense perception without repetition.
D) Revelation from prophets.

Key Takeaway
Experimental self-evident propositions derive their certainty from repeated uniform observation that implies a necessary cause, even if the cause's essence remains unknown.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Classifying Common Self-Evident Propositions: Sensory vs. Inner
Classification of common self-evident into sensory and inner

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any common self-evident proposition as either sensory (based on external senses) or inner (based on internal senses).

Core Idea
Sensory self-evident rests on external senses; inner self-evident rests on internal feelings.

Not all self-evident truths are of the same kind. In this lesson, we examine the fourth type of obvious self-evident propositions: those common to all people. The author splits them into two: sensory (based on external sense perceptions like sight and touch) and inner (based on internal awareness of states like pleasure and pain). Learning this distinction sharpens your ability to identify the foundation of your own certain knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition
Look at the statement being evaluated. Is it something like 'The sun is bright' or 'I am feeling joy'? The kind of proposition determines its source of certainty.
2
Determine the source of judgment
Ask: does the mind rely on external senses (sight, hearing, touch, etc.) to judge the truth of this proposition? Or does it rely on internal perception (feelings, emotions, self-awareness)?
External senses give you information about the outside world; internal senses give you information about your own inner states.
3
Classify as sensory or inner
If the judgment comes from external senses, it is sensory (hissi). If it comes from internal perception, it is inner (wijdani), also called mushahadat.
The passage gives examples: 'The sun is bright' (sensory via sight), 'pleasure is an accident' (inner via internal sense).
4
Test with a new example
Take a proposition like 'I am now in pain'. Which type is it? You don't need external senses; you feel the pain directly. So it is an inner self-evident proposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع من أنواع البديهي الجلي: المشترك بين عامة الناس، وهو نوعان: الأول: الحسي، وهو كل قضية يحكم العقل بثبوت محمولها لموضوعها استناداً إلى إدراك الحواس الظاهرة، كقولك: (الشمس مضيئة)؛ لأنك تدرك إضاءتها بحاسة عينك، وقولك: (هذا الحرير لين)؛ لأنك تدرك لينه بحاسة لمسك، وهذا الكلام واضح)؛ لأنك تدرك وضوحه بحاسة سمعك. وهذه الأمثلة الإدراك فيها بالحواس الظاهرة. والثاني من نوعيه الوجداني، وهو الذي يسميه بعضهم بالمشاهدات، وهو كل قضية يحكم العقل فيها بثبوت المحمول للموضوع استناداً إلى إدراك الحواس الباطنة، كقولك: اللذة عارض يعرض للإنسان، والألم كذلك، والفرح والخوف ونحو ذلك كذلك؛ فإن الإنسان يدرك حصول اللذة والألم والفرح والخوف ونحو ذلك بحواسه الباطنة.
The fourth type of the obvious self-evident is the common among all people, and it has two kinds. The first is the sensory: it is every proposition in which the mind judges the attribution of its predicate to its subject based on the perception of the external senses, like your saying: 'The sun is bright', because you perceive its brightness by your sense of sight; and your saying: 'This silk is soft', because you perceive its softness by your sense of touch; and this speech is clear, because you perceive its clarity by your sense of hearing. These examples involve perception through external senses. The second of its two kinds is the inner (wijdani), which some call 'observations' (mushahadat); it is every proposition in which the mind judges the attribution of the predicate to the subject based on the perception of the internal senses, like your saying: pleasure is an accident that occurs to a person, and pain likewise, and joy and fear and so forth similarly; for a person perceives the occurrence of pleasure, pain, joy, fear and so on by his internal senses.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth type of obvious self-evident propositions: the common (mushtarak) among all people. It has two subtypes. The first is sensory (hissi), where judgment is based on external sense perception. Examples: 'The sun is bright' via sight, 'this silk is soft' via touch, and the statement 'this speech is clear' via hearing. The second subtype is inner (wijdani), also called 'observations' (mushahadat), where judgment is based on internal senses. Examples: pleasure is an accident that occurs to a person, pain, joy, fear, etc., because these are directly felt through internal perception.

Key Terms
الحسي
الوجداني
المشاهدات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Common self-evident propositions are divided into sensory (external senses) and inner (internal sense perception); both are certain but from different sources.

The Intuitive Hidden Self-Evident: From Conjecture to Certainty
Author defines the first type of hidden self-evident: the intuitive, based on strong inner insight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defining characteristics of the intuitive hidden self-evident and distinguish it from mere guesswork.

Core Idea
The intuitive self-evident starts from conjecture but, through a strong inner faculty, yields certain knowledge.

In this short passage, the author introduces the first type of hidden self-evident proposition: the intuitive (hudsi). He acknowledges that the root of 'intuition' (hads) is conjecture and guesswork, yet he defines the intuitive self-evident as a proposition that becomes certain through a strong inner insight. Here we will unpack the definition and see how a starting point of doubt can transform into unshakeable certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its context
The passage begins by introducing the hidden self-evident (al-badihi al-khafi) and immediately lists its two types. The first type is the intuitive (al-hudsi). Note that the author uses the phrase 'أشهر أنواعه اثنان' indicating that these are the most famous types, implying there may be others.
2
Extract the definition of intuitive self-evident
The author gives a precise definition: 'كل قضية يحكم فيها العقل بثبوت المحمول للموضوع استناداً إلى حدس قوي من النفس يزول معه الشك ويحصل به اليقين.' Break it down: (a) it is a proposition (qadiyya), (b) the mind judges the predicate to be true of the subject, (c) based on a strong intuition (hads qawi) from the soul, (d) by which doubt disappears and certainty (yaqin) is obtained. The genus is 'self-evident proposition' and the differentia is 'based on strong intuition that removes doubt and yields certainty'.
The key shift is that the origin (asl) of hads is conjecture, but the strong intuition (hads qawi) transforms it into certainty.
3
Test the definition: does it exclude mere guesswork?
The definition explicitly ties the judgment to a 'strong intuition' that removes doubt. Mere guesswork (takhmeen) does not remove doubt; it remains weak. So the definition successfully distinguishes the intuitive self-evident from ordinary conjecture by requiring the result to be certainty (yaqin). This is a test of completeness: it includes only those intuitive judgments that yield certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما البديهي الخفي فأشهر أنواعه اثنان: الأول: البديهي الخفي الحدسي، وأصل الحدس الظن والتخمين، وضابط البديهي الحدسي هو كل قضية يحكم فيها العقل بثبوت المحمول للموضوع استناداً إلى حدس قوي من النفس يزول معه الشك ويحصل به اليقين.
As for the hidden self-evident, the most famous of its types are two: the first: the intuitive hidden self-evident; the origin of intuition is conjecture and guesswork; the definition of the intuitive self-evident is: every proposition in which the mind judges the attribution of the predicate to the subject based on a strong intuition from the soul by which doubt disappears and certainty is attained.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage introduces the hidden self-evident (badihi khafi) and begins with its first type: the intuitive (hudsi). The author notes that the root of hads is conjecture and guesswork (zann wa takhmeen). However, the intuitive self-evident is defined as a proposition where the mind judges based on a strong inner intuition (hads qawi) that removes doubt and produces certainty (yaqeen). Thus, even though the starting point is guesswork, the strong intuition yields certain knowledge.

Key Terms
البديهي الخفي (al-badihi al-khafi): A self-evident proposition that is not obvious to everyone but becomes certain through a specific inner faculty. [Note: Contrasts with badihi jali; this category includes intuitive and also often other types like the one based on sequential reasoning.]
الحدسي (al-hudsi): A self-evident proposition based on a strong inner intuition (hads) that yields certainty. [Note: Hads in earlier philosophy often means acute intelligence; here it is specifically a source of certain propositions.]
الحدس (al-hads): The inner intuitive faculty that produces certain judgment, though its origin is conjecture. [Note: In earlier texts, hads may refer to simple guess; here it is elevated to a source of certainty when strong.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The intuitive hidden self-evident is defined by a strong inner intuition that transforms conjecture into certainty, and it is one of two main types of hidden self-evident propositions.

Evident Intuitive: Sensory and Introspective Propositions
Author defines evident intuitive propositions and enumerates its two subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish sensory propositions from introspective propositions and correctly classify examples from Islamic sources.

Core Idea
Intuitive knowledge is not only sensory but also inner awareness.

The author of this text presents the fourth type of evident intuitive knowledge, which is shared by all people. He distinguishes two subtypes: sensory, based on external senses such as sight and touch, and introspective, based on internal perception such as awareness of pleasure and pain. Understanding this classification is crucial for identifying the foundations of certain knowledge in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category
Understand that 'evident intuitive' means a proposition known immediately without any mental effort or proof.
This is the starting point: recognize that the passage is about immediate certain knowledge.
2
Note the two subtypes
The author lists sensory (hissi) and introspective (wijdani) as the two subtypes. Write them down.
These are exclusive and cover all immediate common knowledge.
3
Identify the source for each subtype
Sensory propositions rely on external senses (sight, touch, hearing, etc.). Introspective propositions rely on internal senses (inner awareness of states like pleasure, pain, joy, fear).
The source of judgment is the key differentiator.
4
Examine the examples given
The author gives examples: 'The sun is luminous' (sight), 'This silk is soft' (touch), 'This speech is clear' (hearing) for sensory; 'Pleasure is an accident', pain, joy, fear for introspective. Verify that each matches the source.
Notice that introspective examples are not observable externally.
5
Apply to Islamic examples
Given a proposition from Islamic sciences, determine whether its truth is known through external senses (e.g., seeing the crescent moon) or through inner awareness (e.g., feeling intention in prayer).
This step tests your ability to use the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع من أنواع البديهي الجلي : المشترك بين عامة الناس، وهو نوعان : الأول : الحسي، وهو كل قضية يحكم العقل بثبوت محمولها لموضوعها استنادًا إلى إدراك الحواس الظاهرة، كقولك: (الشمس مضيئة)؛ لأنك تدرك إضاءتها بحاسة عينك وقولك: (هذا الحرير لين)؛ لأنك تدرك لينه بحاسة لمسك، وهذا الكلام واضح)؛ لأنك تدرك وضوحه بحاسة سمعك. وهذه الأمثلة الإدراك فيها بالحواس الظاهرة . والثاني من نوعيه الوجداني، وهو الذي يسميه بعضهم بالمشاهدات، وهو كل قضية يحكم العقل فيها بثبوت المحمول للموضوع استنادًا إلى إدراك الحواس الباطنة، كقولك : اللذة عارض يعرض للإنسان، والألم كذلك، والفرح والخوف ونحو ذلك كذلك ؛ فإن الإنسان يدرك حصول اللذة والألم والفرح والخوف ونحو ذلك بحواسه الباطنة .
The fourth type of evident intuitive: the common among the general people, and it is two types: the first: the sensory, which is every proposition in which the intellect judges the affirmation of its predicate to its subject based on the perception of the external senses, such as your saying: 'The sun is luminous' because you perceive its luminosity through your sense of sight, and your saying: 'This silk is soft' because you perceive its softness through your sense of touch, and this speech is clear because you perceive its clarity through your sense of hearing. These examples are perceived by the external senses. The second of its two types: the introspective, which some call 'observed' (mushahadat), and it is every proposition in which the intellect judges the affirmation of the predicate to the subject based on the perception of the internal senses, such as your saying: 'Pleasure is an accident that occurs to man,' and pain likewise, and joy, fear, and the like likewise; for man perceives the occurrence of pleasure, pain, joy, fear, and the like through his internal senses.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage defines the evident intuitive (al-badihi al-jali) of the common type, which is shared by all people. The author explains that it has two subtypes: sensory and introspective. The sensory (al-hissi) relies on external senses like sight and touch, with examples: the sun's luminosity, silk softness, and speech clarity. The introspective (al-wijdani) relies on internal senses (inner perception) such as pleasure, pain, joy, fear. The author notes that some call these 'mushahadat' (observed). This classification is fundamental in Islamic logic for distinguishing types of certain knowledge that are immediate and do not require proof.

Key Terms
البديهي الجلي
الحسي
الوجداني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The sun is luminous
B) This silk is soft
C) Pleasure is an accident that occurs to man
D) The fire is hot

Key Takeaway
Evident intuitive propositions are of two types: sensory (external) and introspective (internal), both providing immediate certain knowledge.

Hidden Intuitive Propositions (Hadsiyyat) and the Definition of Hads
Author defines hidden intuitive (hadsi) and critiques the moon example; al-Bannani refines the definition of hads.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a hadsi proposition and distinguish it from fikr using al-Bannani's definition of hads as immediate attainment of middle terms.

Core Idea
Hads is immediate grasp of middle terms without movement; it can transform conjecture into certainty.

The hidden intuitive (al-badihi al-khafi) includes propositions that require slight attention to be recognized as certain. One type is the hadsi, based on strong conjecture (hads). In this lesson, we unpack the definition of hads and how it differs from discursive thinking, using the example of river and well water, and the refined definition of al-Bannani.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of hadsi proposition
The passage defines a hadsi proposition as one where the intellect judges the predicate to the subject based on a strong conjecture from the soul that removes doubt and brings certainty. Note that 'hads' originally means conjecture.
2
Examine the river-well example
The example 'The rise of water in rivers is the cause of the rise of water in wells not far from rivers' illustrates a hadsi proposition: repeated observation yields a strong conjecture that becomes certain.
This is a physical cause-effect relation.
3
Understand the critique of the moon example
The author avoids the common logical example of the moon's light from the sun out of politeness to Quranic verses that describe the moon as 'nur' (light). This shows that Islamic logicians prioritize revelation over customary examples.
Quran 10:5 and 71:16 are cited.
4
Contrast al-Bannani's definition of hads
Al-Bannani defines hads as the immediate attainment of middle terms (in a syllogism) without mental movement. If there is movement, it is fikr (thinking). This distinguishes true intuition from discursive reasoning.
This definition comes from his commentary on Sullam al-Akhdari.
5
Classify hadsi propositions epistemologically
Some scholars consider hadsi propositions as conjectural, but the author notes that through strengthening indications, they can reach certainty. Recognize the scholarly disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما البديهي الخفي فأشهر أنواعه اثنان : الأول: البديهي الخفي الحدسي، وأصل الحدس الظن والتخمين، وضابط البديهي الحدسي هو كل قضية يحكم فيها العقل بثبوت المحمول للموضوع استنادًا إلى حدس قوي من النفس يزول معه الشك ويحصل به اليقين . كقولك : ارتفاع الماء في الأنهار سبب ارتفاع ماء الآبار التي ليست بعيدة من الأنهار . وجرت عادة أهل هذا الفن وأهل فن المنطق بأنهم يمثلون للحدسيات بأن نور القمر مكتسب من نور الشمس ؛ قالوا لأن القرائن تقوي هذا الحدس حتى يصير يقينيا ؛ لأن نور القمر يشاهد كماله ونقصانه بحسب بعده من الشمس وقربه منها ويجزمون بناء على هذا بأن نوره مكتسب من نورها. ونحن دائما نتوقى هذا المثال ونحوه تأدبا مع ظاهر القرآن في قوله - تعالى -: ﴿ هُوَ الَّذِي جَعَلَ الشَّمْسَ ضِيَاءُ والقَمَرَ نُورًا) [يونس/ ٥] وقوله : وَجَعَلَ الْقَمَرَ فِيهِنَّ نُورًا ﴾ [نوح / ١٦] . وذهب جماعة من أهل العلم إلى أن الحدسيات من الظنيات لا من اليقينيات . وقال العلامة محمد بن الحسن البناني في شرحه لسلم الأخضري والتحقيق أن الحدس عبارة عن الظفر عند الالتفات إلى المطالب بالحدود الوسطى دفعة فلا حركة فيه، وإلا كان فكرًا؛
As for the hidden intuitive, its most famous types are two: the first: the hidden intuitive based on strong conjecture (hadsi). The origin of hads is conjecture and guess. The criterion of the hadsi intuitive is every proposition in which the intellect judges the affirmation of the predicate to the subject based on a strong conjecture from the soul that removes doubt and brings certainty. Like your saying: 'The rise of water in rivers is the cause of the rise of water in wells that are not far from rivers.' And it is the custom of the people of this art and the people of the art of logic that they give as an example for hadsi propositions that the light of the moon is acquired from the light of the sun; they said because the indications strengthen this conjecture until it becomes certain; because the light of the moon is observed in its fullness and deficiency according to its distance from the sun and its proximity to it, and they affirm based on this that its light is acquired from its light. And we always avoid this example and its like out of politeness with the apparent meaning of the Quran in His statement: 'It is He who made the sun a radiance and the moon a light' [Yunus:5] and His statement: 'And made the moon therein a light' [Nuh:16]. And a group of scholars held that hadsi propositions are among conjectural, not certain. And the scholar Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Bannani said in his commentary on Sullam al-Akhdari: 'The verification is that hads is the attainment, upon attention to the demands, of the middle terms in one go, so there is no movement in it; otherwise it would be thought.'
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage defines the hidden intuitive type known as 'hadsi' (based on strong conjecture). The author explains that hads originally means conjecture, but here it refers to a strong mental inference that reaches certainty. Example: inferring that water rise in wells is caused by river water rise — a conjecture that becomes certain. The author then criticizes the common logical example of the moon's light being acquired from the sun, avoiding it out of respect for Quranic verses that describe moon as 'nur' (light) and sun as 'diya' (radiance). The author also notes that some scholars consider hadsi propositions as merely conjectural, not certain. Finally, al-Bannani refines the definition: hads is the immediate attainment of middle terms (in a syllogism) without mental movement; if there is movement, it is thinking (fikr), not hads. This clarifies the epistemological status of hads as a form of immediate inference.

Key Terms
الحدس
البديهي الخفي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Hads involves movement of the mind
B) Hads attains middle terms gradually
C) Hads attains middle terms in one go
D) Hads is always about sensory data

Key Takeaway
Hads is the immediate, non-movement attainment of middle terms, and hadsi propositions can reach certainty despite originating from conjecture.

Consider the statement: 'The regular alternation of day and night indicates a purposeful creator.' According to the passage, could this be a hadsi proposition? Explain why or why not, referencing the criteria of strong conjecture and removal of doubt.

Four Types of Propositions in Dialectic
Author classifies propositions into four types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any proposition from a debate into one of the four types based on its epistemic status and presentation.

Core Idea
In dialectic, propositions are classified by epistemic type and how they are presented.

This passage from a dialectical manual introduces a fourfold classification of propositions. Understanding this classification is crucial for knowing how to properly object in a debate. Let's break down the two dimensions: theoretical vs hidden intuitive, and presented with or without evidence/prompting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two dimensions
The classification uses two axes: epistemic type (theoretical vs hidden intuitive) and presentation (with or without evidence/prompting). Determine for each proposition its place on these axes.
2
Define theoretical (nazari) and hidden intuitive (badihi khafi)
A theoretical proposition requires reasoning and evidence to be known, while a hidden intuitive is self-evident but appears theoretical due to subtlety. Both are contrasted with clear self-evident truths.
The hidden intuitive is a specific dialectical category.
3
Define presentation with or without evidence/prompting
A proposition can be presented 'stripped of' evidence/prompting or 'accompanied by' it. This affects the opponent's permissible responses.
4
List the four combinations
Combine the two axes to get four types: theoretical without evidence, theoretical with evidence, hidden intuitive without prompting, hidden intuitive with prompting. Memorize these as the standard dialectical classification.
5
Apply to examples
Test the classification on examples from the text: 'The world is created' (theoretical without evidence) and 'River water rise causes well water rise' (hidden intuitive without prompting).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو لغرض آخر، والثانية أن يكون ألقاه إليك مقرونا بالدليل. وهاتان هما حالتا البديهي الخفي؛ لأنه تارة يلقيه إليك مجردًا عن التنبيه لأجل إيهام أنه من الجليات أو لغرض آخر، وتارة يلقيه إليك مقرونا بالتنبيه، فالأقسام أربعة: الأول: تصديق نظري مجرد عن الدليل. الثاني: تصديق نظري مقرون بالدليل. الثالث: بديهي خفي مجرد عن التنبيه. الرابع: بديهي خفي مقرون بالتنبيه. فإن كان ألقى إليك تصديقا نظريًا مجردًا عن الدليل، أو بديهيا خفيًا مجردًا عن التنبيه فليس لك في الاعتراض عليه إلا شيء واحد وهو المنع، كأن تقول: أمنع هذه الدعوى، أو: لا أسلم لك هذه الدعوى، أو: هذه الدعوى ممنوعة، أو غير مسلمة، ونحو ذلك ويكفيك الاقتصار على واحدة من تلك العبارات، فيكون منعك هذا منعا مجردًا عن السند، وهو الأظهر عندي. وقد قال غير واحد من أهل هذا الفن: إنه لا مانع من أن تذكر مع المنع المذكور السند الذي استندت إليه في المنع، فيكون منعك المذكور مقرونا بالسند. والذي يظهر أن إتيانه بالسند في منع دعوى لم يقم صاحبها عليها دليلاً أنه من أنواع الغصب، والغصب وظيفة غير مقبولة عند أهل هذا الفن، وسيأتي في الكلام على الغصب أن السائل إذا عمد إلى دعوى غير مستدل عليها أو إلى مقدمة دليل لم يُقم المعلل عليها دليلاً فأقام دليلاً على بطلان إحداهما أنه يكون غاصبا والغصب وظيفة غير مقبولة في هذا الفن، ووجه كونه غاصبا ظاهر؛ لأن الاستدلال لصاحب التصديق المحتاج إلى دليل أو تنبيه، فإذا منعه السائل واستدل على منعه فقد غصب وظيفة المعلل التي هي الاستدلال، واقتران المنع بسند استدلال بذلك السند على بطلان دعوى المعلل قبل أن يستدل عليها فكونه من قبيل الغصب ظاهر. والظاهر أن من أجاز اقتران المنع المذكور بالسند من أهل هذا الفن يرى هذا النوع من الغصب جائزا كما لا يخفى. ومثاله في التصديق النظري أن تقول: العالم كله حادث، فيقول خصمك القائل بقدمه من الفلاسفة الضالين: أمنع دعواك هذه. ومثاله في البديهي الخفي أن تقول: ارتفاع ماء الأنهار سبب في ارتفاع ماء الآبار، فيقول خصمك: أمنع هذه الدعوى، أو: لا أسلمها، ونحو ذلك من العبارات المتقدمة. وسيأتي إن شاء الله أوجه الجواب عن المنع المذكور، مع أمثلة.
Or for another purpose. And the second is that he has presented it to you accompanied by evidence. These two are the states of the hidden intuitive: because sometimes he presents it to you stripped of prompting to give the impression that it is from the self-evident truths or for another purpose, and sometimes he presents it to you accompanied by prompting. So the divisions are four: First: a theoretical assenting proposition stripped of evidence. Second: a theoretical assenting proposition accompanied by evidence. Third: a hidden intuitive stripped of prompting. Fourth: a hidden intuitive accompanied by prompting. If he has presented to you a theoretical assenting proposition stripped of evidence, or a hidden intuitive stripped of prompting, then you have in objecting to it only one thing: denial, such as saying: 'I deny this claim,' or 'I do not concede this claim,' or 'This claim is denied,' or 'not conceded,' and similar expressions. And it suffices for you to restrict yourself to one of those expressions. So your denial becomes a denial stripped of proof, and this is the more evident to me. And more than one of the people of this art have said: There is no preventing that you mention along with the mentioned denial the proof upon which you rely in the denial, so your denial becomes accompanied by proof. But what appears is that bringing proof in denying a claim whose proponent has not established evidence for it is a type of usurpation, and usurpation is an unacceptable function according to the people of this art. And it will come in the discussion on usurpation that if the questioner deliberately takes an unsupported claim or a premise of a proof for which the reasoner has not established evidence, and he brings evidence to invalidate one of them, then he becomes a usurper, and usurpation is an unacceptable function in this art. And the reason for his being a usurper is apparent: because the reasoning belongs to the proponent of the assenting proposition who needs evidence or prompting. So if the questioner denies it and reasons for his denial, he has usurped the function of the reasoner, which is reasoning. And the coupling of denial with proof is reasoning by that proof to invalidate the claim of the reasoner before he has reasoned for it. So it is clearly of the type of usurpation. And it appears that whoever permits the coupling of the mentioned denial with proof among the people of this art considers this type of usurpation permissible, as is not hidden. And its example in theoretical assenting propositions: you say 'The entire world is temporally originated,' and your opponent, who says it is eternal among the misguided philosophers, says 'I deny this claim of yours.' And its example in the hidden intuitive: you say 'The rise of river water is a cause for the rise of well water,' and your opponent says 'I deny this claim,' or 'I do not concede it,' and similar expressions from the preceding ones. And there will come, God willing, the aspects of the answer to the mentioned denial, with examples.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is dialectical in nature, classifying propositions and establishing the proper etiquette for objection. The author first explains that a proposition can be presented in two ways: either stripped of evidence/prompting or accompanied by it. This yields four types: (1) theoretical assenting proposition without evidence, (2) theoretical assenting proposition with evidence, (3) hidden intuitive without prompting, (4) hidden intuitive with prompting. For the first and third types (unsupported claims), the only legitimate objection is simple denial (man') without providing proof. One may say 'I deny this claim' or similar. The author states his preference for denial without proof. Then he mentions that some scholars allow the objector to provide proof along with the denial. However, the author argues that this constitutes ghasb (usurpation) because it takes over the reasoner's role. He explains that the reasoner is the one who should provide evidence for his claim; if the objector provides evidence along with denial, he usurps that function. This is considered unacceptable in the discipline. The passage gives two examples: for theoretical claims, the opponent denies the claim that the world is created; for hidden intuitive claims, the opponent denies the causal link between river water level and well water level. The passage ends by noting that the responses to such denial will be discussed later.

تصديق
نظري
بديهي خفي
تنبيه
دليل

C) Hidden intuitive with evidence
D) Theoretical without prompting

Key Takeaway
The four types of propositions in dialectic are determined by whether they are theoretical or hidden intuitive, and whether they are presented with or without evidence/prompting.

The Proper Objection: Denial Without Evidence
Author discusses the rule of denial and the prohibition of usurpation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of simple denial (man') to unsupported claims and identify when providing evidence with denial constitutes usurpation (ghasb).

Core Idea
Deny an unsupported claim without proof; providing evidence usurps the opponent's role.

After classifying propositions, the author specifies the correct objection: for unsupported claims (types 1 and 3), only simple denial is allowed. He argues against providing proof, calling it usurpation. Let's understand the rule and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify which types require simple denial
The author says if a theoretical claim is presented without evidence, or a hidden intuitive without prompting, the only objection is denial. These are types 1 and 3.
2
Recognize expressions of denial
Standard phrases: 'I deny this claim', 'I do not concede', 'This claim is denied'. One expression is sufficient.
3
Understand the author's preference for denial without proof
The author states that denial should be 'stripped of proof' (man' mujarrad 'an al-sanad), and this is his preferred view. Some scholars allow proof, but he disagrees.
4
Analyze the argument against providing proof
Providing proof with denial is usurpation (ghasb) because reasoning is the role of the original claimant. The objector would be taking over that role.
5
Apply to examples
In the example, the opponent simply says 'I deny your claim' without giving evidence. That is the correct move. If he provided evidence, he would be a usurper.

And the coupling of denial with proof is reasoning by that proof to invalidate the claim of the reasoner before he has reasoned for it. So it is clearly of the type of usurpation. And it appears that whoever permits the coupling of the mentioned denial with proof among the people of this art considers this type of usurpation permissible, as is not hidden. And its example in theoretical assenting propositions: you say 'The entire world is temporally originated,' and your opponent, who says it is eternal among the misguided philosophers, says 'I deny this claim of yours.' And its example in the hidden intuitive: you say 'The rise of river water is a cause for the rise of well water,' and your opponent says 'I deny this claim,' or 'I do not concede it,' and similar expressions from the preceding ones. And there will come, God willing, the aspects of the answer to the mentioned denial, with examples.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is dialectical in nature, classifying propositions and establishing the proper etiquette for objection. The author first explains that a proposition can be presented in two ways: either stripped of evidence/prompting or accompanied by it. This yields four types: (1) theoretical assenting proposition without evidence, (2) theoretical assenting proposition with evidence, (3) hidden intuitive without prompting, (4) hidden intuitive with prompting. For the first and third types (unsupported claims), the only legitimate objection is simple denial (man') without providing proof. One may say 'I deny this claim' or similar. The author states his preference for denial without proof. Then he mentions that some scholars allow the objector to provide proof along with the denial. However, the author argues that this constitutes ghasb (usurpation) because it takes over the reasoner's role. He explains that the reasoner is the one who should provide evidence for his claim; if the objector provides evidence along with denial, he usurps that function. This is considered unacceptable in the discipline. The passage gives two examples: for theoretical claims, the opponent denies the claim that the world is created; for hidden intuitive claims, the opponent denies the causal link between river water level and well water level. The passage ends by noting that the responses to such denial will be discussed later.

Key Terms
منع
غصب
سند
معلل
سائل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what makes providing evidence with denial an act of usurpation (ghasb)?
A) It is always forbidden in any context.
B) It takes over the reasoner's role of providing evidence.
C) It is a stronger form of denial.
D) It is allowed only for hidden intuitive claims.

Key Takeaway
For unsupported claims, the only valid objection is simple denial without proof; providing proof with denial is usurpation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a debate, your opponent says 'The world is created' without any supporting argument. According to the author, what should your objection be? Write it in proper form.
?
If you were to respond to that claim by saying 'The world cannot be created because matter is eternal' (providing evidence), would the author consider that valid? Why or why not?

Propositions
beginner
5 min
Absolute Modality Implies Possible Modality
Author defines absolute and possible modalities and establishes their logical relationship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish absolute and possible modalities and determine whether a given proposition's truth implies its possibility.

Core Idea
Absolute truth guarantees possibility, but possibility does not guarantee actuality.

In this passage, the author explains two types of modal propositions: absolute (iṭlāq) and possible (imkān). He establishes a crucial logical relationship: if a proposition is actually true (absolute), it must be possible. However, the converse does not hold. Understanding this distinction is essential for evaluating claims in Islamic arguments, especially in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two modalities
Locate the definitions of 'al-muwajjaha bi-l-iṭlāq' (absolute modality) and 'al-muwajjaha bi-l-imkān' (possible modality) in the Arabic passage. Note what each modality indicates about the subject-predicate relation.
Absolute means actual occurrence; possible means non-impossibility.
2
Understand the entailment
The author states that absolute modality is more specific (akhaṣṣ) than possible modality, so the truth of an absolute proposition necessarily implies the truth of the corresponding possible proposition. Reason: what actually occurs must already be possible.
This is a one-way implication: absolute → possible.
3
Analyze the example of Abu Lahab
The proposition 'Abū Lahab is a believer' is possible (because faith is not rationally impossible for him) but false in absolute modality (since he actually disbelieved, as per the Qur'an). This illustrates why absolute entails possible but the converse does not hold.
The example also shows that a false absolute proposition can still be possible.
4
Apply the principle to a new case
Given an absolute proposition in an Islamic science context, infer its possibility. Verify that if the absolute is true, the possible must be true, but not vice versa. Try constructing an example from kalam, e.g., 'The world is temporally originated' (absolute) entails 'The world is possibly originated' (possible).
This principle prevents conflating actuality with possibility in theological reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإطلاق أخص من الإمكان، فثبوت الإطلاق يلزمه ضرورة ثبوت الإمكان، كما قال العلامة الشيخ المختار بن بونا الشنقيطي. ونسبة ممكنة محققة ثبوت صدق عند صدق المطلقة هي. وإيضاح هذا الكلام لمن لا يعرف مبحث الجهات في فن المنطق: أن الموجهة بالإمكان ما لا يستحيل عقلاً ثبوت موضوعها لمحمولها وإن كان غير ثابت له بالفعل، ولا نطيل هنا الكلام بالفرق بين الإمكان العام والإمكان الخاص، والموجهة بالإطلاق في الاصطلاح هي القضية التي اتصف موضوعها بمحمولها بالفعل إيجابا أو سلبا، فقولك: أبو لهب مؤمن بالإمكان الخاص صادق؛ لأن اتصافه بالإيمان ليس بمستحيل عقلاً، ولو كان مستحيلا عقلاً لما كلف به، بخلاف ما لو قلت: أبو لهب مؤمن بالإطلاق، أي بالفعل، فهو كذب بل كفر؛ لتكذيبه نص القرآن العظيم. وإذا علمت ذلك علمت أن ثبوت الإطلاق يستلزم ثبوت الإمكان بلا عكس.
Absolute [modality] is more specific than possible [modality]; thus, the establishment of absolute necessarily entails the establishment of possible, as stated by the eminent scholar al-Shaykh al-Mukhtār ibn Būnā al-Shanqīṭī. And a possible [proposition] is a truly verified relationship of truth [predication] when the absolute [proposition] is true. An explanation of this statement for one who does not know the topic of modalities in the art of logic: the modalized [proposition] by possibility is that whose subject's possession of its predicate is not rationally impossible, even if it is not actually established for it. We will not lengthen the discussion here on the difference between general possibility and special possibility. The modalized by absolute in terminology is the proposition in which the subject is actually described by its predicate, either positively or negatively. So your statement: 'Abū Lahab is a believer' with special possibility is true, because his being described with faith is not rationally impossible, even if it were rationally impossible, he would not be obligated [to have faith]. Contrary to if you say: 'Abū Lahab is a believer' with absolute [modality], i.e., actually, then it is a lie, even disbelief, because it denies the text of the great Qur'an. So when you know that, you know that the establishment of absolute necessarily entails the establishment of possible without the converse.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage defines two types of modalized propositions: possible (al-mumkina) and absolute (al-muṭlaqa), and establishes a logical relationship between them. The author begins by citing al-Shanqīṭī's assertion that al-iṭlāq (absolute modality) is more specific (akhaṣṣ) than al-imkān (possibility), so if a proposition is true in the absolute sense, it necessarily implies that it is also possible. This is because what actually occurs must already be possible. Then the author explains each term for a reader unfamiliar with modal logic: 'modalized by possibility' (al-muwajjaha bi-l-imkān) means a proposition whose predicate is not rationally impossible to apply to its subject, even if it is not actually true. And 'modalized by absolute' means the subject actually has the predicate (either affirmative or negative). The example given is the statement 'Abū Lahab is a believer.' In terms of special possibility (al-imkān al-khāṣṣ), this statement is true because it is not rationally impossible for him to have believed — indeed, he was obligated to believe. But if we claim it as absolute (actual), it is false and even blasphemous because the Qur'an explicitly states that Abū Lahab will go to Hell. Thus, we see that absolute entails possibility, but not vice versa. The author notes that he will not delve into the distinction between general and special possibility here.

Key Terms
الموجهة بالإمكان
الموجهة بالإطلاق
الإطلاق
الإمكان
الإمكان الخاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why is the statement 'Abū Lahab is a believer' with absolute modality considered false?
A) Because it is rationally impossible.
B) Because it denies the Qur'anic text.
C) Because Abū Lahab was a disbeliever in actuality.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
Absolute modality entails possible modality, but the converse does not hold: actuality implies possibility, not vice versa.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the absolute proposition 'The world is temporally originated' (from kalam), what can you infer about its possibility? Explain why the converse would not be valid.
?
Critique this reasoning: 'Since it is possible that Zayd will pray, it must be that he actually prays.' Use the principle from this lesson to explain the error.
